Instantaneous vs. Continuous Contracts: Legal Classification and Consequences
Classification of contracts and legal consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between instantaneous and continuous contracts and apply the Hanafi rules of rescission and gradual obligation accrual.

Core Idea
Continuous contracts like ijarah accrue obligations gradually; rescission is prospective, not retroactive.

Contracts in Islamic law are not all alike. Some are performed instantly, like a sale, while others extend over time, like a lease (ijarah) or supply agreement. This distinction carries significant legal consequences that every mufti must grasp. In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi classification and its practical effects on rescission, obligation matching, and defect liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contract types
Recognize that instantaneous contracts (al-ʿuqūd al-fawriyya) are performed in a single exchange, while continuous contracts (al-ʿuqūd al-mustamirra) involve gradual performance over time. Examples: sale vs. lease, supply contracts.
The classification depends on the nature of performance, not the label.
2
Understand retroactivity of rescission
For instantaneous contracts, rescission (faskh) is retroactive, meaning the parties must return what they received. For continuous contracts, rescission is prospective only – obligations fulfilled before rescission stand, and only future obligations are cancelled.
This principle protects completed exchanges in ongoing relationships.
3
Analyze gradual obligation matching
In a continuous commutative contract like ijarah, obligations are matched gradually: rent accrues as benefit is received. Even if rent is prepaid, it does not settle fully until the benefit is enjoyed. If the contract is rescinded early, the lessee recovers prepaid rent for the unused period.
Hanafi jurists state rent is owed moment by moment, but collection is simplified day by day or stage by stage.
4
Compare defect rights
In ijarah, a defect arising after the contract gives the lessee the right to rescind because the contract is treated as a series of renewing contracts – the defect becomes 'pre-existing' for the future. In sale, only pre-existing defects allow rescission after delivery.
This difference stems from the continuous nature of ijarah.

Chapter (47) Classification of Contracts. And foods daily to the owner of a restaurant, hotel, or hospital for example, then it is considered a continuous contract even if in reality it is a type of sale. It is called in legal terminology: supply contract. 15/47 - Results of distinguishing between instantaneous and continuous contracts: This distinction between instantaneous and continuous contracts has important results agreed upon by Islamic legal theory and modern legal theory. The most important are two: 1) An instantaneous contract like sale, if rescinded, its rescission is retroactive, so it entails returning what was performed of the parties' obligations. As for a continuous contract, its rescission is limited, non-retroactive, i.e., without retroactive effect in the discussion of dissolution of contracts. 2) The reciprocal obligations in a continuous commutative contract are established in a symmetrical manner between the two parties little by little, so what is completed on one side is matched on the other side. In a lease contract where benefit is exchanged for rent, the lessee's obligation for rent is realized and settled gradually as he benefits and to the extent of his benefit. So even if acceleration of rent is stipulated, the obligation for rent does not become settled in the lessee's liability (even if acceleration is due) except in installments according to the passing of benefits of the leased property gradually to the lessee's account. If the contract is rescinded before the end of the lease term, he recovers the rent advanced for the remaining period. Our jurists explicitly stated that the original rule in ijarah is that the lessor is entitled to demand rent moment by moment, but due to its difficulty and impossibility, he is given the right to demand the rent of a house day by day, and the rent of an animal stage by stage, unless there is a stipulation or custom to the contrary. A continuous commutative contract is considered in juristic view as multiple renewing contracts over the period of its execution. On this basis, in a lease contract, the lessee has the right to rescind due to a defect arising in the leased property while in his possession, just as he has the right to rescind due to a pre-existing defect. This is different from an instantaneous sale contract, where the buyer does not have the right to rescind due to a defect arising in the sold item after taking possession, but only for a pre-existing defect present before delivery. So, due to the renewing nature of the lease contract over time, a defect arising in the leased property becomes a pre-existing defect for the future.
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage classifies contracts into instantaneous and continuous types and explains their legal consequences. The author first notes that continuous contracts include supply contracts where goods are delivered periodically. The distinction yields two main results. First, rescission of an instantaneous contract (like sale) is retroactive, meaning the parties must return what they received. For continuous contracts, rescission is prospective only. Second, in continuous commutative contracts like ijarah (lease), obligations are matched gradually: rent accrues as benefit is received. Even if rent is prepaid, it does not become fully settled; if the contract is rescinded early, the lessee recovers prepaid rent for the unused period. The Hanafi jurists rule that rent is owed moment by moment, but for practical convenience, it is due day by day for a house and stage by stage for an animal, unless there is a stipulation or custom otherwise. Continuous contracts are treated as a series of renewing contracts. Thus, in ijarah, a defect that arises after the contract gives the lessee the right to rescind, because for the future portion it is considered a pre-existing defect. This contrasts with a sale, where only pre-existing defects allow rescission after delivery. The author references Al-Hidayah and its commentary, as well as the Majallah.

Key Terms
عقد فوري (ʿaqd fawrī): عقد يتم تنفيذه دفعة واحدة مثل البيع
عقد مستمر (ʿaqd mustamirr): عقد يمتد تنفيذه على فترات زمنية مثل الإجارة والتوريد
عقد المعاوضة المستمر (ʿaqd al-muʿāwaḍah al-mustamirr): عقد معاوضة يتقابل فيه العوضان تدريجياً كالإجارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Continuous contracts accrue obligations gradually; rescission is prospective, and prepaid amounts are refunded for the unperformed portion.

Original vs. Accessory Contracts
Classification of contracts into original and accessory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between original and accessory contracts and apply the rule that an accessory contract requires a principal right to be valid.

Core Idea
An accessory contract depends on a principal right for its existence and extinction.

Contracts in Islamic law are not all alike; some stand on their own, while others exist only to secure another right. This distinction is crucial for a mufti: an accessory contract like pledge (rahn) or guarantee (kafalah) cannot be created without a corresponding principal obligation. Understanding this helps in evaluating the validity of security contracts in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the passage and note that contracts are divided into original (mustaqill) and accessory (taba'i). Original contracts exist independently; accessory contracts are linked to a principal right.
The key is independence vs. dependence.
2
Analyze the dependence relationship
Accessory contracts are described as 'branch to root' – they come into being and cease with the principal right. Ask: what happens if the principal right does not exist?
The passage says they 'do not conclude initially' without a right.
3
Apply to scenarios
Consider a pledge without any debt or a guarantee without an obligation. Based on the rule, these would be invalid. Test other examples: can a sale (original) exist alone? Yes.
This classification has practical consequences for fatwa.

16/47 - Twelfth: According to originality and accessoryness: Contracts are classified by this consideration into two types as well: A) Original contracts: they are every contract that is independent in its existence, not linked to another matter as an accessory to it in existence and extinction. Such as sale, lease, deposit, loan, etc. B) Accessory contracts: they are every contract that is accessory to another right and linked in its existence and extinction like the connection of a branch to its origin. Such as pledge (rahn) and guarantee (kafalah). So pledge and guarantee are both securing for something else, so they are not concluded initially if there is not in their opposite a right that is established or expected.
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This passage classifies contracts as either original or accessory. Original contracts are independent: they exist on their own and are not tied to another right. Examples include sale (bayʿ), lease (ijārah), deposit (wadīʿah), and loan (ʿāriyah). Accessory contracts, in contrast, exist as subordinate to another right. They come into being and cease to exist along with that principal right, much like a branch attached to its root. Examples are pledge (rahn) and guarantee (kafālah), both of which serve as security for an obligation. Therefore, such contracts cannot be initiated without a corresponding right, whether already established or anticipated. This classification helps in understanding the legal effects: accessory contracts follow the fate of the principal right.

عقد تبعي (ʿaqd tabaʿī): عقد تابع لحق آخر كالرهن والكفالة
توثيق (tawthīq): تأمين الالتزام

Accessory contracts (rahn, kafalah) depend on a principal right; without it, they are invalid.

Accessory Contracts: Definition and Critical Refinement
Defining accessory contracts and their relationship with principal rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the correct Hanafi definition of an accessory contract from alternative definitions and apply it to identify accessory contracts in legal scenarios.

Core Idea
An accessory contract depends on a right, not on a prior contract.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam sharpens the understanding of accessory contracts (العقد التبعي). The author first illustrates with rahn and kafala, then critiques a prominent modern definition, refining it to center on the right (haqq) rather than the originating contract. This precision is crucial for muftis when classifying security contracts.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the examples
Notice that rahn and kafala are valid for future rights—e.g., rahn for a promised loan, kafala for a debt arising from a future sale. This shows they do not require a pre-existing contract.
2
2. Extract the dependency characteristic
The passage states they 'are not originally concluded except in exchange for another right' and they cease when that right ceases. The accessory contract is derivative (فرع) of a right.
3
3. Contrast with al-Sanhuri's definition
Al-Sanhuri defined accessory contract as 'accessory to a prior original contract.' The author finds this too narrow.
This is the target of refinement.
4
4. Analyze the three critiques
First, accessory contracts can secure debts from torts (e.g., ضمان الإتلاف). Second, even when the right comes from a contract, the contract is accessory to the right, not the contract. Third, the accessory contract may precede the right (future debt).
5
5. Derive the correct definition
The correct definition: 'the contract that is accessory to another right' – the right is the core, not the contract.
This is the mufta bihi principle.

Chapter (47) Classification of Contracts
Mortgage (rahn) is concluded in exchange for a promised debt, as if one person promises another to lend him an amount, and takes from him a mortgage in advance in exchange for it.
Suretyship (kafala) is concluded in exchange for a debt that will be established; as if one says to another: 'Sell to so-and-so, and whatever right is established for you against him, I am surety for it.' [refer to al-Durr al-Mukhtar 2/252 and (298)]
And just as they are not originally concluded except in exchange for another right, they cease with the cessation of that corresponding right, just as every derivative falls with the fall of its origin. So if the creditor releases the original debtor, the suretyship also drops from the surety following the dropping of the debt. The same is said regarding mortgage, for it becomes void by the creditor's release of the debtor. (1)
(1) In the book 'Theory of Contract' by Professor al-Sanhuri (p. 150), the definition of accessory contract is: 'It is the contract that is accessory to a prior original contract.'
From what we have explained, it becomes clear that this definition is not sound for the following observations: 1 - Because mortgage and suretyship may be concluded as security for a debt arising from an act, not from a contract at all, such as suretyship and mortgage in exchange for guarantee of compensation for destruction. 2 - Because debts arising from a contract, such as the price of a sale, the consideration of a compromise, and the rent of a leased object, mortgage and suretyship in exchange for them are not accessory to the contract that created the right or obligation, but rather accessory to the right arising from that contract. Hence, if the seller releases the buyer from the price, the surety for the price is released, and the mortgage taken for it also drops, while the contract of sale remains effective. - And because the accessory contract may be accessory to a right that will come into existence after it, and it is not necessary that it existed before it, such as a mortgage for a promised debt and suretyship for what will arise and be established from rights for the guarantee beneficiary against the guaranteed person, as we have explained above. The correct definition of accessory contract, as we have done, is: 'It is the contract that is accessory to another right.'
647
Source pages p. 614

Commentary
This passage from Chapter 47 of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam deals with the classification of contracts, specifically accessory contracts (al-'aqd al-taba'i). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by giving examples: a mortgage can be concluded for a promised debt, and suretyship can be concluded for a debt that will arise in the future. These contracts are valid even though the principal right does not yet exist at the time of the accessory contract. The key characteristic is that these contracts are dependent on a right; they cannot exist independently. If the principal right is extinguished, the accessory contract also falls. For example, if the creditor releases the debtor, the surety is also released, and the mortgage is void. After stating these principles, the author critiques the definition of accessory contract given by the modern legal scholar al-Sanhuri, who defined it as a contract that is accessory to a prior original contract. Al-Zarqa points out three flaws: first, accessory contracts may secure debts arising from a tort (act) rather than a contract; second, even when the principal right arises from a contract, the accessory contract is accessory to the right, not the contract—e.g., if the seller releases the buyer from the price, the surety is released but the sale contract remains; third, the accessory contract may be concluded before the principal right exists (e.g., for a promised debt). Therefore, the correct definition is that an accessory contract is one that is accessory to another right, not necessarily a prior contract. This shows the precision required in legal definitions in Hanafi fiqh, where the nature of the right—rather than the contract—is the central element.

Key Terms
العقد التبعي (al-'aqd al-taba'i): A contract that is accessory to another right (haqq), not necessarily to a prior contract.
الرهن (al-rahn): A contract making property a security for a debt, valid even for a promised debt.
الكفالة (al-kafala): A contract of suretyship where a person assumes liability for a debt that may arise in the future.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is accessory to a prior original contract.
B) It is accessory to another right, whether existing or future.
C) It can only be concluded for an existing debt.
D) It is a contract that creates a right.

Key Takeaway
The correct definition of an accessory contract is that it is accessory to a right, not necessarily a prior contract.

Origin and Definition of Legal Sanctions: Acts vs Transactions
Defines two key categories of human actions under sharia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'a'māl and mu'āmalāt, distinguish between them based on intention, and apply the distinction to determine when rights arise under Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Rights can arise from unintentional acts, but require intent in transactions.

Sharia is an obligatory system that governs human relationships. To understand how legal sanctions enforce compliance, we must first define the two categories of human actions: acts (a'māl) and transactions (mu'āmalāt). This lesson clarifies their definitions and the role of intention in each.

Guided Path
1
State the purpose of sharia legislation
Identify why sharia was established: to organize relationships, safeguard interests, establish justice, and prevent aggression.
This provides the teleological context for legal sanctions.
2
Define 'a'māl (acts)
Read the definition: physical actions that can produce legal rights even without intent. List the examples given.
Key: intention is not required for rights to arise from acts.
3
Define mu'āmalāt (transactions)
Read the definition: civil dispositions intended to create or extinguish rights. Note the subtypes: unilateral and bilateral.
Key: intention is essential for transactions.
4
Distinguish 'a'māl from mu'āmalāt
Compare the two definitions. The critical difference is intention: acts may create rights without intention, while transactions require it.
This distinction affects liability and enforcement of sanctions.
5
Apply the distinction to a concrete scenario
Consider the author's example: walking (an act) must obey traffic rules. If someone walks on another's land, a right (trespass) arises even without intent to cause harm.
Sanctions enforce compliance even for unintentional acts.

الفصل الثامن والأربعون
منشأ المؤيدات، وتعريفها، وتقسيمها المطلب الأول: منشأ المؤيدات
١/٤٨ - الشرائع إنما تؤسس لتنظيم علاقات الناس، ولصيانة مصالحهم العامة والخاصة وإقامة العدل ومنع العدوان بينهم.

Chapter Forty-Eight: Origin of the Sanctions, Their Definition, and Classification. Section One: Origin of the Sanctions. 1/48 - The laws are established only to organize the relationships of people, to safeguard their public and private interests, to establish justice, and to prevent aggression among them. Among the most important characteristics of practical legislation is that it is mandatory: the legally responsible persons must submit to it, respect its commands and prohibitions, and follow the path it has laid out for them in their acts and transactions that generate rights among them. - By 'acts' is meant all physical actions that emanate from them, such as walking, speaking, eating, drinking, riding, using, consuming, possessing, etc., from which a right can arise, even if the doer did not intend by his action to create this right. By 'transactions' is meant civil dispositions, i.e., those by whose direct performance rights are created or dropped between two or more persons, whether they are unilateral acts of will, such as divorce, relinquishment, and endowment; or bilateral contracts, such as sale and partnership (cf. vol. 3, p. 68). 2/48 - Thus, in all acts and transactions, the system of legislation must be observed and its commands respected: a) In ordinary acts, if a person, for example, walks about his affairs and needs, he must abide by the traffic system and not take another's land as a path.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is an introductory explanation of the origin of legal sanctions (mu'ayyidāt) in Islamic law. The author begins by stating the purpose of sharia: to organize human relationships, protect interests, establish justice, and prevent aggression. He emphasizes that a key feature of practical legislation is its binding nature – mukallafūn (legally responsible persons) must submit to its commands and prohibitions. The passage then provides precise definitions: 'a'māl (acts) are material actions that can give rise to legal rights, regardless of the actor's intent to create those rights. Examples include walking, speaking, eating, etc. 'Mu'āmalāt (transactions) are civil dispositions intended to create or extinguish rights between parties, whether unilateral (like divorce, ibrā', waqf) or bilateral (like sale, partnership). Finally, the author gives a concrete example: in ordinary acts like walking, one must follow traffic rules and not trespass on another's land. This lays the groundwork for the necessity of sanctions to enforce compliance.

Key Terms
أعمال (a'māl): Physical actions that can produce legal effects, regardless of intent.
معاملات (mu'āmalāt): Civil dispositions intended to create or transfer rights between parties.
مؤيدات (mu'ayyidāt): Legal sanctions or mechanisms that enforce compliance with sharia rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of mu'āmalāt according to the passage?
A) Walking on a public road.
B) Eating food that belongs to someone else unintentionally.
C) Divorcing one's wife.
D) Speaking loudly during a conversation.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between acts (a'māl) and transactions (mu'āmalāt) hinges on intention: acts can create rights without intent, transactions require intent.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Basis of Legal Sanctions (Mu'ayyidat)
Introduces the concept of legal sanctions and their role in enforcing sharia regulations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between material acts (a'mal) and civil transactions (mu'amalat) in Islamic legal regulation and explain the function of legal sanctions (mu'ayyidat) in compelling compliance.

Core Idea
Legal sanctions make violation unproductive, compelling obedience by ensuring only the lawful path yields desired results.

This passage introduces the fundamental concept of legal sanctions (mu'ayyidat) in Islamic jurisprudence. It clarifies that law regulates both everyday actions and formal transactions, and that for law to be effective, the Lawgiver must attach consequences that render non-compliance futile. Understanding this framework is essential for anyone studying the enforcement mechanisms of Sharia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of regulated conduct
The passage distinguishes between a'mal (material acts) and mu'amalat (civil transactions). Note that a'mal can generate rights even without intention.
2
Define each category with examples
A'mal: walking, speaking, possession. Mu'amalat: divorce, sale, waqf. Understand the difference in the role of intention.
3
List the legal obligations in each category
For a'mal: respect others' property/dignity, follow traffic rules, etc. For mu'amalat: follow formalities, capacity, known subject, etc.
4
Explain the necessity of sanctions (mu'ayyidat)
The Lawgiver arranges that violations lead to nullity or lack of legal effect, so that the desired result is only achieved through compliance.

فيجب مثلاً في العقد أن يحصل تبادل الإرادتين بصورة جازمة واضحة، وأن يكون العاقد ذا أهلية للعقد، وأن يكون المعقود عليه معلوماً ليمكن التنفيذ وقطع النزاع بلا إشكال وأن لا يكون موضوعه عملاً أو أمراً يمنعه الشرع، وأن يجري وفقاً للمراسم الشكلية التي يفرضها التشريع القائم؛ بأن يكون العقد مثلاً معلناً بحضرة شهود فيما يجب فيه الإشهاد كالزواج، وأن يكون مكتوباً أو مسجلاً بالطرق المخصوصة فيما يجب فيه الكتابة أو التسجيل كالشركات القانونية والوكالة بالخصومة، وكبيع العقار اليوم في مثل بلادنا، وذلك لأن هذه القيود من شرائط ومراسم، إنما فرضها التشريع تنظيماً وضبطاً ودفعاً لمحاذير ورعاية لمصالح، فيجب أن يتقيد بها الناس في معاملاتهم كلما أوجب التشريع شيئاً منها في شيء من المعاملات .

Chapter Forty-Eight: The Origin of Legal Sanctions (Mu'ayyidat), Their Definition, and Their Division. First Demand: The Origin of Legal Sanctions. 1/48 - Laws are only established to organize the relations of people, to safeguard their public and private interests, to establish justice, and to prevent aggression among them. Among the most important characteristics of practical legislation is that it is obligatory: those obligated must submit to it, respect its commands and prohibitions, and adhere to the path it has traced for them in their actions (a'mal) and transactions (mu'amalat) that generate rights among them. - By 'actions' is meant all material acts emanating from them, such as walking, speaking, eating, drinking, riding, using, consuming, possessing, etc., from which a right may arise, even if the doer did not intend by his act to create that right. By 'transactions' is meant civil dispositions, i.e., those by which one intends to create or extinguish rights between two or more persons, whether they are acts of individual will, such as divorce, release, and endowment (waqf); or bilateral contracts, such as sale and partnership (cf. vol. 3, p. 68). 2/48 - So in all actions and transactions, the system of legislation must be observed and its commands respected: A) In ordinary actions, if a person walks, for example, going about his affairs and needs, he must comply with traffic rules and not take another's land as a path. Whoever steals something or takes another's property wrongfully must return what was taken and be punished. Whoever speaks to another or about another must not injure his dignity and sanctity by insult, abuse, or slander. Whoever eats, drinks, uses, consumes, or takes possession must confine his action to the scope of his own wealth and rights or the scope of his authority and competence, so as not to touch therein anything of another's wealth or rights; and so on. B) In transactions, including contracts and other civil dispositions, a person must adhere to the methods, formalities, forms, and all conditions that legislation specifies and requires, because it only requires them for a benefit (maslahah) determined by the Lawgiver, and to prevent problems that may arise from failing to observe them. For example, in a contract, there must be an exchange of wills in a decisive and clear manner; the contracting party must have legal capacity; the subject matter must be known to enable execution and settle disputes without difficulty; its subject must not be an act or matter prohibited by law; and it must be conducted according to the formal procedures that the existing legislation imposes—such as the contract being publicly announced in the presence of witnesses where witnessing is required, like marriage; and being written or registered by specific means where writing or registration is required, such as corporate entities, agency for litigation, and today's sale of real estate in countries like ours. This is because these restrictions of conditions and formalities have been imposed by legislation for organization, control, prevention of harms, and observance of interests, so people must adhere to them in their transactions whenever legislation requires any of them in any transaction. 3/48 - So for regulatory legislation to be respected and obeyed in its command and prohibition, there must be alongside it rulings and arrangements that guarantee this respect and compel people to obey it. That is by the Lawgiver arranging, upon violation of His command, that the violating path be rough, barren of outcomes, so that a person finds the fruit he seeks from his work and endeavor only in following the paths that He has designated.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage opens the forty-eighth chapter, which introduces the theory of legal sanctions (al-mu'ayyidat) in Islamic jurisprudence. The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by stating the purpose of law: to organize human relations, protect interests, establish justice, and prevent aggression. He emphasizes that practical legislation is mandatory (ilzami), meaning that those addressed (mukallafun) must comply with its commands and prohibitions. He then distinguishes between two categories regulated by law: actions (a'mal) and transactions (mu'amalat). Actions are material acts that may give rise to rights even without intention (e.g., walking on another's land can create a right of way). Transactions are legal dispositions intended to create or extinguish rights between parties, whether unilateral (divorce, release, waqf) or bilateral (sale, partnership). The passage then details specific obligations in each category: in actions, one must respect others' property, dignity, and rights; in transactions, one must follow the procedural conditions set by law (e.g., offer and acceptance, capacity, known subject, lawful purpose, and formalities like witnesses in marriage or registration for real estate). Finally, the author explains the necessity of sanctions: for law to be respected, the Lawgiver must attach legal consequences to violations that make the path of non-compliance unproductive. The violating path is made 'rough' and 'barren', meaning that the desired legal result (e.g., ownership, obligation, discharge) is not achieved without following the prescribed ways. This is the core of the theory of mu'ayyidat: the legislator's arrangement that non-compliance leads to nullity or lack of legal effect, ensuring that people obey the law to obtain their objectives.

Key Terms
المؤيدات (al-mu'ayyidat): Legal sanctions or confirmations: the rulings and arrangements that guarantee respect for legislation and compel obedience by making violation unproductive.
التشريع (al-tashri'): Legislation: the body of divine commands and prohibitions that regulate human actions and transactions.
الأعمال (al-a'mal): Material acts (physical actions) that may give rise to rights even without intention.
المعاملات (al-mu'amalat): Civil dispositions and transactions intended to create or extinguish rights between parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary purpose of attaching sanctions (mu'ayyidat) to legal obligations?
A) To punish violators harshly.
B) To make the path of compliance more difficult.
C) To ensure that legislation is respected and obeyed by making violation unproductive.
D) To create additional revenue through fines.

Key Takeaway
Legal sanctions (mu'ayyidat) ensure obedience by making non-compliance unproductive, so that the lawful path is the only way to achieve desired legal outcomes.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

The Theory of Legal Sanctions (Mu'ayyidat) in Islamic Jurisprudence
Introducing the theory of legal sanctions as supporting legislations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mu'ayyidat, distinguish them from original regulatory legislation, and explain their necessity for the binding force of Shariah rulings.

Core Idea
Legal sanctions (mu'ayyidat) are supporting rulings that compel obedience and give legislation its binding force.

This passage from a modern work on Islamic jurisprudence introduces the concept of legal sanctions (مؤيدات). The author argues that for Shariah legislation to be effective and binding, it must be accompanied by rulings that ensure compliance. These supporting rulings are called mu'ayyidat, and without them, legislation would be reduced to mere advice. The passage also connects this to the broader maqasid framework, referencing al-Shatibi's classification of interests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Legislation without binding force is ineffective; people will violate it if violation yields the same result as obedience.
The author contrasts regulatory legislation with exhortative advice.
2
Understand the Solution
Mu'ayyidat are rulings legislated to support the original legislation and compel obedience, giving it the necessary strength.
They are distinct from the original regulatory rulings.
3
Learn the Terminology
The passage introduces three terms: mu'ayyidat (sanctions), dawamin (guarantees), and zawajir (deterrents). All refer to rulings that ensure compliance.
Classical Hanafi texts use related terms like dawabit.
4
Connect to Maqasid
The footnote summarizes al-Shatibi's classification of masalih into daruriyyat, hajiyyat, and tahsiniyyat, showing that sanctions are linked to the wisdom of legislation.
This highlights the deeper purpose behind sanctions.

Chapter Seven: The Theory of Legal Sanctions in Jurisprudence
And whoever speaks to another or speaks about him must not harm his dignity and sanctity by insult, abuse, or defamation.
That his action must be confined to the scope of his property and rights or the scope of his authority and competence, so he should not thereby encroach on any property or rights of others; and so on...
B - In transactions, such as contracts and all civil dispositions, a person must adhere to the methods, formalities, forms, and all conditions that legislation specifies and requires, because it only requires them for a benefit determined by the Lawgiver, and to prevent problems that may arise from non-observance.
For example, in a contract there must be an exchange of wills in a decisive and clear manner, the contracting party must have legal capacity, the subject matter must be known for execution and to settle disputes without ambiguity, its subject must not be an act or matter prohibited by Shariah, and it must be executed according to the formal procedures required by existing legislation; for instance, the contract must be declared in the presence of witnesses where witnessing is required, such as marriage, and must be written or registered by specific means where writing or registration is required, such as legal companies and agency for litigation, and as with the sale of real estate today in countries like ours. Because these restrictions of conditions and formalities were only imposed by legislation for organization, control, prevention of harms, and consideration of benefits, people must adhere to them in their transactions whenever legislation requires any of them in any transaction.
3/48 - Thus, for regulatory legislation to be respected and obeyed in its commands and prohibitions, there must be alongside it rulings and arrangements that guarantee this sanctity and compel people to obey it, by the Lawgiver attaching to violation of His command what makes the path of violation rugged in paths, fruitless in efforts, so that a person finds the fruit he seeks from his action and endeavor only by following the paths He has specified.

Chapter (48): Origin, Definition, and Division of Sanctions
Otherwise, legislation would lack its binding quality, becoming like exhortative advice rather than binding force, making it easy for people to violate it if violation and obedience yield the same result in attaining the fruit sought by the doer.
Thus, those rulings that are legislated not to organize people's relations and communal interests, but to support the original legislation that regulates those relations and interests, and to compel people to obey it in its commands and prohibitions, are what are called in modern legal terminology: sanctions (مؤيدات) (1). Because they support legislation, i.e., give it the necessary strength. They may also be called ضوامن (guarantees) plural of ضامن, because they guarantee this obedience to the existing Shariah and undertake it. Islamic jurists call them زواجر (deterrents), because they deter from deviating from the path of Shariah and violating its commands (2). They are connected to the subject of the wisdom of legislation and are among its most important topics.
(1) Legal scholars in Egypt have agreed to call them 'الجزاء' (sanction), whereas in our region's terminology they are called: مؤيدات.
Linguistically, التأييد means strengthening, from their saying: 'أيدك الله تأييداً' (May Allah strengthen you). It is derived from 'الأيد' (with fatha and sukun) meaning extreme strength, and from it comes what is in the Great Quran: 'والسماء بنيناها بأيد' (And the heaven We constructed with strength) (see al-Misbah and Mufradat al-Raghib).
It is said: a man 'أيد' (with shadda on the ya, kasra, like 'سيد') meaning strong. So المؤيدات التشريعية are the rulings that give legislation strength and make it awe-inspiring and obeyed.
The French equivalent is the word 'sanctions', meaning penalties and rewards that prevent violation of the law and ensure its implementation (Larousse dictionary).
(2) Imam Abu Ishaq Ibrahim al-Shatibi al-Gharnati, in his book 'al-Muwafaqat fi Usul al-Shariah', provides a valuable explanation related to this topic. We summarize it here for its benefit. He divided interests (masalih) and also the rulings legislated to safeguard them into three categories: necessities (daruriyyat), needs (hajiyyat), and improvements (tahsiniyyat) i.e., embellishments, which have been previously explained (see section 4/5-6). Then he said in summary:
Necessities are of two types:
One of them involves an immediate purposeful benefit for a person, such as a person fulfilling the interests of himself and his family in food, housing, clothing, and what that requires of complements, such as sales = 
665
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage, drawn from a modern work on Islamic jurisprudence, discusses the theory of legal sanctions (مؤيدات). The author begins by emphasizing the necessity of respecting others' dignity and confining one's actions to one's own rights and authority. He then addresses transactions, stressing the need to adhere to prescribed formalities, conditions, and procedures (e.g., witnessing for marriage, writing for certain contracts). The core argument is that for regulatory legislation to be effective, there must be accompanying rulings that ensure compliance. These rulings, termed مؤيدات in modern legal terminology, are what give legislation binding force. The author notes alternative terms: ضوامن (guarantees) and زواجر (deterrents) used by Islamic jurists. The passage also includes a footnote summarizing Imam al-Shatibi's classification of masalih (interests) into necessities, needs, and improvements, linking sanctions to the overarching wisdom of Shariah. The commentary follows the author's order, explaining that without such supporting rulings, legislation becomes mere advice, easily disregarded.

Key Terms
مؤيدات (mu'ayyidāt): Rulings legislated to support the original legislation and compel obedience, giving it binding force.
ضوامن (ḍawāmin): Plural of ضامن; rulings that guarantee obedience to Shariah.
زواجر (zawājir): Deterrent rulings that prevent deviation from Shariah.

Key Takeaway
Mu'ayyidat are essential to give legislation binding force; without them, regulations become advice and easily ignored.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
10 min
Definition and Classification of Legal Sanctions (Mu'ayyidat)
Defining legal sanctions and their classification via al-Shāṭibī's maqāṣid framework

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mu'ayyidat, explain their necessity for binding legislation, and apply al-Shatibi's framework to classify which rulings require deterrent sanctions.

Core Idea
Sanctions transform law from advice into obligation; Sharīʿa imposes them only where natural motives are absent.

Islamic law is not merely a set of recommendations; it carries binding force through sanctions (mu'ayyidat), which jurists also call zawājir (deterrents) or ḍawāmin (guarantors). Without these, the law would resemble moral exhortation. This lesson explores the nature of sanctions and the remarkable insight of Imām al-Shāṭibī, who classified human interests into those with immediate personal benefit—where natural inclination suffices—and those without, where the Lawgiver imposes strict deterrents to ensure compliance. Understanding this distinction is crucial for any mufti weighing whether a new regulation should carry a penalty.

Guided Path
1
Define the Central Concept
Read the opening definition: sanctions (mu'ayyidat) are rulings that support original legislation and compel obedience. Note the alternative terms: zawājir (deterrents) and ḍawāmin (guarantors). Ask yourself: why are these necessary?
The author stresses that without sanctions, legislation loses its binding character.
2
Identify the Two Categories of Interests
Now turn to al-Shāṭibī's classification. He divides obligatory matters into two types: (1) those with immediate personal benefit (ḥaẓẓ ʿājil)—e.g., earning a living, trade—where human nature provides sufficient motivation; and (2) those without immediate personal benefit—e.g., avoiding murder, theft—where nature inclines toward violation.
This is not about the ruling itself (wājib vs. mandūb) but about the presence or absence of sanctions.
3
Connect Interest Type to Sanction Strength
Observe the Lawgiver's policy: for the first type, sanctions are minimal or absent; the law is stated permissively (e.g., 'Allāh has permitted trade'). For the second type, strong deterrents are imposed (ḥadd punishments, taʿzīr). Understand the reason: natural drives already push people to seek profit; but no natural drive pushes them to avoid harming others.
This is a teleological (maqāṣid) argument: the purpose of sanctions is to fill the gap where natural motivation is lacking.
4
Apply the Framework to a New Case
Consider a modern scenario: a law requiring halal certification for food products. Is this a matter with immediate personal benefit (consumers want assurance, businesses profit) or without? Determine whether a strong penalty (e.g., heavy fine) is needed or whether market incentives suffice.
Your answer will affect the fatwa on whether a punitive sanction should be attached.
5
Summarize the Legal Wisdom
Conclude by articulating the hikma (wisdom) behind this differentiation: Sharīʿa economizes on coercion, using it only where necessary, and relies on natural human drives otherwise. This reflects the principle of ease (yusr) and proportionality.
This insight is fundamental for a mufti when advising on the design of Islamic regulations.

الشارع. وإلا فإن التشريع يكون فاقداً صفته الإلزامية، فيكون عندئذ أشبه بالمواعظ الإرشادية منه بالقوة الملزمة، فتسهل على الناس مخالفته إذا كانت مخالفته كطاعته من حيث النتيجة في تحصيل الثمرة التي يسعى إليها الفاعل.
فتلك الأحكام التي تشرع لا لتنظيم علاقات الناس ومصالح الجماعة، بل لتأييد أصل التشريع الناظم لتلك العلاقات والمصالح، وإلجاء الناس إلى طاعته في أمره ونهيه هي ما تسمى في اصطلاح الحقوق الحديثة : مؤيدات (۱) . وذلك لأنها تؤيد التشريع، أي تكسبه القوة اللازمة. وقد تسمى أيضاً ضوامن جمع ضامن لأنها تضمن هذه الطاعة للشرع القائم وتتكفل بها. وفقهاء الإسلام يسمونها زواجر، لأنها تزجر عن تنكب جادة الشرع ومخالفة أوامره (۲) ، وهي تتصل بموضوع حكمة التشريع وتعد من أهم مباحثه
(۱) اصطلح القانونيون في مصر على تسميتها بالجزاء، أما في اصطلاح بلادنا فتسمى : مؤيدات . والتأييد في اللغة هو التقوية، ومنه قولهم : أيدك الله تأييداً. وهو مشتق من «الأيد» بفتح فسكون بمعنى القوة الشديدة، ومنه ما جاء في القرآن العظيم والسماء بنينتها بَايْدٍ (ر المصباح، ومفردات الراغب). ويقال : رجل «أيد) بتشديد الياء المكسورة وزان «سيد» أي قوي. فالمؤيدات التشريعية هي الأحكام التي تكسب التشريع قوة وتجعله مهيباً مطاعاً. ويقابلها في اللغة الفرنسية كلمة sanctions ومعناها الجزاء والمكافآت التي تمنع مخالفة القانون وتكفل تنفيذه (معجم لاروس).

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical discussion of legal sanctions in Islamic law. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, defines sanctions as rulings that support primary legislation by compelling obedience. He notes that without sanctions, law becomes mere advice. The passage then cites al-Shatibi's classification of interests (masalih) into necessities, needs, and improvements. Al-Shatibi divides obligatory matters into two types: those with immediate personal benefit (like earning a living) where the Lawgiver does not impose strong sanctions, allowing natural human drives to operate; and those without immediate personal benefit (like prohibitions of murder, theft) where the Lawgiver imposes strict sanctions because human nature inclines toward violation. This explains why some Sharia rulings have strong punishments and others do not.

Key Terms
المؤيدات (al-mu'ayyidat): الأحكام التي تشرع لتأييد التشريع الأصلي وإلجاء الناس إلى طاعته
الزواجر (al-zawajir): تسمية فقهاء الإسلام للمؤيدات لأنها تزجر عن المخالفة
الضوامن (al-dawamin): جمع ضامن، سميت بها لأنها تضمن طاعة الشرع

Definition and Role of Shar'i Supports
Definition and classification of Shar'i supports

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of Shar'i supports and distinguish them from original rulings.

Core Idea
Shar'i supports are protective rulings that safeguard the original Shari'ah system, like an army protects a country.

This lesson introduces the theory of Shar'i supports as presented by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa. Understanding the distinction between original and supporting rulings is essential for a mufti to determine the appropriate level of enforcement in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of rulings in complete legislation
Read the passage and find where the author states that complete legislation consists of original rulings (الأحكام الأصلية) and supporting rulings (الأحكام التأييدية). Note the function of each.
Original rulings form the core system; supporting rulings protect them.
2
Locate the definition of Shar'i supports
Find the sentence that begins with 'يمكننا تعريف المؤيدات الشرعية' and state the definition in your own words.
The definition emphasizes compulsion (حمل) to obey original rulings.
3
Understand the legislative policy for actions with immediate benefit vs. without
The author contrasts two types of actions: those with immediate personal benefit (like trade) and those without (like jihad). Note how the level of enforcement differs.
Actions with immediate benefit are left more permissive; actions without require strict obligation and deterrents.
4
Appreciate the army analogy
Read the analogy comparing Shar'i supports to an army protecting a country. Explain why this analogy is used and what happens if supports are lost.
The analogy emphasizes the necessity of supports for the preservation of the Shari'ah system.

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
المطلب الثاني: تعريف المؤيدات
٤/٤٨ - بناء على ما تقدم يمكننا تعريف المؤيدات الشرعية بأنها هي: كل ما يشرع من التدابير لحمل الناس على طاعة أحكام الشريعة الأصلية.
وبعدما سبق بيانه يصبح هذا التعريف واضحاً بلا حاجة إلى إيضاح. فالتشريع الكامل يتضمن دائماً نوعين من الأحكام:

مع
. وأما في النوع الثاني، وهو ما ليس للإنسان فيه حظ مباشر، فإن الشرع يؤكد الطلب فيه ويوجبه إيجاباً، لأنه ليس له في طبع الإنسان داع إليه يخدمه ويحمل الإنسان على فعله بل هو الضد من ذلك - أي إن في نفس الإنسان ما يدعوه إلى المخالفة - فلذا يؤكد الطلب فيه، ويكف عن المخالفة بالزجر والتأديب وذلك كالنهي عن قتل النفس والزنى والسرقة والخمر وأكل الربا وأكل أموال الناس بالباطل وأشباه ذلك، لأن الطبع النازع بالإنسان إلى طلب حظوظه ومصلحته ودرء مضرته يدعوه إلى الوقوع على هذه الأشياء، فيحتاج إلى الزواجر عنها .اهـ (الموافقات) بتلخيص وتصرف من النوعين الأول والرابع من أنواع مقاصد الشارع، ج ۲ ص ۸ - ۱۲ و ۱۸۰ - ۱۸۳

الفصل (٤٨) منشأ المؤيدات وتعريفها وتقسيمها
والعلاقات والمعاملات وتجب حماية حدودها من تجاوز الناس عليها.
وأحكاماً تأييدية لأجل حماية تلك الأحكام الأصلية.
فالمؤيدات الشرعية هي الأحكام الحامية.
والنظام الأصلي الذي شُرِعَت المؤيدات لحمايته هي الأحكام المحمية .
:
فالمؤيدات بالنسبة إلى أصل الشريعة كالجيش بالنسبة إلى البلاد ومصالحها يذود عنها، ويحمي حدودها، وينكل بمن يتجاوز عليها. وإذا فقد الجيش الحامي أو اضمحلت قوته استبيحت البلاد، وزال سلطان الدولة. فكذلك إذا فقدت المؤيدات التشريعية هتكت حرمة الشريعة ولم يبق لها سلطان .
المطلب الثالث : تقسيم المؤيدات :
٥/٤٨ - المؤيدات الترغيبية والمؤيدات الترهيبية :
إن المؤيدات الشرعية تنقسم إلى نوعين أساسيين متعاكسين، وذلك بحسب كون المقصود الشرعي بها هو حمل الناس على فعل، أو منعهم عنه . وهذان النوعان الأساسيان هما: المؤيدات الترغيبية، والمؤيدات الترهيبية .
أ - فعندما يكون غرض الشارع تشويق الناس إلى فعل مرغوب التحصيل، وتنشيطهم إليه، وحملهم على تحمل مشاقه وأعبائه، يرتب على الإقدام عليه مكافأة ومنحة ترغبهم فيه :
وذلك كما تعفي القوانين المالية اليوم المنشآت الزراعية والصناعية من الضرائب المطروحة على أمثالها عدة سنوات منذ إنشائها، وكما تسن أنظمة لمنح الرواتب المالية التي ترتب للمتزوجين من الشباب، وكالتعويضات العائلية التي توظف لهم، وتقرر على عدد أولادهم في بعض الدول ترغيباً في الزواج.
٦٦٧
Chapter Seven: The Theory of Shar'i Supports in Fiqh
Second Section: Definition of Supports
4/48 - Based on what has preceded, we can define Shar'i supports as: All measures legislated to compel people to obey the original rulings of the Shari'ah.
After what has been explained, this definition becomes clear without need for elaboration. For complete legislation always includes two types of rulings:
- Original rulings, which constitute the system of Shari'ah that organizes interests, rentals, marriages, and other aspects of acquisition by which human structures - i.e., human existence - are established.
- And the second: in which there is no immediate benefit intended for the mukallaf, such as education, jihad, non-aggression against the rights of others, and similar matters that are legislated generally for public interests; if people were assumed to abandon them, the system would break down.
And the policy of Islamic legislation in achieving obedience to its system, which aims to compel man to establish these interests, is that:
In the first type: which involves an immediate benefit, the law does not emphasize the demand upon the person with respect to himself - i.e., it does not attach to it the deterrent guarantees that the emphasis would require. Rather, legislation takes a position closer to permissibility than to emphasized demand. Like His saying: "And Allah has permitted trade" and His saying: "Eat from the good things We have provided you" and the like, because the world is only established through management and acquisition. So this stance of the Lawgiver is like a referral to the innate motivations in man that drive acquisition.
As for the second type: which man has no direct benefit in, the law emphasizes the demand and makes it obligatory, because man has no innate inclination that serves and drives him to do it; rather, it is the opposite - that is, within man's soul is something that calls him to violation. Therefore, the demand is emphasized in it, and prevention from violation is done by deterrence and discipline, such as the prohibition of killing, adultery, theft, wine, usury, consuming people's wealth unjustly, and the like. Because man's natural inclination to seek his desires and benefit and repel harm calls him to fall into these things, so deterrents are needed against them. End quote from al-Muwafaqat, with abridgment and adaptation from the first and fourth types of the Lawgiver's objectives, vol. 2 pp. 8-12 and 180-183.

Section (48): Origin, Definition, and Division of Supports
And relations and transactions require protecting their boundaries from people's transgression.
And supporting rulings for the protection of those original rulings.
So Shar'i supports are the protecting rulings.
And the original system for whose protection the supports were legislated are the protected rulings.

So supports in relation to the Shari'ah are like an army to a country and its interests: it defends it, protects its borders, and punishes those who transgress against it. If the protecting army is lost or its strength diminishes, the country becomes vulnerable and the state's authority vanishes. Similarly, if legislative supports are lost, the sanctity of the Shari'ah is violated and it has no authority.

Third Section: Division of Supports
5/48 - Incentive Supports and Deterrent Supports:
Indeed, Shar'i supports are divided into two basic opposing types, according to whether the legislative purpose is to compel people to do an action or to prevent them from it. These two basic types are: incentive supports and deterrent supports.

A - When the Lawgiver's aim is to encourage people to a desirable action, energize them towards it, and compel them to bear its hardships and burdens, He attaches to undertaking it a reward and a grant that motivates them towards it.
Such as when today's financial laws exempt agricultural and industrial establishments from taxes applicable to similar ones for several years from their establishment, and as when systems are enacted to grant financial allowances to married youth, and family compensations allocated to them, and determined by the number of their children in some countries, as an incentive for marriage.
667
Source pages p. 628

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm is a legal philosophy discussion introducing the theory of Shar'i supports (المؤيدات الشرعية). The author defines Shar'i supports as all measures (تدابير) legislated to compel people to obey the original rulings of the Shari'ah. He explains that complete legislation consists of two types: original rulings (الأحكام الأصلية) that form the core system governing interests like trade, rent, marriage, etc., and supporting rulings (الأحكام التأييدية) that protect those originals. 

Dr. al-Zarqa then elaborates on the legislative policy of the Shari'ah in two cases: (1) For actions that give an immediate personal benefit (e.g., trade), the Shari'ah does not impose heavy deterrents; rather, it takes a permissive stance, relying on human nature's innate drive to seek benefit. (2) For actions that provide no immediate personal gain (e.g., jihad, avoiding aggression), the Shari'ah emphasizes strict obligation and attaches deterrents because human nature tends to avoid such actions.

On page 629, he analogizes supports to an army protecting a country, emphasizing their necessity. Then he divides supports into two types: incentive (الترغيبية) and deterrent (الترهيبية). Incentive supports use rewards to encourage actions, while deterrent supports use punishments to discourage violations. He gives contemporary examples like tax exemptions for new industries and marriage allowances to illustrate incentive supports. This is a modern application of classical fiqh principles to state policy.

Key Terms
المؤيدات الشرعية (al-mu'ayyidat al-shar'iyyah): All measures legislated by the Shari'ah to compel people to obey the original rulings of the Shari'ah. They are protective rulings that safeguard the original system.
الأحكام الأصلية (al-ahkam al-asliyyah): The primary rulings that constitute the core system of Shari'ah, governing interests, transactions, and individual actions.
الأحكام التأييدية (al-ahkam al-ta'yidiyyah): Supporting rulings that protect the original rulings, like an army protects a country.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary function of Shar'i supports?
A) To replace original rulings when they are not followed
B) To protect and enforce the original rulings of the Shari'ah
C) To create new independent obligations
D) To provide financial incentives for worship

Key Takeaway
Shar'i supports are protective rulings that safeguard the original Shari'ah system, and their enforcement varies based on whether the action provides immediate personal benefit.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
6 min
Classification of Shar'i Supports: Incentive and Deterrent
Division of Shar'i supports into incentive and deterrent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify Shar'i supports into incentive and deterrent types and apply them to practical scenarios.

Core Idea
Shar'i supports are either incentive (reward-based) or deterrent (punishment-based), depending on whether the aim is to encourage or prevent an action.

This lesson examines the division of Shar'i supports into incentive and deterrent types. Understanding this classification helps a mufti design effective fatwas that align with the Shari'ah's objectives, whether encouraging desirable acts or preventing harmful ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two basic types of supports
Read the third section of the passage where the author divides supports into two opposing types. Note the basis of the division: whether the aim is to compel an action or prevent it.
The types are incentive (ترغيبية) and deterrent (ترهيبية).
2
Understand the mechanism of incentive supports
When the Lawgiver wants to encourage an action, He attaches a reward or grant. Find the description and the modern examples given by the author.
Examples: tax exemptions, marriage allowances.
3
Understand the mechanism of deterrent supports
For prohibited actions, the Lawgiver uses deterrents. Although the passage focuses on incentive supports, note the earlier mention of deterrents for actions like theft and murder.
Deterrents include punishments and penalties.
4
Apply the classification to a new scenario
Think of a modern issue, such as a government offering subsidies for halal certification. Determine whether this is an incentive or deterrent support and why.
Subsidies are incentives; fines for non-compliance are deterrents.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Definition and Classification of Legal Sanctions (Mu'ayyidat)
Defines legal sanctions and classifies them into incentive and deterrent types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define legal sanctions, classify them into incentive and deterrent types, and apply the classification to examples from Islamic law.

Core Idea
Legal sanctions in Sharia are not only punishments but also rewards that encourage compliance.

This lesson introduces the concept of legal sanctions (mu'ayyidat) in Islamic jurisprudence, explaining their role as protective rulings for the original Sharia. We will explore the two fundamental types—incentive and deterrent—and see how incentive sanctions operate through examples like tanfil in jihad and the hadith on intentions.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition and analogy
Read the opening of the passage where the author defines legal sanctions as supportive rulings (ahkam hima'iyyah) that protect the original Sharia. Understand the army analogy: sanctions defend Sharia like an army defends a country.
The analogy emphasizes that without sanctions, the authority of Sharia collapses.
2
Identify the two types of sanctions
The author divides sanctions into two main categories: incentive (targhibiyyah) and deterrent (tarhibiyyah). Note that this classification is based on whether the legislator aims to encourage or prevent action.
These are opposite but complementary: one attracts, the other repels.
3
Examine the incentive examples
Study the three examples provided: tanfil (spoils in jihad), graduated zakat on livestock, and the hadith qudsi about intentions. For each, identify how the incentive works—reward, tax relief, or divine grace.
The hadith shows that even the mere intention of good deeds is rewarded, a powerful incentive.
4
Connect to maqasid al-Shariah
Reflect on how these incentives serve higher objectives: tanfil encourages courage in defense of Islam, zakat relief supports agriculture and animal husbandry, and the hadith encourages heartfelt righteousness. This shows sanctions are not arbitrary but tied to maqasid.
Incentive sanctions are a tool for realizing the goals of Shariah.
5
Apply the concept to a modern scenario
Consider a contemporary situation (e.g., tax breaks for charitable organizations). Determine whether it aligns with the category of incentive sanctions and explain why using the principles from the passage.
The mufti can use this classification to evaluate new regulations.

... and relations and transactions, and it is obligatory to protect their boundaries from people transgressing upon them.
And supportive rulings for the sake of protecting those original rulings.
So the legal sanctions are the protecting rulings.
And the original system for which the sanctions were legislated is the protected rulings.
:
So the sanctions in relation to the origin of Sharia are like the army in relation to the country and its interests: it defends it, protects its borders, and punishes those who transgress upon them. If the protecting army is lost or its strength weakens, the country is plundered and the authority of the state disappears. Likewise, if the legislative sanctions are lost, the sanctity of Sharia is violated and it has no authority.
The Third Demand: Division of Sanctions:
5/48 - The Incentive Sanctions and the Deterrent Sanctions:
Indeed, the legal sanctions are divided into two basic opposite types, according to whether the legislative intent is to impel people to do an act or to prevent them from it. These two basic types are: the incentive sanctions and the deterrent sanctions.
a - When the objective of the Lawgiver is to encourage people to do a desirable act, to stimulate them towards it, and to make them bear its hardships and burdens, He attaches to undertaking it a reward and a gift that attracts them to it:
For example, as today's financial laws exempt agricultural and industrial establishments from taxes imposed on similar establishments for several years from their establishment, and as systems are enacted to grant financial salaries that are arranged for married young people, and family allowances that are assigned to them and determined on the number of their children in some countries as an incentive for marriage.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Sanctions in Jurisprudence
a dead person.
Of this kind in Islam is the legislated 'tanfil' (spoils incentive) in battles of armed jihad, and 'tanfil' is the right of the commander or leader to announce to the soldiers that whoever kills an enemy will have his spoils, as an incentive for the soldiers and strengthening their resolve, as the Prophet (peace be upon him) did in the battle of Hunain. Then each fighter deserves the spoils of his killed enemy, making it a special gift for him.
Following this same course is also the method that Islam prescribed for the zakat tax on livestock, as it adopted the method of decreasing percentage, because raising livestock involves hardship and general benefit that require encouragement, so its tax was lightened by a percentage inversely proportional to the abundance of what a person owns of them, until the tax is fixed at a single percentage after the amount that an ordinary person can possess and care for.

Moreover, since Islam is a reformative system for the three aspects of life: spiritual, material, and civil together, therefore the otherworldly incentive sanctions in Islam are very many, especially concerning good deeds, sincerity of action, reformation of conditions, obligatory sacrifices, and preferring public interest over private interest when they conflict.
Of this kind is what came from the Prophet (peace be upon him): that whoever intends a good deed but is not able to do it, a good deed is recorded for him and he deserves its otherworldly reward from Allah Almighty; and whoever intends a bad deed but does not do it, nothing is recorded against him.
(1) Narrated by Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both) from the Prophet (peace be upon him) in what he narrates from his Lord, Mighty and Majestic: 'Indeed Allah has written down the good deeds and the bad deeds, then He clarified that. So whoever intends a good deed but does not do it, Allah writes it down with Himself as a complete good deed; but if he intends it and does it, Allah writes it down with Himself as ten good deeds, up to seven hundred times, up to many multiples. And if he intends a bad deed but does not do it, Allah writes it down with Himself as a complete good deed; but if he intends it and does it, Allah writes it down as a single bad deed.' Narrated by Bukhari and Muslim.
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' presents the theory of legal sanctions (المؤيدات الشرعية) in Islamic jurisprudence. The author begins by defining these sanctions as protective rulings that safeguard the original rulings of the Sharia, drawing an analogy with an army that defends a country's borders. He then classifies sanctions into two fundamental types: incentive sanctions (المؤيدات الترغيبية) and deterrent sanctions (المؤيدات الترهيبية). The focus in this section is on the first type—incentive sanctions—which aim to encourage desirable actions by attaching rewards or benefits. Examples from Islamic law include the tanfil (spoils) in jihad, where the leader may promise the spoils of a slain enemy to the soldier who kills him, as happened in the battle of Hunain. Another example is the graduated tax rate for zakat on livestock, which decreases as the herd size increases, lightening the burden to encourage animal husbandry. The passage also highlights the abundance of otherworldly incentives in Islam, citing the hadith qudsi about intentions: one who intends a good deed receives a complete good deed even if not performed, while one who intends a bad deed but refrains receives a good deed. This demonstrates the mercy and encouragement in Islamic law.

Key Terms
المؤيدات الشرعية (al-mu'ayyidat al-shar'iyyah): Supportive legal rulings enacted to protect the primary rulings of the Sharia from violation.
المؤيدات الترغيبية (al-mu'ayyidat al-targhibiyyah): Incentive sanctions that encourage people to perform desirable acts by promising rewards.
المؤيدات الترهيبية (al-mu'ayyidat al-tarhibiyyah): Deterrent sanctions that discourage people from prohibited acts by threatening punishment.
التنفيل (al-tanfil): The right of a military commander to grant additional spoils to soldiers as an incentive in battle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary purpose of legal sanctions (المؤيدات الشرعية)?
A) To punish criminals severely
B) To protect the original rulings of the Sharia from violation
C) To replace the original rulings with new ones
D) To create a separate legal system

Key Takeaway
Legal sanctions protect Sharia rulings through both positive incentives (like rewards and tax relief) and negative deterrents, reflecting the comprehensive nature of Islamic law.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

Theory of Legal Sanctions: Definition and Classification
Defines and classifies Shari'ah legal sanctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define Shari'ah legal sanctions (al-mu'ayyidat al-shar'iyyah) and classify them into incentive and deterrent types, identifying their subcategories and purposes.

Core Idea
Shari'ah uses both incentive and deterrent sanctions to ensure compliance, with deterrent sanctions further divided into penal and civil.

This passage introduces the theory of legal sanctions in Islamic jurisprudence. Al-Zarqa explains that the Shari'ah does not rely solely on punishment but employs a balanced system of incentives (targhib) and deterrents (tarhib) to guide human behavior. Through examples from jihad, zakat, and the hadith on intentions, he illustrates how both worldly and otherworldly sanctions work together to achieve the maqasid al-shari'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept being defined
Locate the term 'al-mu'ayyidat al-shar'iyyah' and note its overarching definition: reinforcements that ensure compliance with Shari'ah rules.
This is not a hukm taklifi but a mechanism for enforcement.
2
Analyze the examples of incentive sanctions
Examine how tanfil (granting spoils) and decreasing zakat rates on livestock serve as worldly incentives. Consider why these methods encourage desired behavior.
Both examples involve reducing burden or offering reward to promote beneficial acts.
3
Examine the hadith evidence for otherworldly incentives
Read the hadith qudsi about intentions. Recognise how it incentivises good intentions and even refraining from evil intentions by promising reward.
This shows the spiritual dimension of incentive sanctions.
4
Classify the deterrent sanctions
Identify the two types of deterrent sanctions: penal (tadibi) for aggressive crimes and civil (madani) for neglect of conditions. Understand their respective purposes.
Penal includes physical, financial, and freedom-restricting punishments; civil addresses proprietary matters.

Chapter (48): Origin, Definition, and Classification of Sanctions
And this is an incentive sanction to encourage good intentions and desist from evil intentions before
b - And when the Legislator's purpose is to prevent people from violating His command and prohibition, He attaches to the violation a painful deterrent punishment, which is the deterrent sanction guaranteeing prevention of violation.
48/6 - Diversity of the Deterrent Sanction:
This deterrent, preventive sanction is of two types: penal and civil (i.e., proprietary). This is because the wisdom of legislation requires diversifying the deterrent sanction according to the types of violations, following the subject of the legislative system whose sanctity is violated by the violation:
a - If the violating act is of the type of aggressive crimes that disrupt the security of society and the system of the community, then the enforcing punishment must be a penal one with a crushing penalty that deters from committing the violating act that necessitates it. This type is the penal sanction, or punishment, and from it are composed the penalties in the legislation of the state.
This punishment may be physical, financial, freedom-restricting like imprisonment and exile, or moral like publicizing and parading the person. It may be composed of two or more elements.
It is clear that this type of sanction does not guarantee the prevention of violations and the eradication of crime from society, but it is the utmost possible in legislation because the material actions of people cannot be completely controlled. A person may commit a crime and surprise with it such that prevention is impossible, and he is satisfied with the punishment even if it is death.

Source pages p. 630

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm discusses the theory of legal sanctions (المؤيدات الشرعية) in Islamic jurisprudence. The author opens by introducing the concept through examples: the tanfil (granting spoils to a killer) in jihad as an incentive, and the progressive reduction of zakat rates on livestock to encourage animal husbandry. These illustrate the principle that Shari'ah uses incentives to promote beneficial acts. The passage then notes the abundance of otherworldly incentives (المؤيدات الترغيبية الأخروية) for good deeds, citing the famous hadith qudsi about intentions: a good intention is recorded as a full good deed, and an evil intention not acted upon is also recorded as a good deed. This shows how Islamic law encourages virtuous intentions.

On page 631, the author begins Chapter 48 on the origin, definition, and classification of sanctions. He distinguishes two main types: incentive sanctions (ترغيبية) and deterrent sanctions (ترهيبية). The deterrent sanction is further divided into penal (تأديبي) and civil (مدني/حقوقي). The penal sanction applies to aggressive crimes that harm society, and it takes the form of punishments (عقوبات) such as physical, financial, or freedom-restricting. The author notes that despite such punishments, crime cannot be completely eliminated, but it is the best possible deterrent. The civil sanction applies when violations involve neglect of legal conditions, presumably in contractual or proprietary matters (though the passage cuts off before completing this point). The classification is based on the nature of the violation and the purpose of legislation.

Key Terms
المؤيدات الشرعية (al-mu'ayyidat al-shar'iyyah): Legal sanctions or reinforcement mechanisms that ensure compliance with Shari'ah rules, either through incentive (targhib) or deterrent (tarhib).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Shari'ah employs a dual system of incentive and deterrent sanctions, with deterrents further divided into penal and civil, to achieve comprehensive compliance.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Types of Deterrent Sanctions: Disciplinary and Civil
Classifies deterrent sanctions into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between disciplinary and civil deterrent sanctions (al-mu'ayyid al-ta'dibi and al-mu'ayyid al-madani) and explain why the civil sanction is considered more effective in preventing violations.

Core Idea
Disciplinary sanctions punish the violator; civil sanctions nullify the violation's legal effect—the latter cuts off the root.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa explains that Islamic law employs deterrent sanctions (al-mu'ayyid al-tarhibi) to prevent violations of divine commands and prohibitions. These sanctions are not monolithic; they are divided into two types based on the nature of the violation: disciplinary (ta'dibi) for aggressive crimes and civil (madani) for violations of transactional conditions. Understanding this classification is essential for a mufti to determine the appropriate legal response to a given action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of deterrent sanctions
Read the passage and locate where the author divides the deterrent sanction (المؤيد الترهيبي) into two types. Note the Arabic terms: تأديبي (ta'dibi) and مدني (madani).
The text explicitly says: 'وهذا المؤيد الترهيبي الزاجر :نوعان تأديبي، ومدني'.
2
Determine the purpose of each type
For each type, identify the purpose the Lawgiver (الشارع) intends. The disciplinary sanction aims to repress aggressive crimes that disturb societal order, while the civil sanction aims to deprive the violating transaction of its legal results.
Disciplinary = عقوبة قامعة; Civil = حرمان المتصرف من النتائج.
3
Compare the effectiveness of the two types
The author argues that the civil sanction is 'more decisive in eradicating violation' (أقطع لدابر المخالفة) than the disciplinary one. Explain why: because legal results are contingent on the Lawgiver's recognition, unlike physical acts that cannot be fully controlled.
The text contrasts material control vs. legal recognition.
4
Apply the classification to a concrete scenario
Consider a person who sells a stolen item. The act involves theft (disciplinary) and a defective sale (civil). Which sanctions apply? The disciplinary sanction punishes the thief; the civil sanction voids the sale so the buyer gets no ownership.
This shows both types can operate simultaneously.
