نظام
وهذه العقوبة قد تكون بدنية أو مالية أو حاجزة للحرية كالحبس والنفي، أو معنوية كالتشهير بالشخص وتجريسه. وقد تكون مركبة من عنصرين فأكثر .
ومن الواضح أن هذا النوع من المؤيدات لا يضمن منع المخالفات وقطع دابر الإجرام بحيث ينتفي من المجتمع، لكنه كل المستطاع في التشريع لأن أفعال الناس المادية لا يمكن السيطرة عليها سيطرة تامة. فقد يقدم الإنسان على الجريمة ويفاجيء بها بحيث لا تمكن الحيلولة دونها، وهو راض بالعقوبة ولو كانت موتاً.
ب - وإذا كان الفعل المخالف من قبيل إهمال الشرائط التي يفرضها
التشريع في النظام المدني لأنواع معاملات الناس وتصرفاتهم الحقوقية من عقود وغيرها، فإنَّ المؤيد لمنع المخالفة ينبغي أن يكون حرمان المتصرف من النتائج التي يبتغيها من تصرفه وذلك بأن يعتبر عمله في نظر التشريع لغواً، أي مسلوب الحكم والنتيجة، فلا يكسب صاحبه الحق المطلوب، ولا يحتج به أمام القضاء عند الاقتضاء.
وهذا النوع هو المؤيد المدني، أو الحقوقي، وينشأ عنه نظرية البطلان
وتفاريعها في التشريع المدني. ومن الواضح أن هذا النوع من المؤيدات هو أقطع لدابر المخالفة من المؤيد التأديبي، لأن ترتيب النتائج الحقوقية على التصرفات ليس هو أثراً مادياً لفعل محسوس ربما لا تمكن الحيلولة دونه، بل هو أمر اعتباري منوط باعتبار الشارع. فإذا سلب الشارع عن التصرف المخالف لنظامه نتائجه كلها أو بعضها لم يستطع الفاعل بوجه من الوجوه أن يكتسب أمام السلطة القضائية ما نفاه الشارع من الحقوق مادام القضاء هو
الشرعي
المطبق والمنفذ الشريعة الشارع.
٧/٤٨ - درجات الإلغاء :
وهذا الإلغاء لحكم التصرف المخالف ليس درجة واحدة.
١ - فقد يكون إلغاء كلياً، وذلك إذا كانت المخالفة لنظام التعامل وقعت في ناحية أساسية. وعندئذ يسمى المؤيد : بطلاناً.
٢ - وقد يكون إلغاء جزئياً من بعض الوجوه، وذلك إذا كانت المخالفة في ناحية فرعية وعندئذ يسمى المؤيد: فساداً.
٣ - وإذا كان التصرف يمس حقاً محترماً للغير، كمن باع مال غيره بلا إذنه، فإنَّ العقد يكون معتبراً في ذاته إذا استوفى شرائطه، لكنه متوقف من حيث الحكم والثمرة حتى يرضى صاحب الحق الممسوس أو يرفض : فإن رضي بالعقد نفذ، وإن رفض بطل. وهذا يسمى: توقفاً؛ وهو حماية لحق الغير.
Its enforcement.
Chapter (48): Origin of legal sanctions, their definition and division
This is an encouraging sanction to incline towards good intention and to desist from bad intention before
b - And when the purpose of the Lawgiver is to prevent people from violating His command and prohibition, He attaches to the violation a painful deterrent penalty that constitutes the deterrent sanction ensuring prevention of violation.
48/6 - Variation of the deterrent sanction:
This deterrent, preventive sanction is of two types: disciplinary and civil (i.e., legal). This is because the wisdom of legislation requires diversification of the deterrent sanction according to the types of violations, following the subject of the legal system whose sanctity the violation infringes:
a - If the violating act is of the nature of aggressive crimes that disturb the security of society and the system of the community, then the supporting penalty must be disciplinary, with a repressive punishment that deters from committing the violating act that necessitates it. This type is the disciplinary sanction, or punishment, and it constitutes the penalties in the legislation of the state.
This punishment may be corporal, financial, or restrictive of freedom such as imprisonment and exile, or moral such as defamation and public humiliation. And it may be composed of two or more elements.
It is clear that this type of sanctions does not ensure the prevention of violations and the eradication of crime such that it disappears from society, but it is the utmost possible in legislation because the material actions of people cannot be completely controlled. A person may commit a crime and surprise with it such that it is impossible to prevent it, and he is satisfied with the punishment even if it is death.
b - If the violating act is of the nature of neglecting the conditions imposed by legislation in the civil system for the types of people's transactions and their legal dispositions, such as contracts and others, then the sanction to prevent violation should be depriving the actor of the results he seeks from his action, by considering his action in the view of legislation as void, i.e., stripped of legal effect and result, so that it does not grant its owner the sought right, nor can it be invoked before the judiciary when required.
This type is the civil or legal sanction, and from it arises the theory of nullity and its branches in civil legislation. It is clear that this type of sanction is more decisive in eradicating violation than the disciplinary sanction, because the arrangement of legal results on actions is not a material effect of a tangible act that perhaps cannot be prevented, but rather it is a legal matter contingent upon the consideration of the Lawgiver. So if the Lawgiver strips the violating action of its results, all or some, the doer cannot in any way acquire before the judicial authority the rights that the Lawgiver has negated, as long as the judiciary is the one applying and implementing the Lawgiver's Sharia.
48/7 - Degrees of nullification:
This nullification of the ruling of the violating action is not a single degree.
1 - It may be total nullification, if the violation of the system of dealing occurs in a fundamental aspect. In that case the sanction is called: nullity (butlan).
2 - It may be partial nullification from some aspects, if the violation is in a subsidiary aspect. In that case the sanction is called: corruption (fasad).
3 - If the action affects a protected right of a third party, such as one who sells another's property without his permission, then the contract is considered valid in itself if it fulfills its conditions, but it is suspended in terms of ruling and result until the owner of the affected right consents or rejects: if he consents to the contract, it is effective; if he rejects, it is void. This is called: suspension (tawaqquf); it is a protection of the right of a third party.
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa presents the theory of legal sanctions (mu'ayyidaat) in Islamic jurisprudence. The author begins by distinguishing between encouraging sanctions (mu'ayyid targhibi) and deterrent sanctions (mu'ayyid tarhibi). The focus here is on the deterrent type. The deterrent sanction is divided into two categories: disciplinary (ta'dibi) and civil (madani/huquqi). The disciplinary sanction corresponds to criminal penalties (uqubat) designed to repress aggression against public order. It may be physical, financial, freedom-restricting, or moral. However, the author notes that such penalties cannot completely eliminate crime because human actions are not fully controllable. The civil sanction, on the other hand, involves nullification of the legal effects of a transaction that violates the conditions set by the lawgiver. This is considered more effective because it directly prevents the actor from achieving his desired legal result. The passage then introduces three degrees of nullification: total nullity (butlan) for fundamental violations, partial nullity (fasad) for subsidiary violations, and suspension (tawaqquf) when a third party's right is involved, giving them the option to ratify or reject the transaction. This classification is central to the Hanafi theory of void and voidable transactions.

Key Terms
المؤيد الترهيبي (al-mu'ayyid al-tarhibi): الجزاء الزاجر المؤلم الذي يشرعه الشارع لمنع المخالفة
المؤيد المدني (al-mu'ayyid al-madani): الجزاء الذي يتمثل في حرمان التصرف المخالف من نتائجه القانونية
البطلان (al-butlan): إلغاء كلي للتصرف المخالف في ناحية أساسية
الفساد (al-fasad): إلغاء جزئي للتصرف المخالف في ناحية فرعية
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): تعليق حكم التصرف على إجازة من له الحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the civil sanction (المؤيد المدني) considered more effective than the disciplinary sanction (المؤيد التأديبي)?
A) It physically prevents the violator from committing the act.
B) It relies on the Lawgiver's recognition, which fully controls legal outcomes.
C) It is easier to implement in courts.
D) It is more painful than disciplinary punishment.

Key Takeaway
Islamic law employs both disciplinary and civil sanctions; the civil sanction (nullification of legal effects) is more decisive because it depends on the Lawgiver's determination, not on controlling physical actions.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
12 min
Degrees of Nullification: Butlan, Fasad, and Tawaqquf
Defines three degrees of nullification of invalid transactions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between butlan (total nullity), fasad (partial nullity), and tawaqquf (suspension) in Hanafi legal theory and apply each to a given transaction scenario.

Core Idea
Butlan voids the entire contract, fasad allows correction, and tawaqquf suspends it pending third-party consent.

Having introduced the civil sanction as a nullification of legal effects, the passage now details three degrees of nullification: total (butlan), partial (fasad), and suspension (tawaqquf). These categories are crucial for a mufti to determine the exact legal status of a transaction that violates Islamic law. The key is identifying whether the violation touches a fundamental aspect, a subsidiary aspect, or a third party's right.

Guided Path
1
List the three degrees of nullification
Read the passage 'درجات الإلغاء' and note the three terms: بطلان, فساد, توقف. Understand each as a response to a different type of violation.
The text introduces them as: ١ إلغاء كلي = بطلان, ٢ إلغاء جزئي = فساد, ٣ توقف.
2
Define each degree
For each, state what it nullifies and why. Butlan is total nullity for a fundamental violation; Fasad is partial nullity for a subsidiary violation; Tawaqquf is suspension when a third party's right is affected.
Example: Butlan = violation in a fundamental aspect (ناحية أساسية); Fasad = violation in a subsidiary aspect (ناحية فرعية); Tawaqquf = violation involving a third party's right.
3
Identify the triggering condition for each
The text specifies the condition that leads to each degree. Note that Tawaqquf requires that the contract itself is valid in form (استوفى شرائطه) but its effect depends on the third party's consent.
Tawaqquf example: بيع مال الغير – the contract is valid in itself but suspended.
4
Apply to a scenario
Given a case (e.g., selling an item with an unknown price), classify the degree and explain why.
Unknown price is a fundamental term, so the sale is batil (total nullity).

٤٨ / ٦ - تنوع المؤيد الترهيبي :
وهذا المؤيد الترهيبي الزاجر :نوعان تأديبي، ومدني (أي حقوقي). ذلك لأن حكمة التشريع تقتضي تنويع المؤيد الترهيبي بحسب أنواع
المخالفات تبعاً لموضوع النظام التشريعي الذي تنتهك المخالفة حرمته :
أ - فإذا كان الفعل المخالف من قبيل الجرائم العدوانية التي تخل بأمن المجتمع ونظام الجماعة، فالجزاء المؤيد يجب أن يكون تأديبياً بعقوبة قامعة ترهب من الإقدام على الفعل المخالف الموجب لها. وهذا النوع هو المؤيد التأديبي، أو العقوبة، ويتألف منه العقوبات في تشريع الدولة.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
12 min
Theory of Legal Sanctions: Degrees and Classification
Classification of legal sanctions into degrees and types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify Islamic legal sanctions into encouraging and deterrent types, distinguish civil from disciplinary sanctions, and subdivide civil sanctions into four degrees (batal, fasid, tawaqquf, takhyir) and disciplinary into prescribed and discretionary punishments.

Core Idea
Islamic law deters by denying legal results in civil matters and by punishment in criminal matters, each with graduated severity.

This lesson from al-Zarqa's foundational text lays out the complete structure of legal sanctions in Islamic jurisprudence. You will learn how the law ensures compliance by either encouraging or deterring, and how deterrent sanctions are further categorized into those affecting the validity of transactions (civil sanctions) and those imposing punishments (disciplinary sanctions). The four degrees of civil nullity—total nullity, partial invalidity, suspension, and option—are essential for understanding contract law.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the purpose of sanctions
The primary purpose is to deter violation by depriving the actor of the legal results he seeks. This is more effective than punishment alone.
2
Distinguish encouraging and deterrent sanctions
Encouraging sanctions (targhibiyya) motivate compliance; deterrent sanctions (tarhibiyya) discourage violation. The passage focuses on deterrents.
3
Separate civil from disciplinary deterrent sanctions
Civil sanctions affect the validity of transactions (e.g., nullity), while disciplinary sanctions are punishments (e.g., hudud, ta'zir).
4
Learn the four degrees of civil nullity
Total nullity (batal) for essential violations, partial invalidity (fasid) for subsidiary violations, suspension (tawaqquf) for third-party rights, and option (takhyir) for contractual imbalance.
5
Differentiate prescribed and discretionary punishments
Prescribed punishments (hudud, qisas) are fixed by text; discretionary punishments (ta'zir) are left to the ruler's judgment based on circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
التشريع في النظام المدني لأنواع معاملات الناس وتصرفاتهم الحقوقية من عقود وغيرها، فإنَّ المؤيد لمنع المخالفة ينبغي أن يكون حرمان المتصرف من النتائج التي يبتغيها من تصرفه وذلك بأن يعتبر عمله في نظر التشريع لغواً، أي مسلوب الحكم والنتيجة، فلا يكسب صاحبه الحق المطلوب، ولا يحتج به أمام القضاء عند الاقتضاء.
وهذا النوع هو المؤيد المدني، أو الحقوقي، وينشأ عنه نظرية البطلان
وتفاريعها في التشريع المدني. ومن الواضح أن هذا النوع من المؤيدات هو أقطع لدابر المخالفة من المؤيد التأديبي، لأن ترتيب النتائج الحقوقية على التصرفات ليس هو أثراً مادياً لفعل محسوس ربما لا تمكن الحيلولة دونه، بل هو أمر اعتباري منوط باعتبار الشارع. فإذا سلب الشارع عن التصرف المخالف لنظامه نتائجه كلها أو بعضها لم يستطع الفاعل بوجه من الوجوه أن يكتسب أمام السلطة القضائية ما نفاه الشارع من الحقوق مادام القضاء هو
الشرعي
المطبق والمنفذ الشريعة الشارع.
٧/٤٨ - درجات الإلغاء :
وهذا الإلغاء لحكم التصرف المخالف ليس درجة واحدة.
١ - فقد يكون إلغاء كلياً، وذلك إذا كانت المخالفة لنظام التعامل واقعة في ناحية أساسية. وعندئذ يسمى المؤيد : بطلاناً.
٢ - وقد يكون إلغاء جزئياً من بعض الوجوه، وذلك إذا كانت المخالفة في ناحية فرعية وعندئذ يسمى المؤيد: فساداً.
٣ - وإذا كان التصرف يمس حقاً محترماً للغير، كمن باع مال غيره بلا إذنه، فإنَّ العقد يكون معتبراً في ذاته إذا استوفى شرائطه، لكنه متوقف من حيث الحكم والثمرة حتى يرضى صاحب الحق الممسوس أو يرفض : فإن رضي بالعقد نفذ، وإن رفض بطل. وهذا يسمى: توقفاً؛ وهو حماية لحق الغير.
٤ - وإذا كان في التصرُّف المدني إخلال بالتوازن العقدي الواجب بين حقوق المتعاقدين المتقابلة بحيث يكون مظنة لطغيان مصلحة أحدهما على مصلحة الآخر دون تحقق رضاه، فإنَّ المؤيد الضامن لإعادة التوازن أو سلامة الرضا يكون بمنح الطرف الذي تعرض حقه للضرر خياراً في فسخ العقد إن شاء. وهذا المؤيد يسمى تخييراً وهو من مباحث شوائب الإرادة العقدية أو عيوب الرضا. وقد تقدم تفصيله في نظرية العقود (ر: ف ٦/٣٧). فعلى هذه الأسس والاعتبارات تتنزل في الفقه الإسلامي نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية العامة التي هي من ضرورات كل تشريع ولوازم فكرته . الخلاصة
٨/٤٨ - يتلخص من ذلك أن المؤيدات في الشريعة الإسلامية، كما في كل تشريع كامل تنقسم إلى قسمين : المؤيدات الترغيبية، والمؤيدات الترهيبية . والقسم الثاني الترهيبي يتفرع أيضاً إلى فرعين :
الأول : المؤيدات التأديبية؛ وهي العقوبات المشروعة للزجر عن
ارتكاب الجرائم والمخالفات وتنقسم إلى نوعين أساسيين: أ - عقوبات مقدرة، وهي التي حددها وقدرها الشارع نوعاً ومقداراً بالنص الصريح ولم يترك لولاة الأمر رأياً في التصرف بأنواعها ومقاديرها، لما للجرائم التي توجبها من خطر اجتماعي في نظر الشارع. وتسمى أيضاً: عقوبات نصية، نسبة إلى نص الشارع.
ب - عقوبات مفوضة: وهي التي لم يحدد التشريع الإسلامي في شيء منها نوعاً ولا مقداراً معيناً، بل فوّضها لولاة الأمور. فيعاقبون المجرمين في كل جريمة بما يرونه متكافئاً معها وكافياً للزجر والإصلاح.
وهذا يختلف في نوع العقوبة وقدرها بحسب الأزمنة والأمكنة والأشخاص ودرجة التهذيب الاجتماعي ولذا فوّضه الشرع إلى ولاة الأمور، أي إلى السلطة الحاكمة
Chapter Seven: The Theory of Legal Sanctions in Jurisprudence
Legislation in the civil system for types of people's transactions and legal dispositions, such as contracts and others. Indeed, the sanction to prevent violation should be depriving the actor of the results he seeks from his action, by considering his action in the view of legislation as void, i.e., stripped of legal effect and result, so he does not acquire the desired right, nor can he rely on it before the judiciary when necessary.
This type is the civil or legal sanction, from which arises the theory of nullity and its branches in civil legislation. It is clear that this type of sanction is more decisive in cutting off violation than the disciplinary sanction, because arranging legal results on actions is not a material effect of a tangible act that perhaps cannot be prevented, but rather it is a juristic matter dependent on the consideration of the Lawgiver. So if the Lawgiver strips the violating action of all or some of its results, the doer cannot in any way acquire before the judicial authority the rights that the Lawgiver has negated, as long as the judiciary is the one applying and implementing the Lawgiver's Sharia.
7/48 - Degrees of Annulment:
This annulment of the ruling of a violating action is not one degree.
1 - It may be total annulment, and that is when the violation of the system of dealing occurs in an essential aspect. In that case, the sanction is called: batil (null).
2 - It may be partial annulment in some respects, and that is when the violation is in a subsidiary aspect. In that case, the sanction is called: fasid (invalid).
3 - If the action infringes upon a protected right of a third party, such as one who sells another's property without permission, the contract is valid in itself if it meets its conditions, but it is suspended in terms of legal effect and result until the owner of the infringed right consents or rejects: if he consents to the contract it becomes effective, and if he rejects it becomes void. This is called: tawaqquf (suspension); it is a protection of the third party's right.
4 - If there is in a civil action a breach of the obligatory contractual balance between the reciprocal rights of the contracting parties such that it is likely to cause the interest of one party to prevail over the interest of the other without his true consent, then the sanction ensuring the restoration of balance or sound consent is granting the party whose right was harmed an option to rescind the contract if he wishes. This sanction is called: takhyir (option), and it is among the topics of defects of contractual will or defects of consent. Its details have preceded in the theory of contracts (ref: f 6/37). Upon these foundations and considerations is established in Islamic jurisprudence the general theory of legal sanctions, which is a necessity of every legislation and a requirement of its idea. Summary
8/48 - It is summarized from the above that sanctions in Islamic Sharia, as in every complete legislation, are divided into two types: encouraging sanctions (encouragements) and deterrent sanctions (deterrents). The second type, the deterrent, is further divided into two branches:
First: Disciplinary sanctions: they are the prescribed penalties for deterring from committing crimes and violations, and they are divided into two basic types:
a - Prescribed punishments (hudud and qisas): they are those that the Lawgiver has determined and specified in type and amount by explicit text and did not leave to the rulers discretion regarding their types and amounts, due to the social danger of the crimes that necessitate them in the view of the Lawgiver. They are also called: textual punishments, relative to the text of the Lawgiver.
b - Discretionary punishments (ta'zir): they are those for which Islamic legislation has not specified a particular type or amount, but rather delegated them to the rulers. They punish criminals for each crime with what they deem commensurate with it and sufficient for deterrence and reform.
This varies in the type and amount of punishment according to times, places, persons, and the degree of social refinement. Therefore, the Sharia delegated it to the rulers, i.e., to the governing authority.
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' discusses the theory of legal sanctions in Islamic jurisprudence. The author begins by explaining the purpose of a legal sanction: to deter violation by depriving the actor of the legal results he seeks. He distinguishes between civil/legal sanctions (which affect the validity of transactions) and disciplinary sanctions (punishments). Civil sanctions are more effective because they directly negate the desired legal outcome.

Then he details the four degrees of annulment for a violating transaction: (1) total nullity (batal) – when the violation is essential; (2) partial invalidity (fasid) – when the violation is subsidiary; (3) suspension (tawaqquf) – when the transaction infringes on a third party's right, pending their consent; (4) option (takhyir) – when there is an imbalance in contractual rights, giving the harmed party an option to rescind.

Finally, he summarizes the classification of all sanctions into encouraging (targhibiyya) and deterrent (tarhibiyya). Deterrent sanctions are further divided into disciplinary sanctions (punishments), which themselves are either prescribed (hudud and qisas, fixed by text) or discretionary (ta'zir, delegated to rulers based on circumstances).

This analysis is foundational for understanding how Islamic law structures its legal system to maintain order and justice, balancing the rights of individuals and the community.

Key Terms
المؤيدات الشرعية (al-mu'ayyidāt al-shar‘iyyah): Legal sanctions in Islamic law that ensure compliance by either encouraging or deterring behavior.
بطلان (buṭlān): Total nullity of a transaction due to a fundamental defect.
فساد (fasād): Partial invalidity due to a subsidiary defect; the contract is defective but may be validated.
توقف (tawaqquf): Suspension of a contract's effect pending the consent of a third party whose right is affected.
تخيير (takhyīr): An option granted to a party to rescind a contract due to imbalance or defect in consent.
عقوبات مقدرة (‘uqūbāt muqaddarah): Fixed punishments prescribed by divine text, such as hudud and qisas.
عقوبات مفوضة (‘uqūbāt mufawwaḍah): Discretionary punishments left to the ruler's judgment (ta'zir).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the sanction called when a transaction infringes on a third party’s right, making its effect dependent on that party’s consent?
A) بطلان (Nullity)
B) فساد (Invalidity)
C) توقف (Suspension)
D) تخيير (Option)

Key Takeaway
Islamic legal sanctions are graduated: civil sanctions deprive legal effects in four degrees, while disciplinary sanctions are either fixed by text or left to discretion.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Classification of Legal Sanctions (Mu'ayyidat) in Islamic Law
Explains the taxonomy of legal sanctions in Islamic law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the overall classification of legal sanctions into encouraging and deterring, and identify the subcategories of deterring sanctions: disciplinary (with fixed and discretionary) and civil (butlan, fasad, tawaqquf, takhyeer).

Core Idea
Islamic law balances deterrence through fixed/discretionary penalties with civil remedies that adjust transactional validity.

This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm sets out the foundational framework for understanding how Islamic law enforces its rules. Unlike a mere list of punishments, the theory of sanctions (mu'ayyidat) reveals the sophisticated interplay between criminal and civil law. You will learn the two broad categories—encouraging and deterring—and then dive into the deterring branch, which splits into penal sanctions and civil measures. These civil measures—butlan, fasad, tawaqquf, and takhyeer—are particularly crucial for Hanafi contract law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching division
The passage first divides sanctions into encouraging (targhibiyya) and deterring (tarhibiyya). Encouraging sanctions are rewards or promises that motivate compliance; deterring sanctions are punishments or loss of rights that prevent violations.
The passage does not elaborate on encouraging sanctions; it focuses on deterring.
2
Recognize the two branches of deterring sanctions
Deterring sanctions are further divided into disciplinary (ta'dibiyya) and civil (madaniyya). Disciplinary sanctions are penal punishments for crimes; civil sanctions affect the validity of transactions.
This is a key structural distinction in Islamic legal theory.
3
Distinguish between fixed and discretionary disciplinary sanctions
Within disciplinary sanctions, there are fixed punishments (uqubat muqaddara) explicitly determined by the Lawgiver (e.g., hudud, qisas) and discretionary punishments (uqubat mufawwada) delegated to rulers (ta'zir) based on circumstances.
Fixed punishments are nass-based; discretionary allow flexibility.
4
Identify the four civil sanctions
Civil sanctions consist of four measures: butlan (nullity), fasad (voidability/irregularity), tawaqquf (suspension pending ratification), and takhyeer (option to rescind). These affect the legal consequences of transactions.
These are central to Hanafi mu'amalat, particularly contract law.

Source Text

الفصل (٤٨) منشأ المؤيدات وتعريفها وتقسيمها

مخالفة نظام المعاملات من سلخ بعض النتائج أو كلها، بتجريد التصرف عن اعتباره الشرعي تجريداً كلياً أو جزئياً، فيفقد بذلك كل نتائجه المدنية أو بعضها.

Chapter (48): Origin of Sanctions, Their Definition and Division
4 - If in a civil transaction there is a breach of the contractual balance required between the reciprocal rights of the contracting parties such that it is likely to cause the interest of one to overpower the interest of the other without the actual consent of the latter, then the sanction guaranteeing the restoration of balance or the integrity of consent is by granting the party whose right is exposed to harm an option to rescind the contract if he wishes. This sanction is called 'option' (takhyeer) and it is among the topics of defects of contractual will or defects of consent. Its detail has preceded in the theory of contracts (ref. sec. 37/6). Upon these foundations and considerations is based in Islamic jurisprudence the theory of general legal sanctions, which are necessities of every legislation and implications of its idea.
Summary
48/8 - It is summarized from that that sanctions in Islamic law, as in every complete legislation, are divided into two types: encouraging sanctions (targhibiyyah) and deterring sanctions (tarhibiyyah). The second, deterring, further branches into two branches:
First: Disciplinary sanctions; they are prescribed punishments for deterring from committing crimes and violations, and are divided into two basic types: A - Fixed punishments, which are those whose type and quantity have been determined and ordained by the Lawgiver by explicit text, leaving no opinion to the rulers in their types and quantities, due to the social danger of the crimes that necessitate them in the view of the Lawgiver. They are also called: textual punishments, in relation to the text of the Lawgiver.
B - Delegated punishments: those whose type or specific quantity have not been determined by Islamic legislation, but rather delegated to the rulers. They punish criminals for each crime with what they deem proportionate and sufficient for deterrence and reform.
This varies in the type and extent of punishment according to times, places, persons, and the degree of social refinement; therefore the Lawgiver delegated it to the rulers, i.e., to the governing authority.

Second: Civil or rights-based sanctions: they are what the Lawgiver imposes upon violation of the system of transactions by stripping some or all of the results, by divesting the transaction of its legal consideration either entirely or partially, so that it loses all or some of its civil consequences.
These civil sanctions comprise four measures: nullity (butlan), corruption (fasad), suspension (tawaqquf), and option (takhyeer).

49/48 - We will discuss in the following the rulings of deterring sanctions in both their disciplinary and civil branches. However, we will limit the disciplinary section to outlining its broad lines and general rulings with utmost brevity, because the subject of the book is the civil section of Islamic jurisprudence, and we only discuss disciplinary sanctions incidentally to enlighten minds in some aspects and to complete the branches of research in the general theory of sanctions.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm presents the foundational classification of legal sanctions in Islamic law. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by explaining that sanctions (mu'ayyidat) are necessary for every legal system. He then divides them into two main categories: encouraging (targhibiyya) and deterring (tarhibiyya). Encouraging sanctions are not further discussed here; the focus is on deterring sanctions.

Deterring sanctions are subdivided into two: disciplinary (ta'dibiyya) and civil (madaniyya). Disciplinary sanctions are penal in nature and aim to deter crime. They are further divided into fixed punishments (uqubat muqaddara), which are explicitly defined by the Lawgiver (such as hudud and qisas), and discretionary punishments (uqubat mufawwada), known as ta'zir, which are left to the ruler's discretion based on circumstances. This reflects the Hanafi principle that certain punishments are fixed by nass, while others are flexible for societal benefit.

Civil sanctions (al-mu'ayyidat al-madaniyya) are consequences for violating transactional rules, resulting in partial or total loss of legal effect. They include butlan (voidness), fasad (voidability/irregularity), tawaqquf (suspension), and takhyeer (option to rescind). These are core concepts in Hanafi mu'amalat, particularly in contracts. The author notes that the book's focus is civil law, so disciplinary sanctions will only be briefly outlined.

Key Terms
المؤيدات (al-mu'ayyidat): Legal sanctions or measures that support the implementation of legal rules, either by encouraging compliance or deterring violations.
المؤيدات الترغيبية (al-mu'ayyidat al-targhibiyya): Sanctions that encourage desired behavior, typically through rewards or promises (e.g., divine rewards); not elaborated here.
المؤيدات الترهيبية (al-mu'ayyidat al-tarhibiyya): Sanctions that deter through punishment or loss of rights; include both penal and civil consequences.
المؤيدات التأديبية (al-mu'ayyidat al-ta'dibiyya): Penal sanctions or punishments for criminal offenses, subdivided into fixed (muqaddara) and discretionary (ta'zir).
المؤيدات المدنية (al-mu'ayyidat al-madaniyya): Civil sanctions that affect the validity of transactions, including butlan, fasad, tawaqquf, and takhyeer.
عقوبات مقدرة (uqubat muqaddara): Fixed punishments determined by explicit textual stipulation (hudud, qisas) with no discretion for rulers.
عقوبات مفوضة (uqubat mufawwada): Discretionary punishments (ta'zir) delegated to the ruler to determine based on circumstances.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses that are not subject to fixed penalties; left to judge's discretion.
البطلان (al-butlan): Complete nullity of a transaction, producing no legal effects.
الفساد (al-fasad): Irregularity or voidability; a transaction that is defective but may be rectified or has partial effects.
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): Suspension of a transaction's effect pending ratification or rejection by a party with the right to annul.
التخيير (al-takhyeer): Granting a party an option to rescind a contract due to a defect or defect, such as in khiyar al-ayb or khiyar al-ru'ya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Two: fixed and discretionary
B) Two: disciplinary and civil
C) Three: nullity, corruption, option
D) Two: encouraging and deterring

Key Takeaway
Islamic law's theory of sanctions is comprehensive, including both penal (fixed and discretionary) and civil (nullity, voidability, suspension, option) measures to enforce legal rules.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Classification of Sanctions and the Five Hudud
Introduces classification of sanctions and enumerates the five hudud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between deterrent (ta'zir) and civil sanctions and identify the five hadd punishments along with their legal basis.

Core Idea
Islamic law combines fixed deterrent punishments (hudud) with flexible discretionary penalties (ta'zir) and civil sanctions affecting transactions.

Islamic jurisprudence classifies legal sanctions into two broad categories: deterrent punishments aimed at preventing crime, and civil sanctions that affect the validity of transactions. Among deterrent punishments, some are fixed by divine texts (hudud), while others are discretionary (ta'zir). This lesson outlines this classification and details the five fixed punishments known as hudud, their sources, and their basic features.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two main categories of sanctions
Identify the difference between deterrent (ta'dibi) punishments, which punish the offender, and civil (madani) sanctions, which affect the legal consequences of an act.
Ta'zir is a discretionary penalty, while hudud are fixed by text.
2
Define ta'zir and understand its scope
Ta'zir is any punishment not fixed by a textual source, left to the judge's discretion. It can be less than a hadd and includes various forms like flogging, imprisonment, or fines.
Ta'zir is the residual category for offenses not covered by hudud or qisas.
3
List the five hudud punishments
Memorize the five hudud: zina (adultery), qadhf (false accusation), theft (sariqa), hirabah (banditry), and drinking alcohol (shurb al-khamr).
The first four are from the Qur'an; the fifth is from the Sunnah.
4
Note the textual sources for each hadd
Recognize that the punishments for adultery, false accusation, theft, and banditry are directly from the Qur'an, while the punishment for drinking alcohol is established by the Sunnah (hadith).
This difference impacts how the punishment is justified and proven.
5
Understand the range of corporal punishments
The hudud range from flogging (80 lashes for qadhf and drinking) to the death penalty. They are physical punishments intended to deter.
The method of flogging is specified: with a medium whip, avoiding vital spots.

Chapter Seven: The Theory of Legal Sanctions in Jurisprudence. And these discretionary punishments are called in the language of Islamic jurisprudence: Ta'zir. We will briefly discuss each of these two types in chapters 49 and 50. The second type: civil or rights sanctions, which are what the Lawgiver attaches to violation of the system of transactions, stripping some or all of the results, by divesting the act of its legal consideration completely or partially, so it loses all or some of its civil consequences. These civil sanctions include four measures: nullity (butlan), corruption (fasad), suspension (tawaqquf), and option (takhyeer). This is what we will discuss in the following pages regarding the rulings of deterrent sanctions in both their disciplinary and civil branches. But we will limit ourselves in the disciplinary section to outlining its broad lines and general rulings with utmost brevity, because the subject of the book is the civil section of Islamic jurisprudence. We only discuss the disciplinary sanctions incidentally to enlighten minds in some aspects and complete the branches of research in the general theory of sanctions. Chapter Forty-Nine: Disciplinary Sanctions: Fixed Punishments. 1/49: The fixed textual punishments in Islamic law are two types: hudud and qisas. First topic: Hudud. 2/4: The hudud are five punishments: the punishment for adultery (zina), the punishment for false accusation (qadhf), the punishment for theft, the punishment for banditry (hirabah), and the punishment for drinking alcohol. The first four hudud are mentioned in the Qur'an, while the punishment for drinking alcohol is not mentioned in the Qur'an but is established by the Sunnah. All these hudud are physical punishments; the lowest is flogging with a whip, and the highest is death. a) The punishment for false accusation and for drinking alcohol is eighty lashes distributed over the body parts except the vital spots, with a medium whip.
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage introduces the general theory of legal sanctions in Islamic jurisprudence, classifying them into two broad categories. The first category is deterrent punishments (ta'zir), which are discretionary penalties left to the judge's discretion. The second category is civil sanctions, which affect the legal validity of transactions, leading to nullity (butlan), corruption (fasad), suspension (tawaqquf), or giving an option (takhyeer). The author notes that while the book focuses on civil sanctions, he will briefly outline the disciplinary sanctions for completeness. The passage then transitions to fixed punishments (hudud), which are specific penalties determined by textual sources. The five hudud are: adultery, false accusation, theft, banditry, and drinking alcohol. The first four are Qur'anic; the fifth is from the Sunnah. The lowest hadd punishment is flogging, and the highest is death. Specifically, the punishments for false accusation and drinking alcohol are eighty lashes, administered with a medium whip over the body except vital areas. The author also provides linguistic and terminological notes: 'hadd' means prevention, and these punishments are called hudud because they prevent people from committing the corresponding crimes. 'Qadhf' literally means throwing, but legally it means accusing someone of adultery. 'Hirabah' means highway robbery.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment not fixed by text, left to the judge's determination.
حد (hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by the Qur'an or Sunnah for specific crimes, intended to deter.
قذف (qadhf): Accusing a chaste person of adultery or illegitimate birth.
حرابة (hirabah): Highway robbery or armed banditry, including murder and theft by force.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many hadd punishments are there according to this passage?
A) Four
B) Five
C) Six
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
The five hadd punishments are fixed penalties for specific crimes, serving as deterrents, and include both Qur'anic and Sunnah-based punishments.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Principle of Dar' al-Hudud bi al-Shubuhat: Averting Punishments by Doubt
Explaining the principle and its Hanafi application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle of dar' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat and apply it to cases of alleged doubt.

Core Idea
Hudud are averted by plausible doubts; the Hanafi school expands this to mere claim in a plausible context.

This lesson explores the core Hanafi principle that hudud punishments are warded off by doubts. Rooted in a Prophetic hadith, this maxim shapes criminal procedure by favoring the accused when uncertainty exists. The Hanafi school distinctively allows even an unproven allegation of doubt to drop the hadd, reflecting a deep commitment to avoiding punishment in ambiguous cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its textual basis
Locate the prophetic command 'Avert the hudud by doubts' and understand its status as a dalil naqli. Note that despite some weakness in its chain, the meaning is supported by multiple companions.
The hadith is the foundational text for this qawida.
2
Understand the Hanafi expansion of shubha
Recognize that Hanafi jurists extended the scope of shubha to include mere claim of doubt in a plausible context (mazinnah), without requiring proof of the claim.
This is a distinctive Hanafi position, broader than some other schools.
3
Apply the qawida to a furu' case
Consider the example of a thief who claims the property was due to him. Under Hanafi fiqh, if this claim is plausible in context, the hadd of amputation is averted, even if the claim is unproven.
The claim must be plausible, not arbitrary.
4
Recognize the exception and complementary maxim
Once the hadd is established (e.g., by confession or witnesses), doubt at that stage does not drop it. This is governed by the maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' (اليقين لا يزول بالشك).
The timing of doubt is crucial.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Confirmers in Fiqh. And its reference is to the saying of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Avert the hudud by doubts' and he also said: 'Avert the hudud from the Muslims as much as you can. If there is an outlet, then free his way. For the Imam to err in forgiveness is better than to err in punishment.' (1). And the meaning of this is that the side of the accused is taken into consideration in case of doubt. This is what modern criminal jurisprudence and its laws have affirmed. And our jurists expanded the scope of the doubt that drops hudud based on the indication of the transmitted reports from the Prophet, peace be upon him, in that regard; until they said: Mere claim of a doubt in its plausible context by the perpetrator of a crime necessitating a hadd drops the hadd without needing to prove that alleged doubt (see al-Durr al-Mukhtar 1503). 49/5 - Conditions of Proof in these Crimes: It is also required in proving these crimes necessitating hudud before the judiciary special conditions that differ in some aspects among themselves and exceed what is required in proving other rights, and they are detailed in the chapters on testimonies in fiqh books. The crime of zina with its characteristics and conditions necessitating the hadd is very difficult to prove, especially since the accuser, if he cannot prove it, is considered a slanderer, and is punished with the hadd of qadhf, unless he is a husband and insists on his accusation against his wife and is unable to prove it; then he is not punished with the hadd of qadhf, but rather li'an (mutual imprecation) occurs between them before the judiciary at the request of the woman, and the result of li'an is the judge's separation between the two spouses who perform li'an. And li'an is specific multiple oaths that the man swears before the judge on the truth of his accusation of his wife of zina, and the woman swears similarly on his lying. (1) Reported by al-Tirmidhi in Hudud (1424) and he declared its marfu' version weak, and by al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak 4/384 who said: Its chain is sahih, but al-Dhahabi criticized it. Also reported by al-Daraqutni in his Sunan 3/84, and by al-Bayhaqi in his Sunan 8/238, and its chain is weak due to the weakness of Yazid ibn Ziyad al-Shami, but its meaning is authentically reported from a group of Companions. (See al-Maqasid al-Hasana by al-Sakhawi p. 30). Chapter (49) Disciplinary Confirmers: Fixed Punishments. 6/49 - And when these crimes are established by their shari'ah-accepted means and their hudud become obligatory, no pardon is allowed from the victim nor leniency from the ruler, because they are among the rights of the shari'ah called the rights of Allah, since their enforcement is related to safeguarding a fundamental aspect of the Islamic social system, namely the preservation of the five necessities: religion, life, lineage, intellect, and wealth. However, the hadd of qadhf (slander) among these hudud differs from the rest in that Islamic ijtihads are almost unanimous that it is not a pure right of the shari'ah, but rather it contains a personal right and a shari'ah right. Therefore, its enforcement requires a personal claim from the person slandered if he is alive, or from one of his ascendants or descendants if the slandered person is dead, and the hadd is waived by their pardon. (1) Dr. Subhi Mahmassani, lawyer and professor, says in his study on punishments in Islamic shari'ah: It must be reminded again that punishments cannot be weighed with a single fixed scale; rather, they are linked to the time and place in which they were set and applied. Even if some of them are not appealing to some people today, that does not mean they are not suitable even for the present day. The hand amputation of the thief, like other hudud, is only applied after deliberation, scrutiny, and the judgment of the ruler, and it falls due to doubts. There is no doubt that it was necessary in Arab society, and that it is still beneficial and useful in many countries today. This is supported by news we receive from the Hijaz region that theft has become rare there after the Saudi government enforced Islamic punishment. (See book 'Wake of the Arabs' by George Antonius, translated by Mr. al-Rakkabi from Damascus, p. 379 ff.) The expert in criminal matters asserts that such punishment is necessary for professional thieves in our present day when it is proven that modern punishments do not achieve their purpose. What about a notorious professional thief who receives successive sentences and boasts of his thieving tricks, and then when he comes out of prison, he does not hesitate to return? Would human society lose this sinful hand, prevent its owner from repetition and continuance, and deter others? And would society be considered cruel if it protects itself in this way out of necessity and despair in disciplining the likes of those mentioned?
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy discussion on two interrelated concepts in Hanafi fiqh: (1) the principle of 'warding off hudud by doubts' (درء الحدود بالشبهات) and its expansive application by Hanafi jurists, and (2) the classification of hadd punishments as rights of Allah (حقوق الله) with the exception of qadhf. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, uses a modern introductory style but faithfully represents the classical Hanafi position. The passage begins by citing the Prophetic hadith that doubt should prevent the application of hudud. The Hanafi school is noted for expanding the scope of shubha (doubt) to the extent that mere plausible claim of a doubt by the perpetrator drops the hadd without requiring proof. This is based on the general direction of the narrations. The discussion then moves to the strict evidentiary requirements for proving hudud crimes, highlighting zina as particularly difficult to prove—its accuser risks facing the hadd of qadhf if unable to prove it. The exception is the husband who can resort to li'an (mutual imprecation) without being punished for false accusation. Next, the author explains that once a hadd is established, no pardon is permissible because it is a right of Allah aimed at preserving the five necessities (maqasid). However, the hadd of qadhf is distinguished: it contains a personal right component, so it requires a claim from the victim or his heirs, and it can be waived by their pardon. Thus, the passage demonstrates the Hanafi legal philosophy of balancing divine rights with human considerations through the mechanism of shubha and the nuanced treatment of qadhf.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A plausible doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of a hadd punishment.
حد (hadd): A fixed, unchangeable punishment prescribed by Allah for specific crimes.
مظنة (mazinnah): A plausible context in which a doubt can reasonably arise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The doubt must be proven by two witnesses.
B) The doubt must be plausible and contextually relevant, even if unproven.
C) The doubt must be sworn by an oath.
D) The doubt must be recognized by the judge as certain.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses the maxim 'hudud are averted by doubts' to protect the accused, requiring only a plausible claim of doubt to drop the hadd.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Hudud as Rights of Allah and the Exception of Qadhf
Explaining the nature of hudud as haqq Allah and the unique status of qadhf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between pure divine rights and mixed rights in hadd punishments, and explain why qadhf is an exception.

Core Idea
Hudud are rights of Allah not subject to pardon, except qadhf which contains a personal right and can be waived by the victim.

This lesson examines the classification of hadd punishments as divine rights (haqq Allah) aimed at preserving public order. While most hudud cannot be pardoned by anyone, qadhf (slander) is unique: it includes a personal right of the victim, so it requires a complaint and can be waived. Understanding this distinction is crucial for muftis in advising on hadd cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of hudud as haqq Allah
Understand that hadd punishments are considered rights of Allah because they protect the five necessities (religion, life, lineage, intellect, wealth). As such, they are not subject to pardon by the victim or the ruler.
This is a standard feature of all hudud except qadhf.
2
Recognize the exception of qadhf
Note that the hadd of qadhf is a mixed right: it has both a personal component (the victim's reputation) and a divine component (public order). Therefore, it requires a personal claim from the victim (or his heirs) and falls if they pardon.
This is a near-unanimous view across schools.
3
Understand the practical implication
If the slandered person does not file a complaint, the state cannot initiate the hadd of qadhf. If the victim pardons before execution, the hadd is dropped.
This contrasts with theft or zina, which are pursued regardless of the victim's wish.
4
Apply to a furu' case
Consider: A man slanders another in public. The victim is alive and sues. Later, he pardons the offender. According to the Hanafi school, the hadd is waived because the personal right overrides the divine right in qadhf.
This shows the practical effect of the mixed nature.

Source Text

وقد توسع فقهاؤنا في نطاق الشبهة المسقطة للحدود نظراً لدلالة الآثار المروية عن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في ذلك ؛ حتى قالوا: إن مجرد ادعاء الشبهة في مظننتها من مرتكب الجريمة الموجبة للحد يسقط الحد دون حاجة إلى إثبات تلك الشبهة المدعاة ( ر ) الدر المختار (١٥٠٣ ) .
٤٩ / ٥ - شرائط الإثبات في هذه الجرائم:
ويُشترط أيضاً في إثبات هذه الجرائم الموجبة للحدود أمام القضاء شرائط خاصة تختلف في بعضها عن بعض وتزيد عما يشترط في إثبات غيرها من الحقوق وهي مفصَّلة في مباحث الشهادات من كتب الفقه وجريمة الزنى بصفاته وشرائطه الموجبة للحد عسيرة الإثبات جداً، ولاسيما أن مدعيها إذا لم يتمكن من إثباتها يعتبر قاذفاً، ويحد حد القذف، إلا إذا كان زوجاً وأصرّ على تهمته لزوجته وعَجز عن الإثبات، فلا يحد حد القذف، بل يجري التلاعن بينهما أمام القضاء بطلب المرأة، ونتيجة اللعان تفريق القاضي بين الزوجين المتلاعنين.
واللعان أيمان معينة متعددة يقسمها الرجل أمام القاضي على صدقه اتهامه لزوجته بالزنى وتقسم نظيرها المرأة على كذبه .

الفصل (٤٩) المؤيدات التأديبية : العقوبات المقدرة
٦/٤٩ ـ ومتى ثبتت هذه الجرائم بمثبتاتها المقبولة شرعاً ووجبت حدودها لا يجوز فيها عفو من المجني عليه ولا تسامح من ولي الأمر الحاكم، لأنها من حقوق الشرع التي تسمى حقوق الله، إذ تتعلق بإقامتها صيانة ناحية أساسية من نظام المجتمع الإسلامي، وهي حفظ الضروريات الخمس الدين والنفس والنَّسل، والعقل، والمال.
إلا أن حد القذف من بين هذه الحدود يفترق عن سائرها بأن الاجتهادات الإسلامية تكاد تكون متفقة على أنه ليس حقاً خالصاً للشرع، بل فيه حق شخصي وحق شرعي. ولذلك يشترط في إقامته الادعاء الشخصي من المقذوف لو حَيَّاً، أو من أحد أصوله أو فروعه إن كان القذف، ويسقط الحد بعفوهم المقذوف ميتا حين

والخبير في الأمور الجزائية يجزم بأن مثل هذه العقوبة ضرورية للصوص المحترفين في أيامنا الحاضرة متى ثبت أن العقوبات العصرية لا تؤدي إلى المقصود منها. فما بالك بلص شهیر محترف تتوالى عليه الأحكام وهو يفتخر بحيله اللصوصية، ثم إذا خرج من السجن لا يلبث أن يعود دون تردد؟ فهل يضير المجتمع الإنساني يخسر هذه اليد الأثيمة، ويمنع صاحبها من التكرار والاستمرار، ويزجر به غيره؟ وهل تعد الهيئة الاجتماعية قاسية إذا حَمَت نفسها بهذه الصورة اضطراراً ويأساً تأديب أمثال من ذكر؟

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Confirmers in Fiqh. And its reference is to the saying of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Avert the hudud by doubts' and he also said: 'Avert the hudud from the Muslims as much as you can. If there is an outlet, then free his way. For the Imam to err in forgiveness is better than to err in punishment.' (1). And the meaning of this is that the side of the accused is taken into consideration in case of doubt. This is what modern criminal jurisprudence and its laws have affirmed. And our jurists expanded the scope of the doubt that drops hudud based on the indication of the transmitted reports from the Prophet, peace be upon him, in that regard; until they said: Mere claim of a doubt in its plausible context by the perpetrator of a crime necessitating a hadd drops the hadd without needing to prove that alleged doubt (see al-Durr al-Mukhtar 1503). 49/5 - Conditions of Proof in these Crimes: It is also required in proving these crimes necessitating hudud before the judiciary special conditions that differ in some aspects among themselves and exceed what is required in proving other rights, and they are detailed in the chapters on testimonies in fiqh books. The crime of zina with its characteristics and conditions necessitating the hadd is very difficult to prove, especially since the accuser, if he cannot prove it, is considered a slanderer, and is punished with the hadd of qadhf, unless he is a husband and insists on his accusation against his wife and is unable to prove it; then he is not punished with the hadd of qadhf, but rather li'an (mutual imprecation) occurs between them before the judiciary at the request of the woman, and the result of li'an is the judge's separation between the two spouses who perform li'an. And li'an is specific multiple oaths that the man swears before the judge on the truth of his accusation of his wife of zina, and the woman swears similarly on his lying. (1) Reported by al-Tirmidhi in Hudud (1424) and he declared its marfu' version weak, and by al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak 4/384 who said: Its chain is sahih, but al-Dhahabi criticized it. Also reported by al-Daraqutni in his Sunan 3/84, and by al-Bayhaqi in his Sunan 8/238, and its chain is weak due to the weakness of Yazid ibn Ziyad al-Shami, but its meaning is authentically reported from a group of Companions. (See al-Maqasid al-Hasana by al-Sakhawi p. 30). Chapter (49) Disciplinary Confirmers: Fixed Punishments. 6/49 - And when these crimes are established by their shari'ah-accepted means and their hudud become obligatory, no pardon is allowed from the victim nor leniency from the ruler, because they are among the rights of the shari'ah called the rights of Allah, since their enforcement is related to safeguarding a fundamental aspect of the Islamic social system, namely the preservation of the five necessities: religion, life, lineage, intellect, and wealth. However, the hadd of qadhf (slander) among these hudud differs from the rest in that Islamic ijtihads are almost unanimous that it is not a pure right of the shari'ah, but rather it contains a personal right and a shari'ah right. Therefore, its enforcement requires a personal claim from the person slandered if he is alive, or from one of his ascendants or descendants if the slandered person is dead, and the hadd is waived by their pardon. (1) Dr. Subhi Mahmassani, lawyer and professor, says in his study on punishments in Islamic shari'ah: It must be reminded again that punishments cannot be weighed with a single fixed scale; rather, they are linked to the time and place in which they were set and applied. Even if some of them are not appealing to some people today, that does not mean they are not suitable even for the present day. The hand amputation of the thief, like other hudud, is only applied after deliberation, scrutiny, and the judgment of the ruler, and it falls due to doubts. There is no doubt that it was necessary in Arab society, and that it is still beneficial and useful in many countries today. This is supported by news we receive from the Hijaz region that theft has become rare there after the Saudi government enforced Islamic punishment. (See book 'Wake of the Arabs' by George Antonius, translated by Mr. al-Rakkabi from Damascus, p. 379 ff.) The expert in criminal matters asserts that such punishment is necessary for professional thieves in our present day when it is proven that modern punishments do not achieve their purpose. What about a notorious professional thief who receives successive sentences and boasts of his thieving tricks, and then when he comes out of prison, he does not hesitate to return? Would human society lose this sinful hand, prevent its owner from repetition and continuance, and deter others? And would society be considered cruel if it protects itself in this way out of necessity and despair in disciplining the likes of those mentioned?
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy discussion on two interrelated concepts in Hanafi fiqh: (1) the principle of 'warding off hudud by doubts' (درء الحدود بالشبهات) and its expansive application by Hanafi jurists, and (2) the classification of hadd punishments as rights of Allah (حقوق الله) with the exception of qadhf. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, uses a modern introductory style but faithfully represents the classical Hanafi position. The passage begins by citing the Prophetic hadith that doubt should prevent the application of hudud. The Hanafi school is noted for expanding the scope of shubha (doubt) to the extent that mere plausible claim of a doubt by the perpetrator drops the hadd without requiring proof. This is based on the general direction of the narrations. The discussion then moves to the strict evidentiary requirements for proving hudud crimes, highlighting zina as particularly difficult to prove—its accuser risks facing the hadd of qadhf if unable to prove it. The exception is the husband who can resort to li'an (mutual imprecation) without being punished for false accusation. Next, the author explains that once a hadd is established, no pardon is permissible because it is a right of Allah aimed at preserving the five necessities (maqasid). However, the hadd of qadhf is distinguished: it contains a personal right component, so it requires a claim from the victim or his heirs, and it can be waived by their pardon. Thus, the passage demonstrates the Hanafi legal philosophy of balancing divine rights with human considerations through the mechanism of shubha and the nuanced treatment of qadhf.

Key Terms
حق الله (haqq Allah): A right that belongs primarily to Allah, related to public order, and cannot be waived by individuals.
حد القذف (hadd al-qadhf): The fixed punishment for slander (false accusation of zina), consisting of 80 lashes.
حق شخصي (haqq shakhsi): A personal right belonging to an individual, as opposed to a purely public right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following hudud can be waived by the victim's pardon?
A) Hadd of theft
B) Hadd of zina
C) Hadd of qadhf
D) Hadd of intoxication

Key Takeaway
Qadhf is a hybrid hadd: it is a divine right but also involves a personal claim, so it requires a victim's complaint and can be waived.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner
8 min
Hudud: Fixed Punishments as Rights of Allah
Defining the legal nature of hudud and the exception of qadhf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the legal nature of hudud as huquq Allah and identify the exception of qadhf.

Core Idea
Hudud are rights of Allah protecting the five necessities; qadhf uniquely includes a personal right.

This lesson introduces the concept of hudud as fixed punishments that are considered rights of Allah. Understanding their nature is crucial for applying Islamic criminal law. The author explains that once established, these punishments cannot be pardoned by the victim or the ruler, except for qadhf (false accusation of adultery) where the victim's pardon is effective because it involves a personal right alongside the divine right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Read that hudud, once established by valid proofs, cannot be forgiven by the victim or the ruler because they are rights of Allah (huquq Allah).
2
Recognize the basis
Note that hudud protect the five essential values (daruriyyat khams): religion, life, lineage, intellect, and property.
These are the fundamental interests of the Muslim community.
3
Learn the exception of qadhf
Qadhf (false accusation of adultery) is distinguished because it combines a personal right (of the accused person) and a divine right. Thus, it requires a personal claim from the victim if alive, or from their ascendants/descendants if deceased, and the punishment falls with their pardon.
This is nearly agreed upon among madhhabs.
4
Understand the practical implication
In qadhf, the victim's pardon drops the hadd; in other hudud (theft, zina, etc.), no pardon is effective.

Chapter (49): Disciplinary Reinforcements: Fixed Punishments. 6/49 - And when these crimes are established by their legally acceptable proofs and their fixed punishments become obligatory, no pardon from the victim nor leniency from the ruler is permissible, because they are from the rights of the Shari'a which are called the rights of Allah, since their enforcement is related to safeguarding a fundamental aspect of the Islamic social system, namely the preservation of the five necessities: religion, life, lineage, intellect, and wealth. However, the hadd of qadhf (slander) among these fixed punishments differs from the rest in that Islamic ijtihads are nearly agreed that it is not a pure right of the Shari'a, but rather it contains a personal right and a Shari'a right. Therefore, for its enforcement, a personal claim is required from the victim if alive, or from one of his ascendants or descendants if the victim is deceased, and the hadd drops with their pardon. (Footnote: Dr. Subhi Mahmasani's comment on punishments in Islamic law...). Note: It must be noted that the dropping of the hadd means dropping its specific punishment, not the dropping of the public right in the original punishment. Thus, the dropping of hadd does not prevent punishing the perpetrator with another punishment lighter than the hadd by way of ta'zir (discretionary punishment) if the dropping of the hadd is due to a doubt while the perpetrator still deserves discipline. So according to Islamic jurists, hadd punishments contain two rights: the general right of the community in the original punishment for discipline and deterrence, and the right of Allah (the right of Shari'a) in the specific fixed punishment. Dropping the specific punishment does not necessitate dropping its origin, which is related to a public right for the benefit of the community. This is similar to what will come in qisas (see paragraph 15/49).
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's general introduction to fiqh discusses the legal nature of fixed punishments (hudud). The author first establishes that hudud are rights of Allah (huquq Allah) because their enforcement protects the five essential values (daruriyyat khams). Therefore, once established, no pardon is valid from anyone. However, an exception is made for qadhf (false accusation of adultery), which has both a personal right (of the victim) and a divine right. Hence, the victim's pardon drops the hadd. The footnote by Dr. Mahmasani argues for the continued relevance of hudud in modern times. The passage then clarifies a crucial point: the dropping of the fixed punishment does not erase the public right to punish; the perpetrator may still receive ta'zir (discretionary punishment). This shows that hudud have two components: the specific fixed penalty (right of Allah) and the general punitive authority (right of the community). Thus, if the specific punishment falls due to a procedural doubt, the judge may still impose a lesser discretionary punishment to uphold public interest.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): A specific fixed punishment prescribed by Shari'a for certain crimes against essential societal interests.
حق الله (haqq Allah): A right that belongs to the public interest of the Muslim community, not to any individual; enforcement is the duty of the ruler.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment imposed by the judge for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd or when the hadd falls due to doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is true about the hadd of qadhf?
A) It is a pure right of Allah and cannot be dropped by anyone.
B) It contains both a personal right and a divine right, so the victim's pardon drops the hadd.
C) It can be dropped by the ruler at any time.
D) It is not actually a hadd but rather a ta'zir penalty.

Key Takeaway
Hudud are rights of Allah protecting the five necessities; qadhf uniquely combines a personal and divine right.

The Maxim: Dropping the Hadd Does Not Prevent Ta'zir
Explaining the dual nature of hudud and the legal maxim regarding ta'zir after fall of hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'سقوط الحد لا ينافي معاقبة الفاعل بعقوبة أخرى أخف من الحد على سبيل التعزير' to cases where a hadd punishment is dropped.

Core Idea
When a fixed punishment falls due to doubt, the judge retains the right to impose a discretionary punishment for the public good.

This lesson clarifies a crucial point: dropping the specific fixed punishment (hadd) does not erase the public right to punish. The same act may still be punished by a lighter discretionary penalty (ta'zir) if the fall of hadd is due to a legal doubt and the perpetrator deserves discipline. This principle is derived from the dual nature of hudud.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two rights in hudud
Every hadd punishment has two components: the specific fixed penalty (the right of Allah) and the general public right to punish for deterrence and discipline.
2
Identify when the specific penalty drops
The hadd drops due to legal doubts (shubuhat) or, in the case of qadhf, pardon.
The specific penalty is the fixed punishment like amputation for theft.
3
Apply the maxim
Learn the maxim: 'سقوط الحد لا ينافي معاقبة الفاعل بعقوبة أخرى أخف من الحد على سبيل التعزير'. This means the judge may still impose a lighter discretionary punishment (ta'zir) when the hadd falls.
This is a sub-maxim (dabt) restricting the general principle that hudud are non-negotiable.
4
Test with a case
Consider a theft where the hadd falls due to a doubt (e.g., the thief was co-owner of the property). The judge can still give a ta'zir punishment to discipline the thief and protect public interest.
This maintains social order even when the strict hadd conditions are not met.

Chapter (49): Disciplinary Reinforcements: Fixed Punishments. 6/49 - And when these crimes are established by their legally acceptable proofs and their fixed punishments become obligatory, no pardon from the victim nor leniency from the ruler is permissible, because they are from the rights of the Shari'a which are called the rights of Allah, since their enforcement is related to safeguarding a fundamental aspect of the Islamic social system, namely the preservation of the five necessities: religion, life, lineage, intellect, and wealth. However, the hadd of qadhf (slander) among these fixed punishments differs from the rest in that Islamic ijtihads are nearly agreed that it is not a pure right of the Shari'a, but rather it contains a personal right and a Shari'a right. Therefore, for its enforcement, a personal claim is required from the victim if alive, or from one of his ascendants or descendants if the victim is deceased, and the hadd drops with their pardon. (Footnote: Dr. Subhi Mahmasani's comment on punishments in Islamic law...). Note: It must be noted that the dropping of the hadd means dropping its specific punishment, not the dropping of the public right in the original punishment. Thus, the dropping of hadd does not prevent punishing the perpetrator with another punishment lighter than the hadd by way of ta'zir (discretionary punishment) if the dropping of the hadd is due to a doubt while the perpetrator still deserves discipline. So according to Islamic jurists, hadd punishments contain two rights: the general right of the community in the original punishment for discipline and deterrence, and the right of Allah (the right of Shari'a) in the specific fixed punishment. Dropping the specific punishment does not necessitate dropping its origin, which is related to a public right for the benefit of the community. This is similar to what will come in qisas (see paragraph 15/49).
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's general introduction to fiqh discusses the legal nature of fixed punishments (hudud). The author first establishes that hudud are rights of Allah (huquq Allah) because their enforcement protects the five essential values (daruriyyat khams). Therefore, once established, no pardon is valid from anyone. However, an exception is made for qadhf (false accusation of adultery), which has both a personal right (of the victim) and a divine right. Hence, the victim's pardon drops the hadd. The footnote by Dr. Mahmasani argues for the continued relevance of hudud in modern times. The passage then clarifies a crucial point: the dropping of the fixed punishment does not erase the public right to punish; the perpetrator may still receive ta'zir (discretionary punishment). This shows that hudud have two components: the specific fixed penalty (right of Allah) and the general punitive authority (right of the community). Thus, if the specific punishment falls due to a procedural doubt, the judge may still impose a lesser discretionary punishment to uphold public interest.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment imposed by the judge for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd or when the hadd falls due to doubt.
شبهة (shubha): Legal doubt that prevents the application of hadd, often due to ambiguous evidence or mixed ownership.
سقوط الحد (suqut al-hadd): The dropping of the fixed punishment due to a legal impediment (e.g., doubt, pardon).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if the hadd punishment for theft is dropped due to a legal doubt, can the judge still punish the thief?
A) No, because dropping the hadd means the act is no longer punishable.
B) Yes, the judge may impose ta'zir (discretionary punishment) because the public right to discipline remains.
C) Yes, but only if the thief is a repeat offender.
D) No, because hadd and ta'zir cannot be combined.

Key Takeaway
Dropping the specific fixed punishment does not erase the public right to punish; ta'zir may still be imposed.

Qisas: Definition, Linguistic Roots, and Quranic Mandate
Defining qisas linguistically and legally with Quranic citation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qisas linguistically and legally, cite its Quranic basis (Quran 2:178-179), and explain its role as a private right that may be waived.

Core Idea
Qisas is a prescribed retributive right that deters aggression and preserves life, but can be pardoned by the victim's heirs.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of qisas (retributive justice) in Hanafi law. You will learn its linguistic origin, precise legal definition, and the primary Quranic evidence that establishes it as a divine prescription. Understanding qisas is essential for grasping how Islamic criminal law balances justice, deterrence, and mercy through the victim's right to demand or forgive punishment.

Guided Path
1
Trace the linguistic root
The term 'qisas' is derived from 'iqtisas al-athar', meaning to follow a trace. Note also 'qawad', which refers to leading the offender to execution. This linguistic origin hints at the precise equivalence required in retribution.
Both terms are used interchangeably in Hanafi fiqh.
2
State the legal definition
Qisas is punishing the intentional offender for murder, amputation, or wounding with an exact equivalent act. Identify the three crimes it covers: killing (nafs), cutting (qat'), and wounding (jarh).
The definition is given in the passage: 'معاقبة الجاني على جريمة القتل أو القطع أو الجرح عمداً بمثلها'.
3
Locate the Quranic proof
The passage cites Quran 2:178 as the primary evidence: 'كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ فِي الْقَتْلَى'. Note the word 'كُتِبَ' indicates prescription (wujub) in Hanafi usul.
The verse establishes qisas for killing; limbs are subject to scholarly disagreement.
4
Understand the wisdom behind qisas
Quran 2:179 explains that qisas preserves life ('لَكُمْ فِي الْقِصَاصِ حَيَاةٌ'). The passage elaborates that this deterrent effect protects society by making the potential aggressor hesitate.
This is a key argument in justifying retributive punishment over other penalties.
5
Note the nature of qisas as a private right
Although the passage focuses on definition, Hanafi law treats qisas as a right of the victim (or heirs) that can be waived or settled for diyah. This distinguishes it from hadd punishments.
This principle explains many practical fatwa rulings.

البحث الثاني : القصاص
٨/٤٩ - القصاص (بكسر القاف) في اللغة مأخوذ من اقتصاص الأثر بمعنى اتباعه وتعقبه. ثم غلب استعماله في معنى قتل القاتل لما فيه من تتبع أثر الدم بالعقاب (ر : المصباح، ومفردات القرآن للراغب الأصفهاني). ويسمى القتل على سبيل القِصَاص أيضاً : قودا (بفتحتين)(۱) إذ كانت العادة أن يقاد القاتل بحبل أو نحوه إلى القصاص (ر ) رد المحتار ٣٤٠/٥).
والقصاص في لسان الشرع هو :
معاقبة الجاني على جريمة القتل أو القطع أو الجرح عمداً بمثلها . وقد جاء القرآن العظيم بشريعة القصاص في النفوس(٢) بقوله تعالى : ﴿ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُواْ كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ فِي الْقَتْلَى ﴾ [سورة البقرة ٢/ ۱۷۸] . ثم بين حكمته وثمرته الاجتماعية بقوله : ﴿ وَلَكُمْ فِي الْقِصَاصِ حَيَاةٌ يَا أُولِي الْأَلْبَابِ لَعَلَّكُمْ تَتَّقُونَ ﴾ [سورة البقرة ٢/ ١٧٩].
فأفاد أنَّ نماء الحياة الاجتماعية الطيبة بحقن الدماء وصون النفوس والأجسام من العدوان يتوقف على القصاص الذي فيه تحقيق للعدالة الكاملة وحماية للبنية الإنسانية من أن يستهان بالعدوان عليها، كي يعلم المعتدي على حياة غيره أو جسمه أنه كأنما يعتدي في النهاية بنظير ذلك على نفسه عن طريق القصاص. فعندئذ يتهيب الإقدام على هذا الجرم الهدام حرصاً على نفسه. وإن الواقع المستمر ينبيء بأن كل العقوبات غير هذه الجريمة الفظيعة من حبس أو تغريم أو غيرهما، إنما (تكملة في المتن) (١) يقال: أقاد الحاكم القاتل بالقتيل، إذا قتله به قوداً، أي قصاصاً. واستقدت الحاكم من القاتل، فأقادني منه أي اقتص لي منه. (المصباح).
(٢) أما قصاص الأعضاء فقد اختلف العلماء في مصدر ثبوته هل هو القرآن أو السنة؟ وسبب الاختلاف أن الآية القرآنية التي تضمنته إنما تحكي عما شرعه الله في التوراة.
Chapter (49) Disciplinary Sanctions: Fixed Punishments. Second Section: Qisas. 49/8 - Qisas (with kasra on the qaf) linguistically derived from 'iqtisas al-athar' meaning following and tracing. Then its usage prevailed in the meaning of killing the killer because it involves following the trace of blood through punishment (see: al-Misbah, Mufradat al-Qur'an by al-Raghib al-Isfahani). Killing by way of qisas is also called 'qawad' (with two fatha) because it was customary to lead the killer with a rope or similar to the place of qisas (see: Radd al-Muhtar 5/340). And qisas in the language of the Law (shar') is: punishing the offender for the crime of intentional killing, amputation, or wounding with its like. The Great Quran came with the legislation of qisas for lives by His saying: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is qisas for the killed...' [Surah al-Baqarah 2:178]. Then it explained its wisdom and social fruit by His saying: 'And there is for you in qisas life, O people of understanding, that you may become righteous.' [Surah al-Baqarah 2:179]. So it indicated that the growth of good social life by preserving blood and protecting lives and bodies from aggression depends on qisas, which achieves complete justice and protects the human structure from being belittled in aggression against it, so that the aggressor against another's life or body knows that he is, in the end, as if aggressing against the like of himself through qisas. Then he becomes hesitant to commit this destructive crime out of concern for himself. And continuous reality indicates that all punishments other than this heinous crime – such as imprisonment, fines, or others – are [insufficient].
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage is a formal legal definition of qisas (retributive punishment) in Hanafi fiqh, presented as part of the discussion on fixed disciplinary sanctions. It first gives the linguistic origin: qisas comes from 'following the trace', and is also called 'qawad' because the offender is led to execution. The legal definition is then stated: qisas is punishing the intentional offender for homicide, amputation, or wounding with the exact equivalent act. The Quranic basis is cited from Surah al-Baqarah (2:178-179), establishing qisas as a prescribed (maktub) law for believers. The author then elaborates on the social wisdom: qisas deters aggression, preserves life, and ensures justice. The passage also hints at a scholarly disagreement regarding the source for qisas of limbs (whether from Quran or Sunnah), which is noted in a footnote. This definition aligns with the Hanafi position that qisas is a private right (haqq al-'abd) that may be waived by the victim's heirs or pardoned, but the state enforces it if pursued. The emphasis on life-preserving wisdom is a common motif in Hanafi legal philosophy.

Key Terms
القصاص (al-qisas): Retributive punishment for intentional killing or injury, carried out by the state or guardians at the request of the victim's heirs.
القود (al-qawad): Synonym for qisas, specifically used for the act of leading the offender to execution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Quranic evidence for the legislation of qisas for killing, as cited in the passage?
A) Surah al-Ma'idah 5:32
B) Surah al-Baqarah 2:178
C) Surah al-Isra' 17:33
D) Surah al-Nisa' 4:92

Key Takeaway
Qisas is a divinely prescribed retributive right that balances justice and mercy, and in Hanafi law it is a private claim that can be waived.
