Types and Conditions of Qisas (Retribution)
Explains the types of qisas and its three essential conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions for qisas and apply the Hanafi ruling on equivalence in qisas to determine whether qisas or alternative punishment applies.

Core Idea
Qisas requires transgression, intent, and exact equivalence; otherwise, the punishment shifts to diya or ta'zir.

This lesson unpacks the Islamic system of retributive justice (qisas) as a measured, state-regulated alternative to tribal vengeance that prevailed in pre-Islamic Arabia. We will explore the two types of qisas—life and limb—and the three essential conditions that must be present for qisas to be applicable. Special attention is given to the Hanafi school's nuanced position on the condition of equivalence (mumathala), which is the most expansive in life qisas and the most restrictive in limb qisas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of qisas
State the two categories of qisas: qisas for life (in murder) and qisas for limbs (in cutting and wounding).
This is foundational before analyzing conditions.
2
List the three conditions
From the text, extract the three conditions: transgression ('udwan), intent ('amd), and possibility of equivalence (imkan al-mumathala).
Each condition has a distinct legal effect.
3
Explain the condition of equivalence in limb qisas
Note that equivalence must be exact—e.g., cutting at the joint for a hand, not breaking a bone. Hanafis additionally require waiting until the victim recovers to assess the exact harm.
The narrowest scope among schools.
4
Explain the condition of equivalence in life qisas
In life qisas, Hanafis apply equivalence broadly: all inviolable lives (Muslim, dhimmi, male, female, free, slave) are equal, provided the killing is intentional and transgressive.
The broadest scope among schools.
5
Reflect on the wisdom behind qisas
Consider why qisas is preferable to personal vengeance and how it prevents greater injustice.
Links legal rule to higher objectives (maqasid).

قصاص في النفس، وذلك في جريمة القتل، وقصاص في الأعضاء وذلك في جريمة القطع والجرح.

اقتص له منه .
فإذا انتفى العدوان فلا جريمة ولا قصاص كالقتل بحق في الدفاع المشروع.
- وإذا انتفى العمد كان خطأ، وموجبه ضمان مالي لا قصاص كما سنرى. وقد يستوجب عقوبة خفيفة على التقصير في الاحتياط، أو عدم الانتباه.
- وإذا لم تكن المماثلة ممكنة أو كانت مشكوكاً في إمكانها بأن كان يخشى أن يزيد القصاص على الجناية في الكيفية أو الكمية انتفى القصاص أيضاً، وانتقل الحكم إلى عقوبة تعزيرية غير مقدرة، مع الضمان المالي الآتي بيانه (ر: ف ١٢/٤٩).

ومن ثم كانت الأحوال المتفق على جريان قصاص الأعضاء فيها ضيقة جداً. وأضيقها ما في الاجتهاد الحنفي ولا يقتص فيها إلا بعد برء المجني عليه لتعرف نتيجة جراحته . أما قصاص النفس فالاجتهاد الحنفي فيه أوسع الاجتهادات في شريطة التماثل هذه. فهو يرى جريانه بين جميع الأنفس المعصومة عصمة مؤبدة دون تمييز بسبب حرية أو جنس أو دين متى كان القتل عمداً عدواناً.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legally Prescribed Punishments in Fiqh. It facilitates their commission because the criminal is reassured that he will ultimately be safe, splendid, and enjoying himself; and it also leaves resentment in souls, driving the victim's family to harbor thoughts of personal revenge, thus making the crime have long-lasting consequences. So if someone's eye is gouged out or his hand cut off intentionally and aggressively by a sinful aggressor, how can he bear to see the offender staring with two eyes or enjoying two hands? In pre-Islamic times, the killing of a person would lead to the annihilation of entire tribes in vengeance, so the system of retribution (qisas) came as a healing, realistic remedy preventing transgression. And if it is said—as seen in some modern foreign theories—that aggression against a person is a destruction of human structure, and we must repair what is destroyed as much as possible, not add another destruction done by our will as retribution, the answer is that the subsequent destruction is avoided by abandoning qisas, but rather it increases, but at the hands of the people themselves, and in an unjust manner; so it is better for us to avoid the much oppressive destruction caused by chaos by the little that the hands of justice inflict by way of similar punishment for which there is no prevention except it, for nothing stops an archer like his certainty that his arrow will rebound to his chest. (Ref: F. 49/11) 9/49 - Types of Qisas and its Conditions: It is clear from the definition of qisas that it is of two types: qisas for life (nafs) - that is for the crime of murder; and qisas for limbs - that is for the crime of cutting and wounding. Generally, for an aggression to be a crime requiring qisas, three conditions are required: transgression ('udwan), intent ('amd), and possibility of equivalence (imkan al-mumathala) between the crime and its qisas. (1) Hence qisas is called imtithal (exacting), as one says in language: 'al-hakim imthala min fulan li-fulan' if he exacted qisas from him. So if transgression is absent, there is no crime and no qisas, like killing by right in legitimate defense. And if intent is absent, it is a mistake (khata'), and its consequence is financial compensation, not qisas, as we shall see. It may also require a light penalty for negligence in precaution or inattention. And if equivalence is not possible or its possibility is doubtful, such that it is feared that the qisas may exceed the crime in quality or quantity, then qisas also ceases, and the ruling shifts to a discretionary punishment (ta'zir) not fixed, along with the financial compensation to be explained later (Ref: F. 49/12). 10/49 - The condition of equivalence is particularly evident in limb qisas, for it is only applied where such equivalence can be achieved, like gouging out an eye, cutting off a hand at the joint, and pulling out a tooth, as opposed to breaking a bone or cutting a hand not at the joint, due to the impossibility of controlling the qisas so that it does not exceed the crime in its result or in the complications it would usually lead to. Similarly, a healthy hand is not cut off in qisas for a paralyzed hand because of the inequality in their benefits. This condition of equivalence has been greatly differed over by ijtihad schools in its limits. Consequently, the cases agreed upon for limb qisas are very narrow. The narrowest is in Hanafi ijtihad: they do not exact qisas for limbs until after the victim's recovery to know the result of his wound. As for life qisas, the Hanafi ijtihad is the broadest among ijtihads regarding this condition of equivalence. It holds that it applies between all inviolable lives with perpetual inviolability, without distinction due to freedom, gender, or religion, whenever the killing is intentional and aggressive. (1) Note that the ruler must take measures to stop bleeding by available medical methods.
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses the theory of legally prescribed punishments (al-mu'ayyidat al-shar'iyya), focusing on qisas (retribution). The author begins by explaining the wisdom behind qisas: it prevents criminals from feeling safe after committing crimes and deters personal vengeance that could spiral into widespread conflict. He contrasts the pre-Islamic practice of tribal vengeance with the Islamic system of qisas, which is a measured and just remedy. Responding to modern theories that oppose retribution, he argues that abandoning qisas would lead to greater injustice through mob violence. Then he formally defines qisas as being of two types: qisas for life (in murder) and qisas for limbs (in cutting and wounding). Three conditions are essential for qisas to apply: 1) 'Udwan (transgression) – the act must be unjustified; if it is in self-defense, there is no crime. 2) 'Amd (intent) – if the act was accidental, only financial compensation (diya) is required. 3) Imkaan al-mumathala (possibility of equivalence) – the retribution must be exactly equivalent to the crime; if exact equivalence is doubtful or impossible (e.g., breaking a bone where the extent of harm cannot be matched), qisas is replaced by ta'zir (discretionary punishment) and compensation. The passage then elaborates on limb qisas: it is only applicable where precise equivalence is achievable, such as gouging an eye, cutting at the joint, or pulling a tooth. For example, a healthy hand cannot be taken for a paralyzed hand due to inequality in benefit. The author notes significant ikhtilaf among schools regarding the limits of equivalence. Importantly, he states that the Hanafi school is the most restrictive in limb qisas, requiring that the victim fully recover before exacting qisas to ascertain the exact result of the injury. However, in life qisas, the Hanafi school is the most expansive: it applies between all inviolable lives (al-nufus al-ma'suma 'isma mu'abbada) without distinction of freedom, gender, or religion, provided the killing is intentional and transgressive. This bold principle underscores the Hanafi commitment to the sanctity of life and equality in retribution.

Key Terms
القصاص (al-qisas): A legally prescribed punishment of exact retaliation: life for life, limb for limb, based on equivalence.
العدوان (al-'udwan): Transgression; an act that is unlawful and unjustified, such as an assault without legal right.
العمد (al-'amd): Intent; deliberate act with the intention to cause harm.
المماثلة (al-mumathala): Equivalence; the condition that the retribution exactly matches the crime in kind, quantity, and quality.
النفس المعصومة (al-nafs al-ma'suma): An inviolable life, i.e., a person whose life is protected by Islamic law (Muslim, dhimmi, musta'min).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Transgression, injury, and death
D) Intent, physical harm, and retaliation

Key Takeaway
For qisas to apply, the act must be transgressive and intentional, and exact equivalence must be possible; otherwise, the punishment shifts to financial compensation or discretionary penalty.

The Condition of Similarity (Mumathala) in Qisas: Hanafi Application for Limbs and Life
Explains the condition of similarity in qisas and its Hanafi application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi condition of similarity in qisas and distinguish its application for limbs (restrictive) versus for life (expansive) using the principle of equality in human sanctity.

Core Idea
Qisas requires exact similarity, but Hanafi school applies it restrictively for limbs and expansively for life based on equal sanctity.

The requirement of exact equivalence (mumathala) is central to qisas. However, its interpretation varies. The Hanafi school takes the narrowest view for limbs — limiting qisas to cases where precise equivalence is achievable — and the broadest view for life, making all protected lives equal. This lesson explores the rationale and practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three necessary conditions for qisas
From the passage, list the conditions: presence of aggression (no self-defense), intent (not a mistake), and possibility of exact similarity. If any condition fails, qisas is replaced by financial compensation or discretionary punishment.
The passage first establishes general principles before discussing similarity.
2
Analyze similarity in limb retaliation
Qisas for limbs is allowed only where exact equivalence can be achieved, e.g., gouging an eye, cutting a hand at the joint, pulling a tooth. It is not allowed for broken bones or cutting a hand elsewhere because precise parity is impossible. Also, a healthy hand cannot be taken for a paralyzed hand.
Hanafi school requires waiting until the victim heals to assess the injury before executing limb qisas.
3
Contrast with similarity in retaliation for life
For life, the Hanafi school is the broadest: qisas applies between all protected lives without distinction of freedom, gender, religion, sanity, age, or number. The free can be killed for the slave, man for woman, Muslim for non-Muslim, etc.
Other schools impose restrictions like free for free, Muslim for Muslim.
4
Understand the underlying reasoning (talil)
The basis for the broad Hanafi position is the equality in the sanctity of the human person (التساوي في حرمة الشخصية الإنسانية). All protected lives are equally inviolable.
This principle is explicitly stated in the passage.
5
Apply the principle to a case
Consider a Muslim killing a non-Muslim citizen. Under Hanafi, qisas is wajib. Under other schools, it may not be. Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).
This distinction is key for fatwa.

- If aggression is absent, there is no crime and no retaliation, like killing in legitimate self-defense.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Support Measures in Jurisprudence
So, the free person is killed for the slave, the man for the woman, the Muslim for the non-Muslim, the sane for the insane, the adult for the newborn child, and the group for the individual, due to equality in the sanctity of the human person.
Bidāyat al-Mujtahid 2/331-342; Radd al-Muhtār 5/342-344 and 353-360.

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm by Mustafa al-Zarqa discusses the legal conditions for qisas (retaliation). The author first lists general principles: qisas requires aggression (without which there is no crime), requires intent (if absent, it becomes a financial liability), and requires exact similarity (mumathala) between the offense and the retaliation. He then elaborates on the condition of similarity in retaliation for limbs, noting that it is restricted to cases where precise equivalence can be achieved, such as gouging an eye or cutting a hand at the joint. The Hanafi school is most restrictive in limb retaliation, requiring the victim to heal first to assess the injury. However, in retaliation for life, the Hanafi school is the broadest, allowing qisas between all categories of protected lives—free and slave, male and female, Muslim and non-Muslim, sane and insane, adult and child, and even one person for a group—based on the fundamental equality of human sanctity. This expansive view is supported by references to classical works.

Key Terms
المماثلة (al-mumāthala): The legal condition that retaliation must be exactly equivalent to the injury in kind, quantity, and manner.
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retaliation in kind, either death for death or injury for injury, prescribed for intentional aggression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Hanafi Definition of Intent (Amad) in Retaliation
Defines 'amad and critiques modern legal distinctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi definition of 'amad for qisas, distinguish it from modern legal concepts of qasd, and evaluate the implications for fatwa on intentional killings.

Core Idea
In Hanafi qisas, 'amad requires only deliberate intention—not premeditation—making both planned and sudden intentional killings liable for retaliation.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi legal definition of 'amad (intent) in the context of qisas (retaliation). The source clarifies that 'amad is simply the deliberate intention to commit the offense, regardless of prior planning. This stands in contrast to some modern penal codes that require premeditation for capital punishment. Understanding this distinction is crucial for muftis when applying qisas in contemporary cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'amad in the source
Locate the author's definition of 'amad: 'the intention to commit the offense, even if not based on prior conception and design.'
This definition is broader than premeditation.
2
Analyze the conditions for qisas liability
Note the two conditions: (1) the killer intends the killing, and (2) the means and manner typically lead to death. Both must be present for liability.
Intent can be instantaneous; no prior design required.
3
Contrast with modern legal distinctions
The author cites Dr. Subhi Mahmasani noting that some modern laws (e.g., Ottoman and Lebanese) distinguish between 'amad (premeditated) and qasd (intent without premeditation), requiring premeditation for execution.
This distinction is foreign to Hanafi fiqh.
4
Critique the modern distinction
Al-Zarqa criticizes this distinction as imitating French law without Islamic basis, arguing it undermines the protective purpose of qisas.
The Hanafi position aligns with justice and deterrence.
5
Apply to fatwa formulation
As a mufti, when asked about qisas for an intentional killing, you would rule that sudden intent suffices for liability, unless there is a valid legal preventer (mani).
The fatwa must rely on the Hanafi definition, not modern codes.

١١/٤٩ - معنى العمد :
ومعنى العمد شرعاً في هذا المقام هو مجرد القصد إلى الجناية الواقعة ولو لم يكن عن سابق تصور وتصميم. فالعمد في القتل أن يقصده القاتل المعتدي بوسيلة وصورة تفضيان إليه ويموت المجنى عليه من ذلك. فهذا يكفي في استحقاق الجاني عقوبة الموت قصاصاً، سواء أكان مبيتاً في نفسه فكرة الجريمة وعازماً عليها بسابق تصميم وإعداد، أو كان قصده إليها آنياً مرتجلاً حين ارتكابها (۱) ..

إن قانون الجزاء العثماني، والقانون اللبناني، قد جارى واضعوهما في ذلك، وقلدوا القانون الإفرنسي وبعض القوانين الأجنبية الأخرى بلا حجة سوى التأثر ببعض النظريات الأجنبية العشواء الجامحة. وبناءً على ذلك ميّز القانون بين العمد والقصد. فاشترط في العمد لاستحقاق الإعدام تبييت العزم على الجريمة في نفس الجاني بتصور وتصميم سابق. أما إذا قَصَدَ القتل بعزيمة آنية مرتجلة فيكون قصداً لا عمداً.
11/49 - Meaning of Amad (Intent):
The meaning of amad legally in this context is simply the intention to commit the offense, even if not based on prior conception and design. So, intent in killing is that the aggressor killer intends it by a means and manner that lead to it, and the victim dies from that. This suffices for the offender to be deserving of the death penalty as retaliation, whether he had harbored the idea of the crime in himself and was determined on it with prior design and preparation, or his intention was instantaneous and improvised at the time of perpetration (1).
(1) The investigating professor Dr. Subhi Mahmasani says in his study on retaliation: “Intent in killing means the intention, even if there is no prior conception and design, and its punishment is retaliation in Islamic law. In many ancient and modern legal systems, as well as in English law, prior conception and design are not required in intentional killing for the death penalty to be deserved. Refer to Harris’s Criminal Code, p. 147. We see that this is justice, indeed justice; rather we consider it necessary to reduce murder crimes and to protect people in society. But some criminal laws, including the old Ottoman Law (Art. 170) and the new Lebanese Law (Art. 549), required for the execution of the killer that amad mean prior conception and design.” End quote.
From the book “Al-Mujibat wa al-‘Uqud fi al-Shari‘ah al-Islamiyyah” by the professor mentioned, 1/141.
I say:
The Ottoman Penal Code and the Lebanese law have followed their drafters in this, imitating French law and some other foreign laws without any proof other than being influenced by some wild foreign theories. Based on that, the law distinguished between amad and qasd. It required for amad to deserve execution the premeditation of the resolve in the offender’s mind with prior conception and design. But if he intended the killing with an instantaneous, improvised will, it is qasd, not amad.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This section defines the key concept of 'amad (intent) in Hanafi law for qisas. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, clarifies that 'amad is simply the deliberate intention to commit the assault, regardless of whether it was premeditated or spontaneous. This is in line with the Hanafi school, which does not require prior planning for the death penalty in qisas. The author then quotes Dr. Subhi Mahmasani, who notes that many legal systems, including Islamic law, do not require premeditation for intentional killing. Al-Zarqa criticizes modern laws (Ottoman and Lebanese) that distinguish between 'amad (premeditated) and qasd (intent without premeditation), attributing this distinction to foreign influence and arguing that it undermines the protective purpose of qisas.

Key Terms
العمد (al-‘amad): The intention to commit the offense, even without prior conception and design.
القصد (al-qaṣd): In modern laws: intent without premeditation, distinguished from 'amad which requires premeditation. In Hanafi fiqh, al-qasd is synonymous with 'amad for liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The killer must have premeditated the act with prior planning
B) The killer must have intended to kill, even if spontaneously
C) The killer must have acted with extreme negligence
D) The killer must have used a deadly weapon

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qisas, 'amad means deliberate intent, not necessarily premeditation, and this broad definition is crucial for justice and deterrence.

Definition of 'Amd in Qisas: Intent Without Premeditation
Definition of legal intent in homicide, distinguishing Islamic law from modern codes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'amd (intent) in qisas and identify the key differences between the Islamic position and modern codes that require premeditation.

Core Idea
ʿAmd is mere intention to commit the harmful act; premeditation is not required for qisas.

This passage defines the concept of 'amd in Islamic criminal law, specifically in the context of qisas (retaliation). The author clarifies that 'amd does not require prior planning or design; spontaneous intent suffices. He then critiques modern legal codes that introduced a distinction between 'amd and qasd, arguing that this deviation from Islamic law is unjust and ineffective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its legal context
Recognize that 'amd is being defined in the context of qisas for homicide. The author first emphasizes equality in legal protection, then defines 'amd.
The passage begins with general principles of equality before turning to the definition.
2
Extract the core components of the definition
Note that 'amd requires: (1) intention toward the criminal act (qasd ila al-jinaya), and (2) a means and manner likely to cause death. It does NOT require prior visualization or design (sabq al-tasawwur wa al-tasmim).
The definition is comprehensive: intent + lethal means + resulting death.
3
Contrast with common misconception and modern laws
Many modern codes (e.g., Ottoman, Lebanese) require premeditation for 'amd. The author argues this is a foreign import without evidence, leading to injustice.
The distinction between 'amd and qasd in modern law is criticized as baseless.
4
Analyze the author's critique
The author quotes Dr. al-Mahmasani and then offers his own opinion. He asserts that the Islamic rule is just and necessary for deterrence.
This critique shows the importance of adhering to the original Hanafi position.
5
Apply the definition to a scenario
Given a case where a person impulsively kills another, determine whether it constitutes 'amd. Use the definition: if the killer intended the act and used a lethal means, it is 'amd regardless of planning.
This step solidifies understanding through practical application.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Sanctions in Jurisprudence. The free person is killed for the slave, the man for the woman, the Muslim for the non-Muslim, the sane for the insane, the adult for the newborn infant, and the group for the individual, due to equality in the sanctity of human personality. See Bidayat al-Mujtahid 2/331-342 and Radd al-Muhtar 5/342-344 and 353-360. 11/49 - Meaning of 'Amd: The legal meaning of 'amd in this context is merely the intention towards the criminal act that occurred, even if without prior planning and design. So 'amd in killing is that the aggressor killer intends it by a means and manner that lead to it and the victim dies from that. This suffices for the offender to deserve the death penalty by qisas, whether the idea of the crime was premeditated in his mind and he was determined upon it with prior design and preparation, or his intention was immediate and spontaneous at the time of its commission. (1) (1) The verifying professor Dr. Subhi al-Mahmasani says in his research on qisas: 'Amd in killing means intention even if there is no prior visualization and design, and its punishment is qisas in Islamic law, and in many ancient and modern laws, as well as in English law, prior visualization and design in intentional killing are not required for eligibility for the death penalty. See Harris's Law of Penalties, p. 147. We see that this is justice, indeed justice; rather we see it necessary to reduce murder crimes and to protect people in society. But some penal codes, including the old Ottoman code (art. 170) and the new Lebanese one (art. 549), required for the execution of the killer that 'amd means prior visualization and design, end quote. I say: The Ottoman Penal Code and the Lebanese Code - their drafters followed in that and imitated the French law and some other foreign laws without any proof except being influenced by some reckless foreign theories. Accordingly, the law distinguished between 'amd and intention (qasd). It required in 'amd for eligibility for execution the premeditation of the resolve in the mind of the offender with prior visualization and design. But if he intended killing with an immediate, spontaneous resolve, it becomes intention, not 'amd.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling defining the concept of 'amd (intent) in the context of homicide and qisas in Islamic criminal law. The author first reiterates the general rule of equality in legal protection across social categories. Then, he provides the legal definition: 'amd is simply the intention to commit the harmful act, regardless of whether the offender premeditated or decided spontaneously at the moment. This is the established Hanafi position that spontaneous intent is sufficient for qisas. The author supports this with a quotation from Dr. Subhi al-Mahmasani and critiques modern Ottoman and Lebanese laws that introduced a distinction between 'amd (requiring premeditation) and qasd (spontaneous intent), allowing a lesser penalty. The author argues that this distinction leads to increased violence. The passage emphasizes that the Islamic rule is both just and necessary for deterrence.

Key Terms
العمد (al-'amd): Intent to commit the criminal act, without requiring prior planning or premeditation.
القصد (al-qasd): Spontaneous intention, distinguished from 'amd in some modern laws but synonymous in Islamic jurisprudence.
سبق التصور والتصميم (sabq al-tasawwur wa al-tasmim): Prior visualization and design; premeditation.

Ta'zir and Financial Compensation When Qisas is Inapplicable
Explanation of discretionary punishments and financial penalties for crimes where qisas cannot apply

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when ta'zir and financial compensation replace qisas, distinguish between diyah, arsh, and hukumat al-adl, and explain the distinction between judicial (qada'i) and religious (diyani) obligations in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
When qisas cannot apply, the state imposes ta'zir and financial compensation; accidental killing adds a religious expiation (kaffarah) beyond state reach.

If we live in the shadow of the Quranic principle 'in qisas is life,' what happens when qisas cannot be carried out? The Hanafi school provides a structured response: the perpetrator still faces discretionary punishment (ta'zir) and must pay financial compensation. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains these alternatives and then turns a critical eye to modern penal codes that replace qisas with fixed imprisonment. This lesson unpacks the fiqh of punishments when qisas is inapplicable and equips the mufti to evaluate secular alternatives against the shari'ah framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the crime and why qisas is inapplicable
Read the passage to find the condition for ta'zir and compensation: any unlawful aggression where qisas is prevented due to missing conditions.
e.g., perpetrator not legally responsible, or victim has no heir demanding qisas.
2
Determine which financial compensation applies
Three types: diyah (for life), arsh (for limb), hukumat al-adl (for other injuries). The passage states amounts are fixed for life and limbs.
Hukumat al-adl is not fixed; it is assessed equitably.
3
Recognize the discretionary nature of ta'zir
Note the explicit phrase: 'عقوبة غير مقدرة، بل مفوّضة إلى رأي السلطان'. Ta'zir is not fixed; the ruler decides the measure to reform the offender.
4
Distinguish the case of accidental killing (qatl al-khata')
In addition to diyah, kaffarah is wajib diyani (religious duty): freeing a slave or fasting two months. It is not enforced by courts.
The passage contrasts this with qisas, which is judicial.
5
Evaluate the modern secular alternative
The author criticizes replacing qisas with fixed imprisonment. As a mufti, you must advise that the shari'ah alternatives are ta'zir (discretionary) plus diyah/arsh, not a fixed prison term.
This step synthesises the fiqh with contemporary application.

(1) على أن قتل الخطأ قد أوجب فيه ،القرآن علاوة على هذا التعويض المالي، جزاء تعبدياً آخر من قبيل المؤيد الديني يسمى «كفارة».
والكفارة عبارة عن إعتاق رقبة مؤمنة. فمن لم يجد فصيام شهرين متتابعين. وهي واجب دياني لا سلطان عليه للقضاء، بل هو متروك لذمّة الإنسان، تكفيراً، أي تغطية لذنب التقصير الذي لا يخلو عنه الخطأ، وتطهيراً للشخص منه .
Chapter (49): Disciplinary Sanctions: Fixed Punishments. 12/49 - Every unlawful aggression against a life or limb, if qisas is prevented due to the absence of some of its conditions, the perpetrator deserves ta'zir (discretionary punishment) - an unfixed punishment, delegated to the opinion of the ruler as he sees sufficient to reform the offender, similar to what was previously explained in the punishment of hudud in (8/49) - and upon him is financial compensation: diyah, arsh, or hukumat al-adl, according to the type of aggression. So al-diyah (with kasr then fatha) is that which is paid from wealth in place of a life. Al-arsh (with fatha then sukun) is that which is paid in place of limbs; i.e., it is the diyah of a limb. Both the diyah of a life and the diyah of a limb are fixed in amount by law. The law made the punishment for intentional killing (qasd) imprisonment for fifteen years, thereby opening the door for the spread of murder on the widest scale for the most trivial reasons. Every bloodthirsty criminal openly threatens whomever he wants that he will kill him and sleep on one side for fifteen years!! It is regrettable that the new Syrian Penal Code, issued on 26 Sha'ban 1386 AH = 22 June 1949 CE, its drafters also adopted this erroneous theory in distinguishing between 'amd and qasd in articles 533-535 thereof. The experiences of 95 years under the previous Ottoman law were not sufficient to teach that this distinction was the greatest factor in making the crime of murder lighter for its perpetrators than drinking water, especially given the criminals' hopes based on the continuous reality that a prisoner rarely serves his full term without a large part of it being dropped by repeated amnesties on many national occasions, so the killer goes out slithering like a snake before the family of his prey whose blood goes to waste!! How often did these results lead the victim's family to take revenge with their own hands. All of this is due to the law substituting temporary imprisonment for intentional killing instead of the legal qisas about which the Holy Quran said: 'And for you in qisas is life, O people of understanding, that you may become righteous' (1). Moreover, for accidental killing, the Quran, in addition to this financial compensation, prescribed another devotional penalty of the type of religious sanction called 'kaffarah'. Kaffarah is the manumission of a believing slave; whoever does not find (the means) must fast two consecutive months. It is a religious obligation (wajib diyani) over which the judiciary has no authority; rather it is left to the conscience of the person, as expiation, i.e., covering the sin of negligence that accidental killing almost always involves, and purifying the person from it.
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage is a ruling explaining the alternatives to qisas when its conditions are not met. The author first states the principle: any unlawful aggression against life or limb, if qisas cannot apply due to missing conditions (e.g., the killer is not legally responsible, or the victim is an heir), the aggressor is subject to ta'zir (discretionary punishment determined by the ruler) and must also pay financial compensation. The compensation comes in three forms: diyah (blood money for life), arsh (compensation for limbs, which is the diyah for a member), and hukumat al-adl (equitable compensation for injuries not covered by fixed amounts). The amounts for diyah and arsh are fixed by shari'ah. The author then criticizes modern penal codes that replace qisas with imprisonment, arguing that this leads to increased crime and vengeance. He then discusses accidental killing (qatl al-khata'), which requires both diyah and kaffarah. Kaffarah is a religious obligation (wajib diyani) consisting of freeing a slave or fasting two months consecutively; it is not enforced by the state but is a matter of personal conscience to expiate the sin of negligence.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): A discretionary punishment administered for crimes that do not have a fixed hadd or qisas penalty; its measure is left to the judge or ruler.
الدية (al-diyah): Financial compensation paid in place of a life (blood money), with a fixed amount prescribed by shari'ah.
الأرش (al-arsh): Compensation paid for injury to a limb; it is the diyah of that limb, and its amount is fixed proportionally.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): An expiatory act required for accidental killing, consisting of liberating a slave or fasting two consecutive months; it is a religious duty not enforced by courts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the nature of ta'zir punishment?
A) Fixed punishment like hadd
B) Unfixed, left to ruler's discretion
C) Always imprisonment
D) It is only for accidental killing

Key Takeaway
When qisas is unavailable, ta'zir and financial compensation (diyah/arsh) become mandatory; accidental killing additionally requires kaffarah as a religious duty.

Method of Qisas Execution and the Wali's Right to Execute
Ruling on qisas method and wali's execution right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the method of qisas execution and explain the wali's right to execute personally, applying the hadith on excellence in killing to fatwa practice.

Core Idea
Qisas is executed by beheading with a sword; the wali may execute personally under state supervision, but cruelty is prohibited.

This passage addresses two critical aspects of qisas (retaliation) in Hanafi fiqh: the method of execution and the right of the victim's guardian to carry it out. The author clarifies that qisas is not about reciprocating cruelty but about just retribution, limited to beheading with a sword. At the same time, Islamic law grants the wali al-dam the option to personally execute the sentence, provided he is capable and under state supervision. This wisdom balances the need for justice with mercy, potentially leading to pardon. Understanding these rulings is essential for a mufti advising on capital punishment cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling on qisas method
Determine what the Hanafi school prescribes as the default method of execution. The text states it is limited to cutting the neck with a sword (beheading) and that no consideration is given to the criminal's previous torture.
This is the general rule; other methods may be allowed by the ruling authority if equivalent or less harmful.
2
Analyze the hadith evidence for excellence in killing
Examine the hadith 'Indeed, Allah has prescribed excellence in everything...' as the basis for requiring a humane method. Understand how it supports the prohibition of torture and mutilation.
The hadith is a key dalil for the principle of ihsan (excellence) even in punishment.
3
Define the scope of the wali's right to execute
Identify who the wali al-dam is (the victim's heirs, including spouses) and under what conditions he may personally execute: he must be able and skilled, and act under the supervising authority.
This is a right, not an obligation; the wali may delegate or pardon.
4
Evaluate the legal wisdom (siyasa shar'iyya) behind the ruling
Reflect on why Islamic law grants the wali this right: to heal the victim's pain, encourage mercy, and allow the possibility of pardon, which preserves social order.
This approach balances individual right with public interest.
5
Correct common misconceptions about qisas
Identify the false belief that Islamic law allows the family to do whatever they wish to the killer. Contrast this with the actual ruling: limited to beheading, no torture, and state oversight.
This misconception is explicitly refuted in the passage.

طريقة تنفيذ القصاص :
لا عبرة في القصاص لما يكون قد فعله الجاني القاتل من تعذيب للقتيل أو تمثيل به بل يُقتصر في القصاص على إماتة القاتل بقطع عنقه ضرباً بالسيف وهذا هو الاجتهاد الحنفي والحنبلي.
ولا يوجد مانع شرعي من استعمال ما يظهر من وسائل أخرى إذا كانت مساوية للسيف كالمفصلة أو أسرع وأسهل قتلاً. فيعود إلى السلطة الحاكمة أمر اختيار الوسيلة مما لا عنف فيه ولا تعذيب ولا وحشية عملاً بقول رسول الله عليه الصلاة والسلام :
إنَّ الله كتب الإحسان على كل شيء، فإذا قتلتم فأحسنوا القتلة، وإذا ذبحتم فأحسنوا الذبحة» (١).
الجلاد ١٤/٤٩ - ويحق لولي الدم الشرعي، إن شاء أن يكون هو ! الذي يكلف من قبل صاحب السلطان بتنفيذ حكم الإعدام على القاتل المحكوم عليه تحت إشراف السلطة المنفذة إن كان يستطيع ويحسن التنفيذ .
(١) رواه مسلم (١٩٥٥)، والترمذي (١٤٠٩) ، والنسائي (٤٤٠٥)، وابن ماجه (٣١٧٠) من حديث شداد بن أوس رضي الله عنه والقِتْلة، والذبحة (بكسر فسكون فيهما) الصورة والكيفية في القتل والذبح وهذا الحديث من أحاديث الأربعين النووية، وله شرح مستفيض في جامع العلوم والحكم لابن رجب ٣٧٩/١ - ٣٩٤.
وأولياء الدم هم الورثة على ترتيب الإرث والحجب، حتى الزوجان. وهذا تدبير أشفى لألم المصاب دون ضرر بالمحكوم عليه أو النظام الاجتماعي، إذ لا بد من جلاد لتنفيذ حكم الإعدام فليكن هو ولي الدم إن شاء .
وفي هذا أيضاً من السياسة الشرعية أسلوب حكيم. فكما أنه أشفى لألم المصاب هو أيضاً أدعى لرحمة ولي الدم وإشفاقه من التنفيذ، فلعله عندما يقف بنفسه موقف الجلاد يتهيب إراقة الدم فيعفو؛ وعندئذ يسقط حق القصاص، وينتقل الحكم إلى عقوبة مفوّضة لرأي الحاكم غير مقدرة، كما سبق ف (١٢/٤٩ وكما سنرى فيما يلي.
هذا حكم الإسلام بحق ولي الدم في تنفيذ القصاص، لا كما يزعم بعض الدساسين أو الجاهلين أن الشريعة الإسلامية توجب تسليم القاتل إلى ولي الدم يفعل به ما يشاء!! .
Method of Executing Qisas: No consideration is given in qisas to whatever torture or mutilation the murderer may have inflicted on the victim; rather, qisas is limited to causing the death of the killer by cutting his neck with a sword strike. This is the Hanafi and Hanbali ijtihad. And there is no legal impediment to using other means that appear, if they are equivalent to the sword, or faster and easier in killing. So the ruling authority has the discretion to choose a method that involves no violence, torture, or cruelty, acting upon the saying of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): 'Indeed, Allah has prescribed excellence in everything. So when you kill, kill in a good manner; and when you slaughter, slaughter in a good manner.' (1) The Executioner 14/49 - And the legal guardian of the blood (wali al-dam) has the right, if he wishes, to be the one commissioned by the ruler to carry out the death sentence on the convicted killer under the supervision of the executing authority, provided he is able and skilled in execution. (1) Narrated by Muslim (1955), al-Tirmidhi (1409), al-Nasa'i (4405), Ibn Majah (3170) from the hadith of Shaddad ibn Aws (may Allah be pleased with him). 'Al-qitlah' and 'al-dhabhah' (with a kasra on the first letter and sukun on the second) mean the manner and method of killing and slaughtering. This hadith is among the Forty Hadith of al-Nawawi, and it has an extensive commentary in Jami' al-'Ulum wa al-Hikam by Ibn Rajab, 1/379-394. And the guardians of the blood are the heirs according to the order of inheritance and exclusion, including the spouses. This is a measure that better heals the pain of the victim without harming the convict or the social order, since an executioner is necessary for carrying out the death sentence, so let it be the guardian of the blood if he wishes. In this there is also a wise approach of shari'a policy. Just as it better heals the pain of the victim, it is also more conducive to the guardian's mercy and compassion from execution; perhaps when he himself stands in the position of executioner, he will dread shedding blood and thus forgive; then the right of qisas falls, and the ruling transfers to a penalty left to the discretion of the ruler, not predetermined, as previously in 12/49 and as we will see below. This is the ruling of Islam regarding the right of the guardian of the blood to execute qisas, not as some insinuators or ignorant people claim that Islamic law requires handing the killer over to the guardian to do with him as he pleases!!
Source pages p. 646

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the method of executing qisas (retaliation) in Hanafi and Hanbali fiqh. The author explains that qisas is limited to beheading the murderer with a sword; any torture or mutilation the criminal may have inflicted on the victim is not reciprocated. The hadith of the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) is cited as the basis for requiring excellence in killing. The passage also grants the wali al-dam (the legal guardian of the blood, i.e., the victim's heirs) the right to personally execute the punishment if they are capable, under state supervision. This is presented as a wise policy that may encourage the wali to pardon when faced with the gravity of execution. The text refutes the false claim that Islam allows the family to do whatever they wish to the killer. The ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle of minimizing harm and promoting mercy, even in punishment.

Key Terms
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation punishment for intentional murder or injury, limited to causing death of the killer by beheading with a sword.
ولي الدم (wali al-dam): The legal guardian of the blood; the victim's heirs who have the right to demand qisas or pardon.

The Right to Pardon in Qisas and Distinction from Hudud
Explaining the dual nature of qisas and the right to pardon as distinct from hudud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qisas and hudud in terms of the right to pardon, and explain the dual nature of qisas as comprising a public and a private right, applying this to fatwa scenarios.

Core Idea
Qisas can be pardoned by the victim's family; hudud cannot.

In this passage, the author highlights a key difference between qisas (retaliation) and hudud (fixed punishments). While hudud are considered rights of Allah and cannot be pardoned, qisas includes a private right of the victim or his heirs to pardon. Understanding this distinction is essential for a mufti issuing fatwas on murder cases, as the family's decision determines whether qisas applies or a discretionary punishment is imposed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key distinction
Compare qisas and hudud regarding the right to pardon. Note that qisas can be pardoned by the victim or his heirs, while hudud prohibit pardon.
This is the foundational difference.
2
Analyze the dual nature of qisas
The passage explains that qisas comprises two rights: a public right for the community in the principle of punishment, and a private right for the victim to choose the type of punishment (qisas) or pardon.
This dual nature distinguishes qisas from both hudud and ta'zir.
3
Trace the transfer of the private right
The private right transfers from the victim to his guardians (heirs) after the victim's death. The heirs then hold the right to demand qisas or pardon.
This transfer is crucial for inheritance rules.
4
Apply the ruling in fatwa
If the victim's family pardons, the qisas punishment drops and the judge may impose a discretionary punishment (ta'zir). The mufti must advise the family on the legal implications of their decision.
This shows the practical impact on fatwa issuance.

15/4 - Characteristic of Qisas and the Right of the Guardian of Blood to Pardon: It is that it has been legislated in it. Qisas differs from hudud in a fundamental aspect: the victim, and then his guardians of blood, have the right to pardon; and qisas falls by their pardon. As for hudud, pardon is prohibited. Hence the jurists considered that qisas comprises two rights in punishment: a public right for the Shari'a in the principle of punishment, as a deterrent for people and a guarantee of security in the community; and a private right for the victim regarding the type of punishment, that it be qisas for the offender equivalent to his crime. And this right transfers from the victim to his guardians of blood, who are the heirs.
Source pages p. 647

Commentary
This passage is a legal analysis distinguishing qisas (retaliation) from hudud (fixed punishments). The author explains that qisas has a unique feature: the right to pardon resides with the victim and his heirs. If they pardon, qisas drops. In hudud, no such pardon is allowed. The jurists then derive that qisas involves two rights: a public right of the state to punish for deterrence and security, and a private right of the victim to choose the type of punishment (qisas) or to pardon. This private right transfers to the heirs. This distinction has practical implications for fatwa issuance: the victim's family can waive qisas, and then the punishment becomes discretionary (ta'zir). The Hanafi position holds that the right to pardon is strong, and the state should facilitate it.

Key Terms
الحدود (al-hudud): Fixed punishments prescribed by Shari'a for certain crimes (e.g., theft, adultery) that cannot be pardoned.
العفو (al-afw): Pardon by the victim or his heirs that drops the qisas punishment.
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation punishment for intentional murder or injury, equivalent to the crime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Qisas can be pardoned by the victim's family; hudud cannot be pardoned.
B) Both can be pardoned by the ruler.
C) Hudud can be pardoned by the victim; qisas cannot.
D) There is no difference; both are unpardonable.

Key Takeaway
Qisas differs from hudud in that it includes a private right of the victim to pardon, and this right transfers to the heirs, making pardon a valid and recommended option in Hanafi fiqh.

The Dual Rights in Qisas: Public and Private
Explaining the dual nature of qisas and its implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two rights in qisas (public and private) and apply the principle that the private right is limited to the type of punishment, not the basis of punishment.

Core Idea
Qisas comprises two distinct rights: a public right to punish and a private right to choose retaliation or pardon.

In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa explains that qisas (retaliation) is not merely a private matter between the victim's family and the killer. Rather, it contains both a public right (haqq al-shar') for deterrence and a private right for the victim or heirs to request or pardon qisas. Understanding this dual nature is crucial for a mufti when issuing fatwas on criminal cases involving qisas and ta'zir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rights
Read the passage and locate where the author distinguishes between the public right (haqq al-amm) and the private right (haqq al-khas). The public right is the Shari'ah's right to punish for deterrence; the private right is the victim's right to choose qisas or pardon.
The private right is only in the specific type of punishment (i.e., qisas), not in the punishment itself.
2
Understand the effect of pardon
When the blood-guardians pardon qisas, only the private right is waived. The public right remains, allowing the state to impose a discretionary ta'zir punishment. Do not confuse the dropping of qisas with the dropping of all punishment.
This is the key distinction from hudud, where pardon is not allowed.
3
Apply to a fatwa scenario
Suppose a killer's victims' family pardons him. As a mufti, you must rule that qisas is dropped, but the judge may still apply ta'zir if deemed necessary for public order. The private right is limited to the type of punishment, not the punishment itself.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
4
Connect to broader legal theory
Recognize that this dual-right theory is part of Islamic legal philosophy, balancing individual rights and public interest. It refutes the misconception that Islamic law leaves murder entirely to private vengeance.

Chapter (49): Supportive Penalties: Fixed Punishments. The blood-guardians are the heirs according to the order of inheritance and exclusion, including the spouses. This is a measure that heals the pain of the victim without harming the convicted or the social system, for there must be an executioner to carry out the death penalty, so let him be the blood-guardian if he wishes. This is the ruling of Islam regarding the right of the blood-guardian to execute qisas, not as some meddlers or ignorant people claim that Islamic law obligates handing the killer to the blood-guardian to do with him as he wishes!! 15/4 - The nature of qisas and the right of the blood-guardian to pardon: It is that it has been legislated in it. Qisas differs from hudud in a fundamental aspect: the victim and then his blood-guardians have the right to pardon; and qisas falls with their pardon. As for hudud, pardon is forbidden. Hence, the jurists considered that qisas involves two rights in punishment: A public right for the Sharia in the basis of punishment, as deterrence for people, and as a guarantee of security in the community. And a private right for the victim in the type of punishment that it be retaliation for the offender similar to his offense. This right transfers from the victim to his blood-guardians, and they are the heirs. 685: Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Sanctions in Fiqh. From this, qisas (i.e., specific punishment) is contingent for its ruling and execution upon the claim and request of the holder of the private right. If he pardons qisas, it falls, because this type of punishment is his right. Then the general legal right of the ruling authority remains to punish the offender with what it deems sufficient for deterrence. Thus the punishment for the offense becomes a discretionary punishment not fixed, then it enters into the realm of ta'zir as will be explained. If the killed person has no heir, the right of qisas belongs to the ruler; he executes it on behalf of the public and does not have the right to pardon because it would harm them (see: Radd al-Muhtar 5/346). And thus Islamic law has taken a middle path in the matter of qisas, in which laws differed between severity and leniency due to its seriousness. 16/49 - However, the ijtihadat in this regard differ. Some held that the pardon of the blood-guardian absolutely drops the punishment. According to this, the public right of the Sharia in punishing the killer is subsidiary to the private right in it. This has led some people today to imagine that this great Sharia has failed to grasp the idea of public right in the punishment for murder! This is a major error, for one ijtihad among different opinions does not necessarily represent the theory of the Sharia; it does not establish the correct understanding of its texts, but rather represents the opinion of its holder in the disputed matter. The correct view in this matter is what other ijtihadat have articulated, which we have explained above: that the right of the blood-guardian is only in the type of punishment. As for its basis, it is the right of the Sharia, not [؟]
Source pages p. 647

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical analysis of the nature of qisas (retaliation) in Islamic criminal law. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that qisas differs from hudud in that it incorporates both a public right and a private right. The public right is the right of the community (or the Shari'ah) to deter crime and maintain security. The private right belongs to the victim (or his heirs) to demand exact retaliation or to pardon. The author emphasizes that the private right is only in the specific type of punishment (qisas), not in the punishment itself. Thus, if the blood-guardians pardon the qisas, the state still has the right to impose a discretionary ta'zir punishment for the public interest. The author then critiques some who misunderstand this, thinking that Islamic law only has a private right and neglects public interest. He clarifies that such a view is based on one ijtihad that makes the public right dependent on the private right, but the correct position (as presented) maintains the distinct public right. The passage also mentions that if the victim has no heir, the right of qisas goes to the ruler, but he cannot pardon it because that would harm the public. This analysis reflects the Hanafi perspective that qisas involves two separate rights, which has implications for fatwa: a mufti must consider both the public and private dimensions.

Key Terms
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation punishment where the offender is subjected to exactly what he did to the victim, specifically in cases of intentional killing or wounding.
ولي الدم (wali al-dam): The blood-guardian, i.e., the legal heir of the victim who has the right to execute or pardon qisas.
الحق العام (al-haqq al-amm): The public right of the Shari'ah or the community to punish the offender for deterrence and security.
الحق الخاص (al-haqq al-khas): The private right of the victim or his heirs to choose qisas or pardon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The public right to punish via ta'zir remains
D) The state must still execute qisas

Key Takeaway
The private right in qisas is limited to the type of punishment; the public right to punish for deterrence remains after pardon, enabling ta'zir.

The Qawida: Public Rights Are Not Dropped by Individuals
Explaining the qawida and its application to qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'حقوق الشرع العامة لا تسقط بإسقاط أحد' to cases of qisas and ta'zir, and identify the mufta bihi ruling in Hanafi fatwa practice.

Core Idea
Private pardon drops specific punishment, but the state's right to deter crime remains.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational Hanafi principle that distinguishes between private and public rights in criminal law. The passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa explains that while the victim's heir can forgive qisas, the public interest in punishment persists through ta'zir. This is governed by the legal maxim that general rights of the law cannot be nullified by individual action, a key rule for any mufti dealing with retaliation cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the private and public elements in qisas
Retaliation (qisas) is a specific punishment that the victim or heir has the right to claim or forgive. However, the community also has a right to see that crime is punished for deterrence. Recognize that the passage treats these as two distinct layers: private right to the specific type of punishment, and public right to the fact of punishment.
The private right is to the 'type' not the 'origin' of punishment.
2
Apply the qawida to the case of pardon
The qawida 'حقوق الشرع العامة لا تسقط بإسقاط أحد' states that general legal rights (like the public right to punish for deterrence) are not dropped when an individual relinquishes his private right. Use this to deduce: the heir's pardon drops only his private claim to qisas, but the ruler retains the authority to impose a discretionary ta'zir punishment.
3
Confirm with evidence from the text
The passage cites the scenario where the slain has no heir: the ruler steps in to exact qisas and cannot pardon, proving a public right exists. Also analogizes to hudud (e.g., theft) where victim's pardon does not drop the hadd, because hadd is a public right.
Reference: Radd al-Muhtar 5/346 and Kashf al-Asrar 4/161.
4
Refute the opposing view
Some early ijtihadat held the pardon drops all punishment absolutely. The author argues this is erroneous because it ignores the public right. Distinguish between a single ijtihad and the definitive theory of the law, which is supported by mainstream Hanafi and Maliki positions.
The opposing view does not represent the correct theory.
5
Formulate the fatwa hierarchy
In fatwa practice, one must identify the mufta bihi ruling: the heir's pardon drops qisas but the ruler may impose ta'zir at his discretion. This is from the zahir al-riwayah (Hanafi books) and supported by later fatwa authorities like Ibn Abidin.
The ta'zir is discretionary; however, some specify flogging and imprisonment (see lesson 2).

Source Text

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
ومن ثم كان القصاص (أي العقاب النوعي) يتوقف الحكم به وإنفاذه على الادعاء والطلب من صاحب الحق الخاص فإذا عفا القصاص سقط، لأن هذا النوع من العقوبة هو حقه فيبقى الحق العام الشرعي للسلطة الحاكمة في عقاب الجاني بما تراه كافياً للردع . فتصبح عقوبة الجناية عقوبة تفويضية غير مقدرة، فتدخل عندئذ في حظيرة التعزير الآتي بيانه.
فإذا لم يكن للقتيل وارث كان حق القصاص إلى الحاكم يستوفيه باسم العامة وليس له العفو لأنه ضرر بهم (ر: رد المحتار ٥/٣٤٦).
وبذلك تكون الشريعة الإسلامية قد انتهجت نهجاً وسطاً في أمر القصاص الذي اختلفت فيه الشرائع تشدداً وتساهلاً لما له من خطر.
على أنَّ الاجتهاداتِ في هذه الناحية مختلفة، فقد ذهب بعضها إلى أن عفو ولي الدم مسقط للعقوبة مطلقاً. وعلى هذا يكون الحق العام للشرع في عقوبة القاتل تابعاً للحق الخاص فيها .
وهذا ما أوهم أناساً اليوم أن هذه الشريعة العظمى قد قصرت عن إدراك فكرة الحق العام في عقوبة القتل ! .
وهذا خطأ كبير فإن أحد الاجتهادات المختلفة لا يمثل حتماً نظرية الشريعة، إذ لا يتعين به الصواب في فهم نصوصها، وإنما يمثل رأي صاحبه في الناحية المختلف فيها.
والصواب في هذه القضية ما عليه الاجتهادات الأخرى مما أوضحناه آنفاً: أن حق ولي الدم إنما هو في نوع العقوبة. أما أصلها فحق الشرع لا يسقطه عفو المجني عليه أو وليه لأن سقوط نوع العقوبة لا يستلزم سقوط أصلها، كما تقدَّم.
هذا ما تشهد له قواعد الشريعة ومقاصدها العامة التي يجب تنزيل النصوص الخاصة على منازلها منها. ولذا لو لم يكن للقتيل وارث كان حق القصاص إلى الحاكم، وليس لهذا الحاكم حق العفو كما نقلناه آنفاً.
وإن نصوص الفقهاء صريحة مستفيضة في أن القصاص يشتمل على حق خاص للشخص، وحق عام للشرع (ر: كشف الأسرار شرح أصول البزدوي ١٦١/٤).
ومن المقررات البديهية في فقه الشريعة أن حقوق الشرع العامة لا تسقط بإسقاط أحد. ولذا لا يسقط حد السرقة مثلاً بعفو المسروق منه، وكذا غيره من الحدود، لأنها حق عام.
وسنرى قريباً أن العقوبة التعزيرية على الضرب والشتم ونحوهما إذا عفا فيها المعتدى عليه لا يكون عفوه في نظر الفقهاء مسقطاً لحق السلطان في عقاب الفاعل بما يكفي للتأديب والزجر باسم المصلحة العامة (ر: ف ٥/٥٠) ففي جناية القتل يكون ذلك بطريق الأولوية.
ثم إنَّ الاجتهادات التي اتجهت هذا الاتجاه السديد قد اختلفت بعد ذلك في تحديد عقوبة القاتل أو تفويضها إذا عفا عنه ولي القصاص. فذهب مالك والليث بن سعد وأهل المدينة إلى أنه يعاقب بعد العفو عنه بالجلد والسجن. وحجتهم في هذا التحديد أنه المروي عن عمر بن الخطاب .
وذهب غيرهم إلى أن عقوبته بعد العفو مفوّضة للإمام فيما يراه كافياً من العقوبات.
Chapter Seven: The Theory of Legal Confirmations in Fiqh
Hence, retaliation (i.e., specific punishment) its ruling and execution depend on the claim and request from the holder of the private right. So if he forgives, the retaliation drops, because this type of punishment is his right. Then the general legal right of the ruling authority remains to punish the offender with what it deems sufficient for deterrence. Thus the punishment for the crime becomes a discretionary, non-specific punishment, and thereby enters the realm of ta'zir which will be explained.
If the slain has no heir, the right of retaliation goes to the ruler, who executes it on behalf of the public, and he has no right to pardon because that would harm them (See: Radd al-Muhtar 5/346).
Thus Islamic law has adopted a middle path regarding retaliation, concerning which other legal systems have differed in strictness and leniency due to its gravity.
However, ijtihadat in this regard differ. Some held that the pardon of the heir drops the punishment absolutely. According to this, the general right of the law in punishing the killer is subservient to the private right in it.
This has misled some today into thinking that this great law fell short of grasping the idea of public right in the punishment for murder!
This is a grave error, for one among different ijtihadat does not necessarily represent the theory of the law, since correctness in understanding its texts is not determined by it. Rather, it represents the opinion of its holder in the matter of disagreement.
The correct view in this issue is what the other ijtihadat hold, as we explained above: that the right of the heir is only over the type of punishment. As for its origin, it is a right of the law which is not dropped by the pardon of the victim or his heir, because dropping the type of punishment does not necessitate dropping its origin, as previously stated.
This is witnessed by the principles and general objectives of the law, to which particular texts must be related. Hence, if the slain has no heir, the right of retaliation goes to the ruler, and this ruler has no right to pardon, as we quoted above.
And the texts of the jurists are explicit and widespread that retaliation comprises a private right for the individual and a public right for the law (See: Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul al-Bazdawi 4/161).
It is among the established axioms in the fiqh of the law that general rights of the law are not dropped by anyone's relinquishment. Hence, the hadd punishment for theft, for example, is not dropped by the pardon of the victim, and similarly other hadd punishments, because they are a public right.
We will soon see that the ta'zir punishment for hitting, insulting, and the like, if the victim pardons, his pardon in the view of the jurists does not drop the authority's right to punish the perpetrator sufficiently for discipline and deterrence in the name of public interest (See: F 5/50). So in the case of homicide, it is even more so.
Then, the ijtihadat that took this sound direction have differed afterwards in specifying the punishment of the killer or leaving it discretionary if the heir of retaliation pardons him. Malik, al-Layth ibn Sa'd, and the people of Medina held that after pardon he is punished with flogging and imprisonment. Their evidence for this specification is that it is reported from Umar ibn al-Khattab.
Others held that his punishment after pardon is left to the discretion of the imam regarding what he deems sufficient among punishments.
Source pages p. 648

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy exposition on the nature of retaliation (qisas) and the interaction between private and public rights in Islamic criminal law. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that retaliation as a specific punishment (i.e., qisas or diyah) is contingent upon the claim of the private right-holder (the victim or his heir). If the heir forgives, the specific punishment drops, but the public right of the state to punish via ta'zir (discretionary punishment) remains. This is based on the principle that while the private right extends to the type of punishment, the underlying right to punish for the public good is a right of God (haqq Allah) and cannot be annulled by individuals.

The author refutes the misconception that some ijtihadat imply no public right in qisas. He clarifies that those ijtihadat do not represent the definitive theory of Shari'ah; rather, the correct position is that the public right remains. Evidence is drawn from the case where the victim has no heir—here the ruler steps in and cannot pardon, indicating a public right. Also, qisas is likened to hudud (fixed punishments) like theft, which are not dropped by the victim's pardon.

Finally, the passage notes that even among those affirming the public right, there is a further disagreement: some (including Imam Malik) specify flogging and imprisonment after pardon, while others leave the punishment to the imam's discretion. This reflects different approaches to implementing the public right.

Throughout, the author emphasizes that Shari'ah balances private and public interests, avoiding extremes of harshness or leniency. The discussion is rooted in Hanafi fiqh as seen in references to Radd al-Muhtar and Kashf al-Asrar.

Key Terms
القصاص (al-qisas): A fixed, specific punishment (retaliation in kind or diyah) that is a private right of the victim or heir but also involves a public right.
الحق العام (al-haqq al-'amm): The right of the community or the state (representing Shari'ah) to punish for public interest, which cannot be annulled by individuals.
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment administered by the ruler for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd or qisas, aimed at deterrence and reform.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view presented in this passage, what remains after the heir pardons the killer in a qisas case?
A) No punishment at all
B) The ruler's discretionary right to punish (ta'zir)
C) Only diyah (blood money) is due
D) The killer is executed anyway

Key Takeaway
General rights of the law (like the public interest in punishing crime) cannot be annulled by an individual's forgiveness; the ruler retains ta'zir authority even when qisas is pardoned.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
advanced
6 min
Implementing the Public Right: Discretion vs Specified Ta'zir After Pardon
Examining the post-pardon ta'zir ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two major opinions on the ta'zir punishment after pardon in qisas, and evaluate which view is stronger in Hanafi fatwa practice.

Core Idea
After private pardon, some schools specify the ta'zir; others leave it to the ruler's discretion.

After establishing that the public right remains, classical jurists differed on the exact punishment: should it be a fixed ta'zir (flogging and imprisonment) as reported from Umar, or wholly discretionary? This lesson unpacks that ikhtilaf, showing how even within the same framework, further detail matters for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the point of ikhtilaf
All those who affirm the public right agree that the ruler must punish, but they disagree on whether the punishment is predetermined (specified) or left to the ruler's discretion. The passage mentions two groups: Malik and others specify flogging and imprisonment; others leave it to the imam's judgment.
The disagreement is about the implementation of the public right, not its existence.
2
State each position and its evidence
Group A (Malik, al-Layth, Madinans): After pardon, the killer is punished with flogging and imprisonment. Their evidence: it is reported from Umar ibn al-Khattab. Group B (others, including the predominant Hanafi view): The punishment is left to the ruler's discretion based on what suffices for deterrence.
The passage does not name the second group explicitly, but the author seems to side with discretion (since earlier description of ta'zir is 'discretionary').
3
Distinguish between the two in terms of authority
The specified view relies on a specific practice (athar) from Umar. The discretionary view relies on the general principle that ta'zir is not fixed and that the ruler knows best what deters.
In Hanafi fiqh, ta'zir is primarily discretionary; thus the Hanafi school leans toward discretion.
4
Apply the tarjih mechanism
In fatwa, the mufti must decide which view is mufta bihi. The Hanafi school, as reflected in the passage's references to Radd al-Muhtar and the general theory, prefers discretion. However, the specified view of Malik is respected and may be followed if the ruler deems it appropriate.
The passage cites 'F 5/50' likely from al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya, supporting discretion.
5
Formulate the fatwa
The operative ruling: after pardon, the ruler may choose any ta'zir punishment that serves deterrence, and is not bound to flogging and imprisonment, though those are among the options.
This gives the ruler flexibility while ensuring the public right is not ignored.

Key Terms
التعزير المفوض (al-ta'zir al-mufawwad): Discretionary punishment left to the ruler's judgment, as opposed to a fixed punishment (muqaddar).
الأثر (al-athar): A narrative report from a Companion (Sahabi) regarding a practice or ruling, which may be used as evidence in ijtihad.

What is the primary evidence cited by Malik and others for the specified punishment of flogging and imprisonment after pardon in qisas?

The Dual Nature of Qisas: Private and Public Rights
Legal philosophical analysis of qisas as dual right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the distinction between haqq al-'abd and haqq Allah in qisas and apply the principle that public rights are not waived by individual pardon.

Core Idea
Qisas combines private and public rights; pardon drops the private but not the public.

This passage analyzes the legal nature of qisas (retaliation) in Islamic law, explaining that it involves both a private right for the victim's family and a public right for society. Understanding this dual nature is essential for a mufti when advising on cases of pardon and subsequent state punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of qisas
The passage states that qisas includes both a private right (حق العبد) and a public right (حق الله). Recognize that the private right belongs to the victim's heirs, the public right to the community.
2
Understand the effect of pardon on the private right
Pardon by the victim's guardian drops the private right to demand qisas, but does not affect the public right.
3
Apply the general rule: public rights cannot be waived
The passage cites an axiom: public rights do not fall by the waiver of anyone. This means that even after pardon, the state retains the right to punish for public interest.
4
Note the analogy with hadd and ta'zir
The passage compares qisas to hadd penalties (e.g., theft) and ta'zir, showing consistent application of the rule.
