الفصل (٤٩) المؤيدات التأديبية : العقوبات المقدرة يسقطه عفو المجني عليه أو وليه لأن سقوط نوع العقوبة لا يستلزم سقوط أصلها، كما تقدَّم (ف ٨/٤٩). هذا ما تشهد له قواعد الشريعة ومقاصدها العامة التي يجب تنزيل النصوص الخاصة على منازلها منها. ولذا لو لم يكن للقتيل وارث كان حق القصاص إلى الحاكم، وليس لهذا الحاكم حق العفو كما نقلناه آنفاً. وإن نصوص الفقهاء صريحة مستفيضة في أن القصاص يشتمل على حق خاص للشخص، وحق عام للشرع (ر: كشف الأسرار شرح أصول البزدوي ١٦١/٤). ومن المقررات البديهية في فقه الشريعة أن حقوق الشرع العامة لا تسقط بإسقاط أحد. ولذا لا يسقط حد السرقة مثلاً بعفو المسروق منه، وكذا غيره من الحدود، لأنها حق عام. وسنرى قريباً أن العقوبة التعزيرية على الضرب والشتم ونحوهما إذا عفا فيها المعتدى عليه لا يكون عفوه في نظر الفقهاء مسقطاً لحق السلطان في عقاب الفاعل بما يكفي للتأديب والزجر باسم المصلحة العامة (ر: ف (٥/٥٠) ففي جناية القتل يكون ذلك بطريق الأولوية. ثم إنَّ الاجتهادات التي اتجهت هذا الاتجاه السديد قد اختلفت بعد ذلك في تحديد عقوبة القاتل أو تفويضها إذا عفا عنه ولي القصاص. فذهب مالك والليث بن سعد وأهل المدينة إلى أنه يعاقب بعد العفو عنه بالجلد والسجن. وحجتهم في هذا التحديد أنه المروي عن عمر بن الخطاب . وذهب غيرهم إلى أن عقوبته بعد العفو مفوّضة للإمام فيما يراه كافياً من العقوبات. ٦٨٧

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه (ر) بداية المجتهد آخر بحث موجب القصاص ۳۳۸/۲ والمغني لابن قدامة الحنبلي ٤٦٨/٩). وهذا الرأي الأخير هو مقتضى قواعد المذهب الحنفي (1) . : (1) يقول الأستاذ الكبير الشيخ عبد الوهاب الخلاف أستاذ الشريعة الإسلامية في كلية : الحقوق بالقاهرة - رحمه الله تعالى - في محاضرة له عنوانها (الشريعة الإسلامية مصدر صالح للتشريع الحديث ما نصه : هذا هي النظرية الشرعية في هذا - أي حق ولي القتيل في العفو عن القصاص - مبنية على أن جريمة القتل وقعت على المجني عليه وذويه وعلى الهيئة الاجتماعية وقوانينها. ولهذا كان عقاب القاتل حقاً لهما. وهذا معنى قول الأصوليين القصاص فيه حق العبد وحق الله. وبناء على هذا إذا ثبت القتل العمد العدوان وطلب ولي المقتول القصاص من القاتل وجب القصاص منه . وأما إذا عفا سقط حقه في القصاص، ولكن لم يسقط حق الحكومة في عقاب القاتل بما تراه ورأيي أن النظرية الإسلامية في النظرية الفطرية التي تؤيدها غرائز الناس لأن المجني عليه وأولياءه وقعت عليهم الجريمة مباشرة، فليس من العدل إهمال جانبهم بل العدل مراعاة شعورهم فإن طلبوا القصاص كان فيه شفاء ،صدورهم، وإطفاء نار حقدهم. وإن طلبوا العفو كان طلبهم جديراً بالنظر والتقدير لأنه لا بد من ضرورة دعتهم إليه . وإسقاطهم حقهم لا يمنع الحكومة من استيفاء حقها بعقاب القاتل انتقاماً للمجتمع ونظمه . · • العدل. وأما النظرية التي بُنيت على أن الجريمة وقعت علي المجتمع وحده، ولا نظر إلى شخص المجني عليه، فهي نظرية خيالية بعيدة عما فُطِرَت عليه النفوس .. ومن أن نراعي جانبي أولياء المقتول والحكومة التي تمثلها النيابة العمومية. وفي هذا توفيق النظريتين» اهـ. (انظر المحاضرة في مجلة القانون والاقتصاد المصرية السنة العاشرة العدد (٣) . هذا ولينظر في تفصيل مباحث القصاص من المؤلفات المصرية المستوعبة كتاب القصاص في الشريعة الإسلامية للدكتور أحمد محمد إبراهيم. وهو رسالة قيمة نال بها شهادة الدكتوراه من كلية الحقوق بالقاهرة، وكتاب القصاص في فقه القرآن والسنة للأستاذ الجليل الشيخ محمود شلتوت عضو جماعة كبار العلماء بمضر ثم. شيخ الأزهر سابقاً. ٦٨٨
Chapter (49) Disciplinary Sanctions: Prescribed Punishments. It is dropped by the pardon of the victim or his guardian because the dropping of the type of punishment does not necessitate the dropping of its origin, as previously stated (F 8/49). This is attested to by the general rules and objectives of Sharia, by which particular texts must be interpreted in their proper context. Hence, if the slain has no heir, the right of qisas goes to the ruler, and this ruler does not have the right to pardon, as we have transmitted earlier. The texts of the jurists are explicit and numerous that qisas includes a private right for the individual and a public right for the law (see: Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul al-Bazdawi 4/161). It is an axiomatic principle in fiqh that public rights of the law do not fall by the waiver of anyone. Therefore, the hadd of theft, for example, does not fall by the pardon of the victim, and likewise other hadd punishments, because they are a public right. We will soon see that the ta'zir punishment for beating, insulting, and the like, if the victim pardons, his pardon in the view of the jurists does not nullify the right of the authority to punish the perpetrator sufficiently for discipline and deterrence in the name of public interest (see: F 5/50). So in the crime of killing, this is even more applicable by priority. Then the ijtihads that took this sound direction differed afterwards in determining the punishment of the killer or leaving it discretionary if the guardian of qisas pardons him. Malik, al-Layth ibn Sa'd, and the people of Medina held that he is punished after pardon with flogging and imprisonment. Their proof is that this is narrated from 'Umar ibn al-Khattab. Others held that his punishment after pardon is left to the imam to decide what punishment he deems sufficient. 687 Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Sanctions in Fiqh (See: Bidayat al-Mujtahid, last section on cause of qisas, 2/338; and al-Mughni by Ibn Qudamah al-Hanbali, 9/468). This last view is what is required by the principles of the Hanafi madhhab (1). (1) The great professor, Shaykh 'Abd al-Wahhab al-Khallaf, professor of Islamic law at the Faculty of Law in Cairo - may Allah have mercy on him - says in a lecture entitled 'Islamic Law as a Suitable Source for Modern Legislation', verbatim: 'The legal theory in this - i.e., the right of the slain's guardian to pardon qisas - is based on the fact that the crime of killing occurred against the victim and his family and against the social body and its laws. Hence, punishing the killer was a right for both. This is the meaning of the usulis' statement: Qisas involves the right of the servant and the right of Allah. Based on this, if intentional transgressive killing is proven and the guardian of the slain demands qisas from the killer, qisas becomes obligatory. But if he pardons, his right to qisas falls, but the government's right to punish the killer as it sees fit does not fall. My view is that the Islamic theory is the natural theory supported by human instincts, because the victim and his guardians are directly affected by the crime; it is not just to neglect their side. Rather, justice requires consideration of their feelings: if they demand qisas, it contains healing for their hearts and extinguishing the fire of their hatred; if they seek pardon, their request deserves consideration and appreciation because there must be a necessity driving them to it. Their dropping of their right does not prevent the government from exacting its right by punishing the killer in retribution for society and its systems. ... Justice. As for the theory built on the idea that the crime occurred against society alone, with no regard to the person of the victim, it is an imaginary theory far from what souls are naturally disposed to ... and we should consider both sides: the guardians of the slain and the government represented by the public prosecution. In this is a reconciliation of the two theories.' End. (See the lecture in the Egyptian Journal of Law and Economics, year 10, issue 3). For detail on qisas topics, refer to comprehensive Egyptian works: Kitab al-Qisas fi al-Shari'ah al-Islamiyyah by Dr. Ahmad Muhammad Ibrahim, a valuable thesis for which he earned a doctorate from the Faculty of Law in Cairo; and Kitab al-Qisas fi Fiqh al-Qur'an wa al-Sunnah by the venerable professor Shaykh Mahmud Shaltut, member of the body of senior scholars in Egypt and former Shaykh al-Azhar. 688
Source pages p. 649

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophical analysis of the nature of qisas (retaliation) and the effect of pardon by the victim's guardian. It begins by establishing a key rule: the pardon of the victim or his guardian regarding qisas does not drop the entire punishment; it only drops the type (i.e., the private right to exact qisas), but the public right remains. This is based on the general maxim that public rights (حقوق الله) are not waived by individuals. The author cites that qisas combines a private right (حق العبد) and a public right (حق الله). The proof is that if the slain has no heir, the right of qisas goes to the ruler (the public authority), but the ruler may not pardon—this shows that the public aspect is paramount. This principle is analogous to hadd punishments: theft is not dropped by the victim's pardon because it is a public right. Similarly, in ta'zir crimes like assault or slander, the pardon of the victim does not nullify the ruler's right to punish for public deterrence. By priority (اولوية), the same applies to killing. The text then presents a historical ikhtilaf among jurists: Imam Malik and others held that after pardon, the killer must be punished with a specific set punishment (flogging and imprisonment) based on a narration from Umar ibn al-Khattab. The majority (including the Hanafis) held that the punishment is discretionary, left to the imam to determine based on circumstances. The passage concludes that the Hanafi position (discretionary punishment after pardon) is consistent with Hanafi principles. The long footnote by Shaykh Abd al-Wahhab al-Khallaf expands on the theoretical basis, arguing that Islamic law balances the private rights of the victim's family and the public right of society, reconciling both interests.

Key Terms
حق العبد (haqq al-'abd): The private right of the individual (the victim or his heirs) to demand qisas or pardon.
حق الله (haqq Allah): The public right that belongs to the community and the Sharia, which cannot be waived by individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes the effect of pardon on qisas?
A) It drops all punishment entirely.
B) It drops the private right but the public right remains.
C) It drops the public right but the private right remains.
D) It has no effect on any right.

Key Takeaway
Pardon in qisas only drops the private right; the state retains the public right to punish for deterrence.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Post-Pardon Punishment: Hanafi-Maliki Dispute on Discretion vs. Fixed Penalty
Madhhab dispute on the specific punishment after pardon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Maliki positions on post-pardon punishment in qisas, and evaluate the tarjih in favor of the Hanafi view.

Core Idea
After pardon, Hanafis leave punishment to the ruler's discretion; Malikis impose fixed flogging and imprisonment.

After establishing that the public right remains after pardon, the passage presents a specific ikhtilaf between the Hanafi and Maliki schools regarding what punishment the state may impose. This lesson unpacks that dispute and its practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the point of disagreement
After the guardian pardons qisas, what punishment can the state impose? Fixed (Maliki) or discretionary (Hanafi)?
2
Examine the Maliki proof
They cite the practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab, who ordered flogging and imprisonment.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis argue that the narration is a specific case, not a general rule; the imam's discretion is more aligned with maqasid.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, Hanafi law gives flexibility to the ruler, while Maliki law imposes a fixed penalty.
5
Note the tarjih within the Hanafi school
The passage states that the discretionary view is required by Hanafi principles.

حق الله (haqq Allah): The public right that belongs to the community and the Sharia, which cannot be waived by individuals.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which school held that a killer after pardon must be punished with flogging and imprisonment?
A) Hanafi

C) Shafi'i
D) Hanbali

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school leaves post-pardon punishment to the imam's discretion, while the Maliki school prescribes a fixed penalty based on Umar's precedent.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

The Definition and Scope of Ta'zir
Definition, philosophy, and types of ta'zir in Hanafi law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ta'zir, identify its range of punishments, and evaluate the Hanafi position on financial penalties.

Core Idea
Ta'zir is the discretionary punishment that allows Islamic law to adapt to new crimes and changing circumstances.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm introduces ta'zir, the third pillar of Islamic criminal law. Unlike fixed hudud and qisas, ta'zir is delegated to the ruler's discretion. Understanding ta'zir is essential for any mufti because it is the tool that enables Islamic law to respond to modern criminality while remaining rooted in Shari'a principles.

Guided Path
1
Define ta'zir linguistically and technically
Identify the lexical meaning (prevention and discipline) and the juristic definition: punishment of a criminal by a penalty legally entrusted to the ruler's discretion in type and amount, excluding hudud and qisas.
The term 'ta'zir' literally means prevention and discipline.
2
Identify the legal reasoning (talil) behind delegation
Why did Islamic law fix hudud but leave ta'zir to discretion? Because crimes are unlimited, new crimes emerge, and even known crimes may require new methods. The ruler tailors punishment to the person, time, place, and social context.
The principle: 'Crimes are not limited' (al-jara'im la tanhasir).
3
List the range of ta'zir punishments
From the lowest (a stern look or verbal reprimand) to the highest (execution), including flogging, imprisonment, exile, and financial penalty.
The specific list in the passage: flogging, imprisonment, exile, financial penalty, and execution.
4
Analyze the dispute on financial penalties
Some earlier jurists prohibited financial ta'zir due to fear of tyranny. Abu Yusuf allowed it, citing a hadith about taking half the property of a zakat evader. The author al-Zarqa argues that today the danger is removed because fines go to the public treasury and are subject to accountability.
This is a classic example of taghayyur al-fatwa bi-taghayyur al-zaman (change of fatwa with change of time).
5
Apply to a modern fatwa scenario
A mufti today can rule that financial penalties are permissible as ta'zir, provided they are legally regulated and used for public benefit.
Consistent with Abu Yusuf's view and the later tarjih.

الفصل الخمسون
المؤيدات التأديبية : العقوبات المفوضة، أو التعزير ١/٥٠ - التعزير في أصل اللغة: المنع والتأديب(۱)
وفي الاصطلاح الفقهي هو : معاقبة المجرم بعقاب مفوّض شرعاً إلى رأي ولي الأمر نوعاً ومقداراً.
وذلك في جميع أنواع الجرائم والأعمال الممنوعة التي تستوجب
الزجر والتأديب غير موجبات الحدود والقصاص.
والنظر الشرعي في ذلك أن الجرائم لا تنحصر، وقد يجد منها أنواع لم تكن معهودة من قبل والجريمة الواحدة المعهودة قد تحدث لها صور وأساليب تستدعي تدابير أخرى في قمعها. فلذلك تناول الشرع بالتخصيص تلك الجرائم الشائعة ما أشد إخلالاً بنظام الإسلام الاجتماعي الأساسي، وهي موجبات الحدود فقدر عقوبتها وترك ما وراءها مفوضاً ترتيبه وتقديره إلى أولياء الأمر العام، يقدرونه في كل جريمة بحسبها، تبعاً لاختلاف الأزمنة والأمكنة والأشخاص ودرجة التهذيب الاجتماعي.
فمن الناس من ينزجر بالتنبيه والزجر اليسير ومنهم من لا يكفيه ويكفه إلا العقاب الكثير . ومن ثم كان للحاكم أن يعاقب على الفعل الواحد بعقوبات متفاوتة بحسب تفاوت الأشخاص ودرجة تأثرهم بالعقاب (ر: فتح القدير، وشرح الكنز للزيلعي باب التعزير والدر المختار ورد المحتار ٣

-
٢/٥٠ ـ ويكون التعزير بكل ما يراه الحكام مناسباً من أنواع العقوبات المشروعة وهي التي فيها تأديب بلا تعذيب، من جلد وحبس ونفي وتغريم مالي وهو المسمى اليوم : الجزاء النقدي، أو الغرامة المالية ويمكن أن يبلغ التعزير الإعدام.
فقد صرّح فقهاء الحنفية بأن أدنى التعزير نظرة شزر من الحاكم إلى المذنب أو أن يقول له: بلغني عنك كذا إذا كان الذنب هفوة صغيرة والمذنب من ذوي الشرف والمروءات الذين يكفيهم للردع هذا القدر، وأن أعلى عقوبة التعزير هو الإعدام إذا كان الجرم خطيراً وتحتاج سياسة قمعه بين الناس إلى هذه الشدة، كما في جرم المتاجرة بتهريب المخدرات اليوم.

أقول :
لا شك اليوم أنه بعدما أصبحت عقوبة التغريم المالي يسجل الحكم بها ويخضع تنفيذها للمحاسبة، وتدخل الخزينة العامة رأساً، لم يبق فيها ذلك المحذور.
Chapter Fifty: Disciplinary Sanctions: Discretionary Punishments, or Ta'zir 1/50 - Ta'zir in the original language means prevention and discipline. In juristic terminology, it is: punishing the criminal with a punishment that is legally entrusted to the opinion of the ruler in type and amount. This applies to all types of crimes and prohibited actions that require deterrence and discipline, except those necessitating prescribed punishments (hudud) and retaliation (qisas). The legal reasoning behind this is that crimes are not limited, and there may arise types that were not previously known, and a single known crime may take on forms and methods that require other measures for its suppression. Therefore, the Law specified those common crimes that most severely disrupt the basic social system of Islam, which are those necessitating hudud, and set their punishments, and left what is beyond them entrusted to the general rulers to arrange and determine for each crime according to its circumstances, following the differences in times, places, individuals, and the level of social refinement. For among people, some are deterred by a simple warning and reprimand, while for others nothing less than severe punishment suffices and deters them. Hence, the ruler may punish a single act with varying punishments according to the differences in persons and their degree of being affected by punishment. 2/50 - Ta'zir can be carried out by whatever the rulers deem appropriate from among the types of legitimate punishments that involve discipline without torture, such as flogging, imprisonment, exile, and financial penalty, which today is called monetary penalty or fine, and ta'zir can reach execution. The Hanafi jurists explicitly stated that the lowest ta'zir is a stern look from the ruler at the offender or saying to him: 'I have heard such-and-such about you,' if the sin is a minor slip and the offender is of honorable and chivalrous character for whom this degree suffices as deterrence, and that the highest punishment of ta'zir is execution if the crime is serious and the policy of suppressing it among the people requires such severity, as in the crime of trafficking in drug smuggling today. (1) The jurists differed in the past regarding the permissibility of ta'zir by taking money, i.e., monetary penalty, fearing that tyrannical rulers would use it to seize people's wealth in the name of punishment and then consume it. Judge Abu Yusuf held its permissibility. It has been reported by Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Qurashi, known as Ibn al-Ukhuwwa, from the Shafi'i jurists, that Imam al-Shafi'i obligated specific financial fines as punishment for committing some wrongs. He argued with what was narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding the zakat of camels: 'For every forty grazing camels, a bint labun... Whoever gives it seeking reward shall have his reward, and whoever withholds it, we will take it and half of his wealth, as a determined obligation from the obligations of our Lord, Blessed and Exalted; nothing of it belongs to Muhammad or the family of Muhammad.' I say: There is no doubt today that after the financial penalty is recorded by judgment and its execution is subject to accountability, and it goes directly to the public treasury, that danger no longer exists.
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of ta'zir, the third category of punishment in Islamic law alongside hudud and qisas. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, first gives the linguistic meaning (prevention and discipline) and then the technical definition: a punishment delegated to the ruler's discretion. The key principle is that crimes are not limited to those specified by texts (hudud and qisas); new crimes can emerge, and even established crimes can evolve in form. Therefore, Islamic law deliberately fixed only the most severe offenses and left the rest to the discretion of those in authority, to be determined according to time, place, and circumstance. This flexibility accounts for differences in human temperament and social refinement. The passage then describes the range of ta'zir: from a mere stern look or a verbal reprimand (for minor offenses and honorable persons) all the way to execution (for grave crimes like drug trafficking). This is firmly established in the Hanafi school. A significant legal debate is then presented: the permissibility of financial penalties (gharamah maliyyah). Some classical jurists opposed it due to the risk of tyranny. However, the Hanafi scholar Abu Yusuf permitted it. The author cites a hadith about taking half of the property of a person who withholds zakat as a precedent for financial punishment. Finally, al-Zarqa gives his own view, arguing that the danger is now eliminated because fines are recorded, subject to accountability, and go to the public treasury. This reflects the principle that legal rulings can change with changing circumstances, a core element of Hanafi methodology.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zīr): معاقبة المجرم بعقاب مفوّض شرعاً إلى رأي ولي الأمر نوعاً ومقداراً، في غير موجبات الحدود والقصاص.
الحد (al-ḥadd): عقوبة مقدرة شرعاً على جرائم معينة كالزنا والسرقة.
تغريم مالي (taghrīm mālī): الجزاء النقدي أو الغرامة المالية التي تؤخذ من المذنب كعقوبة تعزيرية.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Imprisonment
C) Execution
D) A stern look

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is a flexible discretionary punishment that can adapt to new crimes, and financial penalties are permissible today under the Hanafi school.

Ta'zir: Definition, Scope, and the Dispute over Monetary Fines
Explains ta'zir punishment, its forms, and the dispute over monetary fines

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ta'zir, list its forms, explain the Hanafi position on monetary fines, and state the rule limiting ta'zir flogging.

Core Idea
Ta'zir is discretionary discipline, but flogging must be less than the minimum hadd; monetary fines are now accepted.

The Hanafi school provides a flexible framework for discretionary punishments (ta'zir) to maintain public order. This lesson unpacks the definition, permissible forms, and the critical boundary between ta'zir and fixed punishments. We also examine a historical dispute about monetary fines that shaped modern Hanafi practice.

Guided Path
1
Define ta'zir and list its forms
Identify ta'zir as a discretionary punishment that includes any lawful disciplinary measure, such as flogging, imprisonment, exile, or monetary fines.
The key is that it disciplines without torture.
2
Understand the scope: minimum and maximum
The minimum can be as light as a stern look; the maximum can be execution for serious crimes like drug trafficking.
This shows ta'zir's flexibility to match the crime and the offender's status.
3
Analyze the dispute over monetary fines
Some early jurists opposed fines fearing ruler abuse; Qadi Abu Yusuf permitted them. The Hanafi school now accepts fines due to modern accountability systems.
The hadith about zakat evaders (taking half the property) served as evidence for fines.
4
Apply the rule about flogging in ta'zir
Flogging in ta'zir must be less than the minimum hadd (40 lashes for slaves). This ensures ta'zir remains distinct from hudud.
This is a binding limit derived from prophetic guidance.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Shar'i Penalties in Fiqh
...and the explanation of Al-Kanz by Al-Zayla'i, chapter of Ta'zir, and Al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar 3 (179 - 2/50). And ta'zir is whatever the rulers deem appropriate from the types of lawful punishments, which are those that involve discipline without torture: flogging, imprisonment, exile, and monetary fine, which is today called pecuniary penalty or financial penalty, and ta'zir can reach execution.
(1) The Hanafi jurists have stated that the lowest ta'zir is a stern glance from the ruler at the offender or that he says to him: 'I have heard such and such about you' if the sin is a small slip and the offender is among people of honor and manliness who are deterred by this amount. And the highest ta'zir punishment is execution if the crime is serious and the policy of suppressing it among people requires such severity, as in the crime of drug trafficking today.
(1) The jurists differed in the past regarding the permissibility of ta'zir by taking property, i.e., pecuniary penalty, for fear that tyrannical rulers would dominate the people's wealth, take it in the name of punishment, and then consume it. Qadi Abu Yusuf held its permissibility (Radd al-Muhtar 3/178). Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Qurashi, known as Ibn al-Ukhuwwa, among the Shafi'i jurists transmitted that Imam al-Shafi'i obligated certain specific monetary fines as punishment for committing some wrongdoings. He used as evidence what was narrated from the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) regarding the zakat of camels: 'For every forty grazing camels, a bint labun... Whoever gives it seeking reward, he has his reward. And whoever withholds it, indeed we will take it and half of his wealth, as a determination from the determinations of our Lord, Mighty and Majestic. Neither Muhammad nor the family of Muhammad has any right to it.'
See the fiftieth chapter of the book 'Ma'alim al-Qurba fi Ahkam al-Hisba' by Ibn al-Ukhuwwa, which is the most extensive and important work we have seen on hisba. Its noble author arranged it into seventy chapters. The orientalist Ruben Levy published it, printed at the Dar al-Funun press in Cambridge, England, with a comprehensive introduction in English. The hadith was narrated by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and al-Nasa'i, and declared hasan by the muhaddith al-Albani in Sahih al-Jami' al-Saghir.
I say:
There is no doubt today that after the monetary fine penalty is recorded in the judgment and its execution is subject to accounting, and it goes directly to the public treasury, that objection no longer remains.

Chapter (50): Disciplinary Penalties: Delegated Punishments
And upon this the ijtihad of the Hanafis has settled (Radd al-Muhtar 3/179).
It was narrated from Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said:
'People will encounter judgments according to the amount of immorality they commit.' (1)
And Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah honor his face) introduced prisons for incarcerating criminals. 3/50 - The noble jurist, Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Mawardi al-Shafi'i transmitted the opinions of some mujtahids and followers of their schools regarding the determination of ta'zir punishments for some crimes and accusations according to the severity of the crime and the strength of the accusation, then he said regarding that:
'This classification, although apparently good, is devoid of a definitive evidentiary basis.' (R: Al-Ahkam al-Sultaniyya by al-Mawardi, chapter on Ta'zir).
The meaning is that the jurists' estimation of some of those ta'zir punishments they mentioned is only a way of illuminating the path for rulers and authorities, not a binding determination based on evidence from the origin of Sharia. Indeed, the right of estimation in ta'zir is legally returned to the rulers according to temporal interest.
However, the punishment of flogging specifically has a legal upper limit that cannot be exceeded. The jurists have stated: 'It is not permissible for flogging in ta'zir to reach the least of the hadd punishments (2), rather it must be less than it, even by one lash, due to the saying of the Messenger (peace be upon him)...
(1) R: Mu'in al-Hukkam by Shaykh Ala al-Din al-Tarabulsi al-Hanafi, the third section on adjudication by siyasa shar'iyya, p. 217. A similar statement is also narrated from Ziyad in his famous stunted speech.
(2) The least of the hadd punishments is forty lashes, because it is the hadd for qadhf and drinking for slaves; their punishment in these hadd punishments is half the punishment of free persons.
Source pages p. 652

Commentary
This passage is a legal exposition on the nature and scope of ta'zir (discretionary punishment) in Hanafi fiqh. It begins by stating that ta'zir comprises any lawful punishment the ruler deems appropriate, provided it disciplines without torture. Forms include flogging, imprisonment, exile, and monetary fines (today known as pecuniary penalties). The text then details the extremes: the minimum ta'zir is merely a stern look or a verbal rebuke for minor offences by honorable persons; the maximum is execution for serious crimes like drug trafficking. A significant historical dispute is presented: some jurists opposed monetary fines due to fear of ruler abuse, while Qadi Abu Yusuf (Hanafi) permitted them. The Shafi'i scholar Ibn al-Ukhuwwa reports that Imam al-Shafi'i mandated certain fines, citing a hadith about taking half the property of a zakat evader. The author notes that today, with proper judicial recording and treasury deposit, this objection is obsolete. The passage then notes that Hanafi ijtihad has settled on this view. It cites historical precedents: Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz's statement that judgments evolve with corruption, and Ali's introduction of prisons. Al-Mawardi's opinion is quoted, emphasizing that jurists' suggested amounts for ta'zir are non-binding guidelines; the real authority lies with rulers based on public interest. Finally, a crucial limit is given: flogging in ta'zir must be less than the minimum hadd punishment (forty lashes for slaves), based on prophetic guidance. This ensures ta'zir remains distinct from fixed punishments.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment determined by the ruler, not fixed by sharia, used for crimes without specific hadd or kaffarah.
التغريم المالي (al-taghrim al-mali): Monetary fine imposed as a ta'zir punishment.
الحد (al-hadd): Fixed punishment prescribed by sharia, cannot be increased or decreased.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the maximum number of lashes allowed in ta'zir punishment according to the Hanafi school?
A) 40 lashes
B) 39 lashes
C) 80 lashes
D) No limit

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is flexible but not boundless: flogging cannot reach the minimum hadd, and monetary fines are now established as permissible due to modern safeguards.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Discretionary Punishments (Ta'zir) and Their Limits
Explains the limit on ta'zir flogging and the Maliki dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that ta'zir flogging must be less than the minimum hadd and explain the basis for this limit.

Core Idea
Ta'zir flogging cannot reach the minimum hadd; the Prophet warned against exceeding limits.

Ta'zir allows rulers flexibility in punishment, but the Hanafi school imposes one firm limit: flogging must stay below the minimum hadd. This lesson explores the evidence for that limit and the Maliki counterargument, so you can confidently apply this rule in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The passage states that ta'zir is discretionary, left to rulers. Jurists' guidelines are not binding. This is the starting point.
Distinguishes ta'zir from hadd.
2
Isolate the exception for flogging
Unlike other ta'zir penalties, flogging has a maximum: it must be less than the minimum hadd (40 lashes for slaves in qadhf/drinking).
Thus ta'zir flogging is at most 39 lashes.
3
Analyze the legal proof (dalil)
The hadith: 'Whoever reaches a hadd in other than a hadd is among the transgressors' is cited. Identify thubut (zanni, ahad) and dalala (qati forbidding reaching hadd).
The direct prohibition is the primary evidence.
4
Examine the Madhhab dispute
Malik allowed exceeding the hadd if needed, citing Umar's 100-lash punishment. The Hanafi response: the hadith overrides, and Umar's case may be a special siyasa.
Practical difference: Hanafi caps at 39; Maliki allows more.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and application
The Hanafi position is relied upon. The mufti must ensure flogging is below hadd; if more deterrent is needed, combine with prison or fines.
This is the operative rule for fatwa.

Chapter (50): Disciplinary Supports: Discretionary Punishments. The ijtihad of the Hanafis settled upon this. It is reported from Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz that he said: 'There will arise for people judgments according to the amount of immorality they commit.' Ali ibn Abi Talib invented prisons for detaining criminals. 3/50 - The noble jurist Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Mawardi al-Shafi'i transmitted the views of some mujtahids and their madhhab followers regarding setting the amounts of ta'zir punishments for some crimes and accusations according to the strength of the crime and the accusation, then said: 'This arrangement, though seemingly good, the istihsan in it is devoid of a textual basis to determine it.' This means that the jurists' estimation of some of those ta'zir punishments is only to light the way for rulers and authorities, not a binding determination based on a proof from the origin of Sharia, for the right of estimation in ta'zir legally reverts to the rulers according to temporal maslaha. However, the punishment of flogging specifically has a maximum limit that may not be exceeded. The jurists have stated: It is not permissible for flogging in ta'zir to reach the minimum hadd, but it must be less, even by one lash, due to the saying of the Prophet: 'Whoever reaches a hadd (limit) in other than a hadd is among the transgressors.' But it can be intensified by adding another punishment to flogging, such as imprisonment and fines. Imam Malik said: It is permissible to increase ta'zir beyond the hadd if the need for repression requires it, and he cited that Ma'n ibn Zaidah forged the seal of the treasury and took money, so Umar ibn al-Khattab struck Ma'n ibn Zaidah one hundred lashes. Then he was spoken to about him, so he struck him another hundred and imprisoned him. Then after that he was spoken to, so he struck him another hundred and exiled him. 4/50 - The criterion for what necessitates ta'zir. Since maslaha has determined that the ta'zir punishment is left delegated legally...
Source pages p. 653

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of discretionary punishments (ta'zir) in Hanafi fiqh. The author explains that, unlike fixed hadd punishments, ta'zir penalties are not predetermined by sacred texts but are delegated to the ruler or judge to determine according to circumstances and public interest (maslaha). The jurists may suggest guidelines, but these are not binding. The exception is that the number of lashes in ta'zir may not reach the minimum hadd punishment (which for flogging is 40 lashes for the hadd of qadhf and drinking for slaves; equivalently 80 for freemen). The hadith from the Prophet (PBUH) is cited: 'Whoever reaches a hadd in other than a hadd is among the transgressors.' This means that in ta'zir, one cannot flog as many as even the lowest hadd; it must be less. However, other punishments like imprisonment or fines may be added to compensate. The passage also presents the Maliki position, which allows exceeding the hadd if necessary for repression, using the incident of Umar (RA) with Ma'n ibn Zaidah. The Hanafi position, however, is that the maximum is the lower hadd minus one. The author also clarifies the identity of Ma'n ibn Zaidah, noting a possible confusion. The section ends with the heading for a criterion defining when ta'zir is applicable, indicating that ta'zir is left to the ruler's discretion for public interest.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd or kaffara, determined by the ruler or judge.
حد (hadd): Fixed punishment prescribed by Sharia for specific crimes (e.g., zina, qadhf, theft).
ضرب (darb): Flogging; a form of ta'zir punishment with a maximum limit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the maximum number of lashes allowed in ta'zir punishment for a slave?
A) 80 lashes
B) 40 lashes
C) 39 lashes
D) No limit

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir flogging must always be less than the minimum hadd, a rule derived from the Prophet's hadith and a defining limit of Hanafi criminal fiqh.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
The General Principle of Ta'zir: Discretionary Punishment in Hanafi Law
Defining ta'zir and its dual nature as private and public right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general principle (dabit) that triggers ta'zir, distinguish between ta'zir as a pure right of the Shari'ah and ta'zir involving a personal right, and apply the ruling on the effect of pardon.

Core Idea
Ta'zir is wajib upon the ruler for any munkar or harm; the ruler must still punish even after victim's pardon for public discipline.

Ta'zir is the discretionary punishment that fills gaps in Islamic penal law. Unlike fixed hadd penalties, ta'zir is flexible but not lawless. In this passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm, Mustafa al-Zarqa presents the jurists' general principle for when ta'zir becomes obligatory and explains how the ruler must balance the victim's right to retribution with the state's duty to maintain public order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle (Dabit) for Ta'zir
Locate the exact wording of the principle: 'Whoever commits an evil deed (munkar) or harms another without right, by word, action, or gesture, ta'zir is obligatory upon him.' This is the core trigger for ta'zir.
The principle covers both direct harm and violations of religious norms.
2
Enumerate the Categories of Offenses under the Principle
The text categorizes offenses into: (1) aggression against persons or property (e.g., beating, fraud), (2) violations of religious sanctities (e.g., public fasting, mocking religion), and (3) neglect of religious duties (e.g., teaching and learning).
Neglect of teaching and learning is explicitly included via the hadith.
3
Distinguish Between Pure Shari'ah Right and Combined Right
When the offense only violates religious sanctities (e.g., drinking alcohol privately), ta'zir is solely a right of the Shari'ah. When it involves harm to a person, ta'zir combines a private right of the victim and a public right of the state.
This distinction determines the effect of pardon.
4
Apply the Effect of Pardon
If the victim demands retribution, the ruler cannot pardon. If the victim pardons, his private right falls, but the ruler's duty to punish for public discipline remains. If the offense is purely a Shari'ah right, the ruler must punish regardless.
The state's corrective role is independent of private forgiveness.
5
Analyze the Hadith on Teaching as an Example
The hadith shows that neglecting the duty to teach and learn is a munkar deserving ta'zir. It illustrates how the general principle applies to communal obligations.
This example underscores the societal importance of knowledge.

٦٩٤
Chapter (50) Disciplinary Sanctions: Punishments delegated to the opinion of the rulers without fixed determination. It is necessary to determine the cause, i.e., to specify the criminal state for which a person deserves ta'zir. This is what the jurists did, as they set a general principle for what necessitates ta'zir, saying: The principle is that whoever commits an evil deed, or harms another without right, by word, action, or gesture, ta'zir is obligatory upon him. (Ref: al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar 3/182, 185). So, included under this general principle are all crimes against the system, whether they are aggression by some people against others in life or property, such as beating, insulting, intimidation, forgery, false testimony, fraud, deception and the like; or they are violations of religious sanctities and open commission of sins, such as breaking the fast of Ramadan, mocking religion, calling to heresy, violating public morals, and the like. Also included under committing evil is the neglect of religious duties, among which are teaching and learning. So if the scholar falls short in the duty of teaching, or the ignorant person falls short in learning the obligatory amount of knowledge, they deserve the punishment of ta'zir for the shortcoming. Indeed, the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Seeking knowledge is an obligation upon every Muslim.' (1) (1) Al-Hafiz al-Mundhiri mentioned in al-Targhib wa al-Tarhib, Kitab al-'Ilm 1/86, that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, gave a sermon one day and praised some groups of Muslims well, then said: 'What is the matter with people who do not make their neighbors understand, nor teach them, nor admonish them, nor enjoin them, nor forbid them? And what is the matter with people who do not learn from their neighbors, nor gain understanding, nor take admonition? By Allah, some people must teach their neighbors, make them understand, admonish them, enjoin them, and forbid them, and some people must learn from their neighbors, gain understanding, and take admonition, or I will hasten the punishment upon them in this world.' So the people said: 'Whom do you think he meant by these?' They said: 'He meant the Ash'aris; they are a people of jurists, and they have rough neighbors from the people of the waters and the Bedouins.' That reached the Ash'aris, so they came to the Messenger of Allah and said: 'O Messenger of Allah, you mentioned some people well and mentioned us with evil; what is our matter?' He repeated his warning to them. They said: 'O Messenger of Allah, must we make others understanding?' He repeated his statement to them, and they repeated their question, and he repeated his statement to them, then they said: 'O Messenger of Allah, give us a year's respite.' So he gave them a year to make them understand, teach them, and admonish them. Then the Messenger of Allah recited the saying of Allah Almighty in the Quran: [verse] 693. [Page 656] Section 7: Theory of Legal Sanctions in Fiqh. And the word 'Muslim' here includes men and women, because the ruling is attached to a common attribute, which is Islam. 5/50 - Nature of Ta'zir and the Effect of Pardon upon It: If the crime requiring ta'zir is of the type of pure violation of religious sanctities without aggression upon any person, then the punishment of ta'zir is purely a right of the Shari'ah. If it involves aggression upon a person by beating, insulting, or otherwise, then ta'zir involves two rights: a right of the person to seek retribution from the perpetrator, and a right of the Shari'ah in discipline and suppression. So whenever it involves a right of an aggrieved person and he demands retribution, the ruling authority may not pardon the punishment, because it does not have the right to drop the rights of people. If the aggrieved person pardons, or if the ta'zir is in a matter of purely Shari'ah rights, then the ruler must punish for discipline despite the pardon of the holder of the personal right. This is because the holder of the right, as the jurists say, only has the right to pardon his own right, while the authority's right in rectification and refinement remains. (Ref: al-Ahkam al-Sultaniyya by al-Mawardi, chapter on ta'zir). These are those who disbelieve from the Children of Israel were cursed by the tongue of David and Jesus son of Mary. That is because they disobeyed and used to transgress. They used not to forbid one another from the evil they did. Wretched was what they used to do. Narrated by al-Tabarani in al-Kabir from Bukayr ibn Ma'ruf from 'Alqama (r): al-Rasul al-Mu'allim by Sheikh 'Abd al-Fattah Abu Ghuddah, may Allah have mercy on him, pp. 15-17. And their statement 'anafatighun ghayrana' means: is it obligatory upon us to make others intelligent, i.e., to make them possess understanding, which is alertness and knowledge. I say: This great stance of considering negligence in teaching and learning a social crime deserving worldly punishment is a stance that history has not recorded a parallel in respecting knowledge before the Prophet, peace be upon him, nor after him. 694.
Source pages p. 655

Commentary
This passage from Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses the Islamic legal concept of ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The author first establishes that ta'zir is a punishment delegated to the ruler's discretion, but it is not arbitrary. The jurists set a general principle (dabit) that ta'zir is obligatory for anyone who commits a wrong (munkar) or harms another without right, whether by word, action, or gesture. This covers a wide range of offenses, from crimes against individuals (e.g., beating, fraud) to violations of religious norms (e.g., breaking the fast publicly) and even neglect of religious duties like teaching and learning. The passage then highlights the dual nature of ta'zir: when the offense is purely against God (e.g., drinking alcohol), it is solely a right of the Shari'ah; when it involves harm to a person, it combines a private right of the victim and a public right of the state. The effect of pardon is crucial: the ruler cannot pardon if the victim demands retribution, but if the victim pardons or the offense is purely against God, the ruler still has the duty to impose ta'zir for discipline. This reflects the Hanafi view that the state's corrective role remains even after private forgiveness. The passage uses a hadith about teaching neighbors to illustrate that neglect of educational duties is a punishable offense, emphasizing the societal responsibility for knowledge.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): A discretionary punishment imposed by the ruler for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd punishment, or for hadd offenses where the hadd cannot be applied due to procedural deficiencies.
منكر (munkar): Any act that is legally prohibited or disliked, including both sins and offenses against public order.
ضابط (dabit): A general legal principle or rule that defines the scope of a ruling. In this context, the dabit for ta'zir is: 'Whoever commits an evil deed or harms another without right is liable to ta'zir.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a crime requiring ta'zir involves both a personal right and a public right, and the victim pardons the perpetrator?
A) The ruler cannot punish because the victim has waived his right.
B) The ruler must still punish because the public right remains.
C) The ruler may pardon if he deems it appropriate.
D) The victim's pardon also drops the public right automatically.

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is obligatory for any evil act or harm; the ruler must punish for public discipline even if the victim pardons.

The Dual Nature of Ta'zir and the Ruler's Authority to Pardon
Expansion on ta'zir categories and pardon rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ta'zir as a pure public right and ta'zir involving a personal right, and apply the Hanafi rules on pardon in each case.

Core Idea
In ta'zir, the victim's pardon only waives his personal right; the ruler's public right to discipline remains, subject to maslahah.

This passage from al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm explores a subtle but crucial aspect of ta'zir: it is not a single uniform category. When a crime violates both a divine command and a personal right, the ruler's authority to pardon is restricted. The victim can waive his personal claim, but the state retains the right to punish for the public interest—a principle demonstrated by Caliph Ali (RA). Understanding this duality is essential for issuing sound fatwas on discretionary punishments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of ta'zir
From the passage, note the distinction: ta'zir for pure religious violations (public right only) vs. ta'zir involving aggression against a person (public + private right).
2
Analyze the effect of the victim's pardon
When a personal right exists, the victim's pardon removes only his claim; the ruler's duty to discipline remains. If no personal right, the ruler may pardon if maslahah is served.
3
Examine the historical proof
Review the incident of Ali (RA): the seller forgave the slap, but Ali still administered nine lashes as 'the right of the sultan'. This illustrates the inalienable public right.
4
Apply the principle to new cases
Given a hypothetical ta'zir offense (e.g., public slander), determine which category it falls under and whether the ruler may pardon after victim forgiveness.
5
Connect to modern legal parallels
The author notes that modern systems also allow executive pardon even after judgment, balancing public interest—similar to the Hanafi view of restricted ruler discretion.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Supports in Fiqh. The word Muslim here includes man and woman, because the ruling is attached to a shared quality which is Islam. 
5/50 - Description of Ta'zir, and the Effect of Pardon in it: If the crime necessitating ta'zir is of the type of violation of purely religious sanctities without aggression against any person, the punishment of ta'zir is a pure right of the Shari'ah. And if it involves aggression against a person by beating or insult or otherwise, then ta'zir is two rights: a right for the person to obtain redress from the perpetrator, and a right for the Shari'ah in discipline and suppression. So whenever it involves a right of an aggrieved person and he demands redress, it is not for the ruler (sultan) to pardon the punishment, because he does not have the right to drop the rights of people. And if the aggrieved person forgives, or if the ta'zir is in a matter of purely Shari'ah rights, then the ruler must punish for discipline despite the forgiveness of the person with the personal right. That is because the owner of the right, as the jurists say, only has the right to forgive his own right, and the right of authority in correction and refinement remains. (See: al-Ahkam al-Sultaniyya by al-Mawardi, chapter on Ta'zir). 
Al-Tabari narrated: that the Commander of the Faithful, Ali (may Allah honor his face), heard a cry for help while on the road, so he hurried saying, 'Help has come to you,' and the matter became clear: a man had sold a garment to another for nine dirhams, and the seller wanted to return some of the dirhams due to a defect, but the buyer refused and slapped him when he insisted. So Ali asked for evidence of the slap. When it was given, he seated the buyer and said to the seller, 'Here, take retaliation.' The seller said, 'I have forgiven him, O Commander of the Faithful.' Ali said, 'I only wanted to be cautious regarding your right.' Then he struck the man nine lashes and said, 'This is the right of the sultan.' (See: Tarikh al-Umam wa al-Muluk by al-Tabari 6/90). 
6/50 - However, in the case where the harmed person forgives, or the ta'zir is free of a personal right, it is permissible for the ruler to pardon if he is certain that the perpetrator has refrained and he sees that pardon is better for him (see: al-Ahkam al-Sultaniyya also, and Radd al-Muhtar 3/186-187). And it is clear that the Shari'ah granting the ruler the right to pardon in ta'zir without making punishment obligatory does not violate the idea of public right, because this grant is restricted by the requirement of maslahah (public interest) as we have seen. Moreover, modern criminal theories and laws in our country and in the most advanced nations in legal legislation today grant the head of state the right of special pardon even in the greatest crimes, such as murder and below, after judgment, if pardon is deemed beneficial; and this is not considered a violation of the idea of public right. And the method of suspended sentence, known in French legislation as 'sursis' and counted among the most advanced methods of modern criminal legislation...
Source pages p. 656

Commentary
This passage from 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm' by Mustafa al-Zarqa discusses the concept of ta'zir (discretionary punishment) and the role of pardon. The author first defines ta'zir according to its subject matter: when the offense is purely against religious sanctities (e.g., breaking a fast publicly without excuse), the punishment is a pure right of the Shari'ah—i.e., a public right. When the offense involves aggression against a specific person (e.g., assault, defamation), ta'zir comprises both a personal right (the victim's right to retribution or compensation) and a public right (the state's right to discipline and deterrence). The Hanafi position is that if the victim demands punishment, the ruler cannot pardon because he cannot waive the rights of individuals. However, if the victim forgives, the ruler must still punish for the public right. The passage illustrates this with an anecdote from Caliph Ali (RA): a buyer slapped a seller over a defective garment; Ali allowed retaliation but when the seller forgave, Ali still lashed the buyer nine times, declaring it the 'right of the sultan' (the public authority). In section 6/50, the author explains that if the victim forgives or if there is no personal right, the ruler may pardon if he sees that the offender has reformed and pardon is in the public interest. This is a nuanced view: the ruler's discretion is bounded by maslahah. The author then compares this to modern legal systems that grant pardon even for serious crimes. The passage reflects Hanafi fiqh's balance between individual rights and public order, and shows that even a discretionary punishment like ta'zir is not arbitrary but governed by principles.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd punishment; it is a right of the Shari'ah and may also involve a personal right.
عفو (afw): The act of waiving punishment; in ta'zir, the victim can only waive his personal right, not the public right.
حق السلطان (haqq al-sultan): The public right of the state to enforce discipline and deterrence through ta'zir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) He must drop the punishment if the offender repents.

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is not purely discretionary; when a personal right exists, the ruler must punish even after the victim's forgiveness, because the public right to discipline remains independent.

Ta'zir and the Public Right (Haqq al-Sultan)
Ruling on mutual ta'zir and illustration from Ali's judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that ta'zir serves both private and public rights, and construct a fatwa on whether a ruler can impose punishment after victim's pardon.

Core Idea
Ta'zir is not only a private right—the ruler may punish even after pardon.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi concept of ta'zir (discretionary punishment) and its dual nature: it vindicates both the victim's personal right and the public order (haqq al-sultan). The passage presents a ruling and a historical precedent from Imam Ali that illustrates how the ruler retains authority to punish even after the victim pardons. Understanding this distinction is crucial for issuing fatwas on discretionary penalties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal ruling
Read the ruling: if a person strikes another and the victim strikes back, both are subject to ta'zir. This establishes that retaliation beyond proportionality incurs punishment.
2
Analyze the illustration of Imam Ali
Examine the narrative: a buyer slapped a seller; after testimony, Ali offered qisas, but the seller pardoned. Despite the pardon, Ali still struck nine lashes, stating 'This is the right of the Sultan.'
Understand the sequence: pardon was accepted for the private right, but the public right remained.
3
Extract the legal principle
Derive that ta'zir has two components: private right (can be waived by victim) and public right (haqq al-sultan, cannot be waived by individual). The ruler has independent authority to enforce ta'zir for public interest.
This principle is distinctive to the Hanafi school.
4
Apply to fatwa scenario
Consider a modern case: a victim pardons the offender for a ta'zir offense that also disturbs public peace. The mufti must advise the ruler that punishment is still permissible to maintain order.
This shows the practical relevance of the principle.

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on discretionary punishments (ta'zir) in Hanafi fiqh. The author states that if a person strikes another and the victim retaliates by striking back, both are subject to ta'zir. This reflects the principle that retaliation must be proportional and within legal bounds; exceeding it incurs punishment. The historical account of Imam 'Ali illustrates this: a buyer slapped a seller, and when the seller wanted to pardon, 'Ali still imposed lashes, explaining that while the seller's right to retribution could be waived, the public order (right of the Sultan) required punishment. This establishes that ta'zir serves both private and public interests, and the ruler has authority to punish beyond the victim's pardon if necessary to deter and maintain order.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment determined by the ruler for offenses without fixed penalties, aiming at deterrence and reformation.
حق السلطان (haqq al-sultan): The right of the ruler (public authority) to enforce order and punish transgressions that disturb public peace, regardless of private pardon.

Key Takeaway

The Ruler's Pardon in Ta'zir: Conditioned by Public Interest
Explanation of the ruler's conditioned pardon authority in ta'zir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the ruler's pardon in ta'zir is conditioned by public interest and the offender's reform, and identify when the victim's pardon is required.

Core Idea
The ruler may pardon ta'zir only if public interest and the offender's reform are verified.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm addresses a key legal philosophy point: the ruler's authority to pardon ta'zir punishments. While hudud and qisas are fixed, ta'zir is discretionary, and the ruler has leeway. However, this discretion is not absolute; it is bound by the principle of public interest (maslahah). Understanding this limitation is crucial for a mufti when advising rulers or issuing fatwas about punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the passage is ruling on – it is about the permissibility (jawaz) of the ruler's pardon in ta'zir.
2
Extract the conditions
The text gives two conditions: (a) the offender has desisted (i.e., reform is evident) and (b) the ruler sees that pardon is more beneficial (i.e., serves public interest). If both are met, the ruler may pardon.
3
Distinguish personal rights
The passage notes that if the offense involves a personal right (haqq adami), the victim's pardon is also needed. So the ruler's pardon alone suffices only when there is no personal right (or the victim has already pardoned).
4
Trace the evidence
The author cites Al-Ahkam al-Sultaniyya and Radd al-Muhtar. These are authoritative sources in the Hanafi tradition.
5
Apply the maqasid lens
Understand that this rule preserves the concept of public right (haqq 'amm) while giving the ruler flexibility to achieve reform.

٥/٥٠ - على أنه في حال عفو المتضرر أو خلو التعزير عن حق لشخص يجوز للحاكم أن يعفو إذا تحقق انزجار الفاعل ورأى أن العفو أصلح له (ر ) الأحكام السلطانية أيضاً، ورد المحتار ١٨٦/٣ - ١٨٧). ومن الواضح أنه لا يُعتبر منح الشرع لولي الأمر حق العفو في التعزير دون تحتيم العقاب مخلاً بفكرة الحق العام لأنَّ هذا المنح مقيد بمقتضى المصلحة كما رأينا .
5/50 - Moreover, in the case of the victim's pardon or when ta'zir is free from a personal right, it is permissible for the ruler to pardon if he is certain that the offender has desisted and he sees that pardon is more beneficial for him (R: Al-Ahkam al-Sultaniyya also, and Radd al-Muhtar 3/186-187). And it is clear that the Shari'ah granting the ruler the right to pardon in ta'zir without making punishment mandatory does not violate the concept of public right, because this grant is conditioned by what serves the public interest, as we have seen.

Commentary
This section elaborates on the ruler's discretionary power in ta'zir. If the victim has pardoned the offender or if the offense involves no personal right (e.g., purely public order violations), the ruler may choose to pardon if he ascertains that the offender is deterred and that pardon is more beneficial. This is not a violation of public right because the ruler is bound by public interest; he must act in the best interest of society. The authority to pardon is not absolute but is a tool to achieve the maqasid of Shari'ah.

Key Terms
العفو (al-'afw): The ruler's pardon of a ta'zir punishment when it serves the public interest and the offender has shown reform.

Islamic Ta'zir and Modern Sursis: A Parallel in Pardon
Comparison of Islamic ta'zir with modern suspended sentence (sursis)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Islamic ta'zir system's delegated pardon principle is mirrored by the modern suspended sentence (sursis) and apply this understanding to evaluate contemporary penal reforms from a Hanafi perspective.

Core Idea
The modern sursis is merely a formalized application of the ta'zir suspended pardon that Shari'ah always allowed for rulers.

In this lesson, Mustafa al-Zarqa draws a compelling parallel between Islamic ta'zir and a modern French penal innovation called sursis. He argues that the ruler's discretion to defer or pardon ta'zir punishments is not a weakness but a feature, one that anticipated modern rehabilitative justice. By examining this comparison, you will see how classical Hanafi fiqh accommodates evolving legal practices while remaining faithful to its principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal practices
First, note the two things being compared: the Islamic ta'zir pardon (delegated to rulers) and the modern suspended sentence (sursis). Understand each in isolation.
Ta'zir is discretionary punishment for offenses without fixed hadd or qisas.
2
Analyse the Islamic principle of delegated pardon
Recognize that in ta'zir, the ruler (wali al-amr) has the authority to postpone or pardon punishment if it serves public interest (maslahah). This is based on the general principle that the ruler's actions are tied to public welfare.
The delegation is from Shari'ah, not from the people.
3
Examine the modern sursis
The French sursis is a court-ordered suspended sentence: the convict is not imprisoned if they meet conditions. It aims at rehabilitation while preserving the public right to punishment.
It is considered a progressive reform.
4
Contrast and compare the mechanisms
Notice the similarity: both defer punishment conditionally for societal benefit. The difference is that sursis is usually granted by the court, while ta'zir pardon is by the ruler.
Al-Zarqa emphasizes the functional equivalence.
5
Derive the legal and philosophical implication
Conclude that Islamic law is not rigid; its principles can accommodate modern penal theories. This understanding is crucial for a mufti when issuing fatwas on contemporary criminal justice matters.
This supports the maqasid al-shari'ah approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦/٥٠ - على أنَّ النظريات والقوانين الجزائية الحديثة لدينا ولدى أرقى الأمم في التشريع القانوني اليوم تمنح رئيس الدولة حق العفو الخاص حتى في أعظم الجرائم من القتل فما دونه بعد القضاء بها إذا وجد في العفو مصلحة؛ ولم يعتبر هذا مخلاً بفكرة الحق العام.
وإن طريقة الحكم بالعقاب مؤجل التنفيذ وهي المعروفة في التشريع الفرنسي باسم sursis والمعدودة من أرقى طرائق التشريع الجزائي الحديث التي جمعت بين المحافظة على الحق العام واعتبار الحيثيات الشخصية وسياسة المصالح، ليست تلك الطريقة إلا لوناً من العفو المعلق الذي فوضه الشرع الإسلامي لولاة الأمر الحاكمين يستعملونه بحسب المصلحة .
6/50 - Moreover, modern penal theories and laws in our country and among the most advanced nations in legal legislation today grant the head of state the right of special pardon even for the greatest crimes, such as murder and below, after judgment, if pardon is found to be in the public interest; and this is not considered a violation of the concept of public right. And the method of sentencing with deferred execution, known in French legislation as sursis and considered one of the most advanced methods of modern penal legislation, which combines the preservation of public right with consideration of personal circumstances and policy of interests, is nothing but a type of suspended pardon that Islamic Shari'ah delegated to the rulers to use according to public interest.
Source pages p. 657

Commentary
This section draws a parallel between Islamic ta'zir and modern legal concepts. The author argues that the modern practice of granting executive clemency (pardon) and suspended sentences (sursis) is entirely consistent with Islamic principles. In ta'zir, the ruler can defer or pardon punishment if it serves public interest. The French sursis is essentially a suspended ta'zir pardon. This demonstrates the flexibility and foresight of Islamic penal law. The ruler's discretion in ta'zir is not a weakness but a strength, allowing adaptation to changing circumstances while upholding justice.

Key Terms
sursis (sursis): A suspended sentence, deferring punishment to allow for rehabilitation, conditioned on good behavior.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses that do not have fixed hadd or qisas; the ruler may determine the punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author compare the modern suspended sentence (sursis) to Islamic ta'zir?
A) He rejects it as un-Islamic.
B) He considers it a type of conditioned pardon delegated to rulers by Shari'ah.
C) He says it has no equivalent in Islamic law.
D) He claims it is only for minor crimes.

Key Takeaway
The modern suspended sentence is a concrete expression of the ta'zir pardon delegated to rulers by Islamic Shari'ah, demonstrating the adaptability of Hanafi fiqh.
